We need to determine the value of \\( a \\).\n\n1. **Identify the Amplitude:**\n   - The amplitude of a cosine function \\( y = a \\cos bx \\) is given by the absolute value of \\( a \\).\n   - The amplitude is the distance from the midline of the graph to its maximum or minimum value.\n\n2. **Determine the Maximum and Minimum Values:**\n   - From the graph, the maximum value of the function is 3.\n   - The minimum value of the function is -3.\n\n3. **Calculate the Amplitude:**\n   - The amplitude is half the distance between the maximum and minimum values.\n   - The distance between the maximum and minimum values is \\( 3 - (-3) = 6 \\).\n   - Therefore, the amplitude is \\( \\frac{6}{2} = 3 \\).\n\n4. **Determine the Value of \\( a \\):**\n   - Since the amplitude is 3, the value of \\( a \\) is 3.\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( a \\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nFirst, we need to understand the matrices involved:\n\n1. **Dilation Matrix \\(\\mathbf{D}\\):** A dilation matrix with scale factor \\(k\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{D} = \\begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\\\ 0 & k \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Rotation Matrix \\(\\mathbf{R}\\):** A rotation matrix by an angle \\(\\theta\\) counter-clockwise is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{R} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\nThe product \\(\\mathbf{D} \\mathbf{R}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{D} \\mathbf{R} = \\begin{pmatrix} k & 0 \\\\ 0 & k \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} k \\cos \\theta & -k \\sin \\theta \\\\ k \\sin \\theta & k \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{D} \\mathbf{R} = \\begin{pmatrix} -7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nBy comparing the matrices, we have:\n\\[\nk \\cos \\theta = -7, \\quad -k \\sin \\theta = 1, \\quad k \\sin \\theta = -1, \\quad k \\cos \\theta = -7\n\\]\n\nFrom the equations \\(k \\sin \\theta = -1\\) and \\(-k \\sin \\theta = 1\\), we see that they are consistent, confirming that \\(k \\sin \\theta = -1\\).\n\nNow, let's solve for \\(k\\):\n\n1. From \\(k \\cos \\theta = -7\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\theta = -\\frac{7}{k}\n   \\]\n\n2. From \\(k \\sin \\theta = -1\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin \\theta = -\\frac{1}{k}\n   \\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), we substitute:\n\\[\n\\left(-\\frac{7}{k}\\right)^2 + \\left(-\\frac{1}{k}\\right)^2 = 1\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{49}{k^2} + \\frac{1}{k^2} = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{50}{k^2} = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\nk^2 = 50\n\\]\n\n\\[\nk = \\sqrt{50} = 5\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(k\\) is \\(\\boxed{5\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe series given is:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^\\infty (\\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{n} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{n+1})\n\\]\n\nThis is a telescoping series. In a telescoping series, most terms cancel out with subsequent terms. Let's write out the first few terms to see the pattern:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{align*}\na_1 &= \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{1} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{2}, \\\\\na_2 &= \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{2} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{3}, \\\\\na_3 &= \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{3} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{4}, \\\\\n&\\vdots\n\\end{align*}\n\\]\n\nWhen we add these terms together, we see that:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{align*}\nS_N &= (\\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{1} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{2}) + (\\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{2} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{3}) + \\cdots + (\\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{N} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{N+1}) \\\\\n&= \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{1} - \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{N+1}\n\\end{align*}\n\\]\n\nAs \\( N \\to \\infty \\), the term \\( \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{N+1} \\to \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\) because \\(\\tan^{-1}x \\to \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\) as \\(x \\to \\infty\\).\n\nThus, the sum of the series is:\n\n\\[\n\\lim_{N \\to \\infty} S_N = \\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{1} - \\frac{\\pi}{2}\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\tan^{-1}\\sqrt{1} = \\tan^{-1}(1) = \\frac{\\pi}{4}\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of the series is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi}{4} - \\frac{\\pi}{2} = -\\frac{\\pi}{4}\n\\]\n\nSo, the value of the series is \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{4}\\) radians."
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. **Rotation of \\(\\mathbf{v}_0\\) to \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\):**  \n   The vector \\(\\mathbf{v}_0\\) is rotated counter-clockwise by \\(42^\\circ\\) to become \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\). This rotation can be represented by the rotation matrix:\n   \\[\n   R_{42^\\circ} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos 42^\\circ & -\\sin 42^\\circ \\\\ \\sin 42^\\circ & \\cos 42^\\circ \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Reflection of \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\) to \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\):**  \n   The vector \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\) is reflected over a line with direction vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\cos 108^\\circ \\\\ \\sin 108^\\circ \\end{pmatrix}\\). The reflection matrix over a line with direction vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\phi \\\\ \\sin \\phi \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n   \\[\n   M_\\phi = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos 2\\phi & \\sin 2\\phi \\\\ \\sin 2\\phi & -\\cos 2\\phi \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   For \\(\\phi = 108^\\circ\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   M_{108^\\circ} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos 216^\\circ & \\sin 216^\\circ \\\\ \\sin 216^\\circ & -\\cos 216^\\circ \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Combined Transformation:**  \n   The combined transformation from \\(\\mathbf{v}_0\\) to \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v}_2 = M_{108^\\circ} R_{42^\\circ} \\mathbf{v}_0\n   \\]\n\n4. **Direct Reflection of \\(\\mathbf{v}_0\\) to \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\):**  \n   We want to find an angle \\(\\theta\\) such that reflecting \\(\\mathbf{v}_0\\) over a line with direction vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta \\end{pmatrix}\\) results in the same vector \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\). The reflection matrix for this line is:\n   \\[\n   M_\\theta = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos 2\\theta & \\sin 2\\theta \\\\ \\sin 2\\theta & -\\cos 2\\theta \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Equating Transformations:**  \n   We need:\n   \\[\n   M_\\theta = M_{108^\\circ} R_{42^\\circ}\n   \\]\n\n6. **Finding \\(\\theta\\):**  \n   The transformation \\(M_{108^\\circ} R_{42^\\circ}\\) is equivalent to a reflection over a line that is the angle bisector of the angle between the direction of the rotation and the reflection. The angle of rotation is \\(42^\\circ\\) and the reflection line is at \\(108^\\circ\\). The angle bisector of these two angles is:\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\frac{42^\\circ + 108^\\circ}{2} = 75^\\circ\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{75^\\circ}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n(b \\sin C)(b \\cos C + c \\cos B) = 42.\n\\]\n\nWe want to find the area \\(K\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\), which can be expressed as:\n\n\\[\nK = \\frac{1}{2}ab \\sin C.\n\\]\n\nFirst, let's denote:\n\n\\[\nx = b \\sin C.\n\\]\n\nThen the given equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nx(b \\cos C + c \\cos B) = 42.\n\\]\n\nWe need to express \\(b \\cos C + c \\cos B\\) in terms of known quantities. Using the law of cosines, we have:\n\n\\[\nb \\cos C = \\frac{a^2 + b^2 - c^2}{2a},\n\\]\n\\[\nc \\cos B = \\frac{a^2 + c^2 - b^2}{2a}.\n\\]\n\nAdding these two expressions, we get:\n\n\\[\nb \\cos C + c \\cos B = \\frac{a^2 + b^2 - c^2}{2a} + \\frac{a^2 + c^2 - b^2}{2a} = \\frac{2a^2}{2a} = a.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this back into the equation, we have:\n\n\\[\nx \\cdot a = 42.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x = b \\sin C\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nb \\sin C \\cdot a = 42.\n\\]\n\nThus, the area \\(K\\) is:\n\n\\[\nK = \\frac{1}{2}ab \\sin C = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot 42 = 21.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{21}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the radius of the circle where the two spheres intersect, we can use the formula for the radius of the circle of intersection of two spheres. The formula is derived from the geometry of the spheres and the distance between their centers.\n\n1. **Calculate the distance between the centers of the spheres:**\n\n   The centers of the spheres are \\( (3, -5, 7) \\) and \\( (0, 1, 1) \\). The distance \\( d \\) between the centers is given by the distance formula:\n   \\[\n   d = \\sqrt{(3 - 0)^2 + (-5 - 1)^2 + (7 - 1)^2} = \\sqrt{3^2 + (-6)^2 + 6^2} = \\sqrt{9 + 36 + 36} = \\sqrt{81} = 9.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Use the formula for the radius of the circle of intersection:**\n\n   If two spheres with radii \\( R_1 \\) and \\( R_2 \\) intersect, and the distance between their centers is \\( d \\), the radius \\( r \\) of the circle of intersection is given by:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{\\frac{(R_1^2 - R_2^2 + d^2)^2}{4d^2} - \\frac{(R_1^2 - R_2^2 + d^2)^2 - 4R_1^2R_2^2}{4d^2}}.\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying the expression inside the square root, we get:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{R_1^2 - \\left(\\frac{R_1^2 - R_2^2 + d^2}{2d}\\right)^2}.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Substitute the values:**\n\n   - \\( R_1 = 5\\sqrt{5} \\)\n   - \\( R_2 = 2\\sqrt{17} \\)\n   - \\( d = 9 \\)\n\n   First, calculate \\( R_1^2 \\) and \\( R_2^2 \\):\n   \\[\n   R_1^2 = (5\\sqrt{5})^2 = 125, \\quad R_2^2 = (2\\sqrt{17})^2 = 68.\n   \\]\n\n   Now, substitute these into the formula:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{125 - \\left(\\frac{125 - 68 + 81}{18}\\right)^2}.\n   \\]\n\n   Simplify the expression inside the square root:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{125 - \\left(\\frac{138}{18}\\right)^2}.\n   \\]\n\n   Calculate \\( \\frac{138}{18} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\frac{138}{18} = \\frac{23}{3}.\n   \\]\n\n   Now, calculate \\( \\left(\\frac{23}{3}\\right)^2 \\):\n   \\[\n   \\left(\\frac{23}{3}\\right)^2 = \\frac{529}{9}.\n   \\]\n\n   Substitute back into the formula:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{125 - \\frac{529}{9}}.\n   \\]\n\n   Convert 125 to a fraction with denominator 9:\n   \\[\n   125 = \\frac{1125}{9}.\n   \\]\n\n   Now, calculate:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{\\frac{1125}{9} - \\frac{529}{9}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{596}{9}}.\n   \\]\n\n   Simplify:\n   \\[\n   r = \\frac{\\sqrt{596}}{3}.\n   \\]\n\n   Since \\( 596 = 4 \\times 149 \\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{596} = \\sqrt{4 \\times 149} = 2\\sqrt{149}.\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   r = \\frac{2\\sqrt{149}}{3}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the radius of the circle where the two spheres intersect is \\(\\frac{2\\sqrt{149}}{3}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the matrix multiplication:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a & 0 \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & c \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & -6 \\\\ -6 & 34 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nPerforming the matrix multiplication on the left-hand side, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ac \\\\ ca & c^2 + d^2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe need this to equal the matrix on the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & -6 \\\\ -6 & 34 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the following system of equations:\n\n1. \\( a^2 = 4 \\)\n2. \\( ac = -6 \\)\n3. \\( ca = -6 \\) (which is the same as equation 2)\n4. \\( c^2 + d^2 = 34 \\)\n\nLet's solve these equations:\n\n1. From \\( a^2 = 4 \\), we have two possible solutions for \\( a \\): \\( a = 2 \\) or \\( a = -2 \\). Since \\( a > 0 \\), we choose \\( a = 2 \\).\n\n2. Substituting \\( a = 2 \\) into \\( ac = -6 \\), we get:\n   \\[\n   2c = -6 \\implies c = -3.\n   \\]\n\n3. Now, substitute \\( c = -3 \\) into the equation \\( c^2 + d^2 = 34 \\):\n   \\[\n   (-3)^2 + d^2 = 34 \\implies 9 + d^2 = 34 \\implies d^2 = 25.\n   \\]\n\n4. Solving for \\( d \\), we have two possible solutions: \\( d = 5 \\) or \\( d = -5 \\). Since \\( d > 0 \\), we choose \\( d = 5 \\).\n\nThus, the values of \\( a \\), \\( c \\), and \\( d \\) are \\( a = 2 \\), \\( c = -3 \\), and \\( d = 5 \\).\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{L}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{L} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 0 \\\\ -3 & 5 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that \\( |w| = 1 \\) and \\( |z| = 10 \\), we need to find the maximum possible value of \\(\\tan^2 \\theta\\), where \\(\\theta = \\arg \\left(\\frac{w-z}{z}\\right)\\).\n\nFirst, let's express \\(\\frac{w-z}{z}\\) in terms of magnitudes and arguments:\n\\[\n\\frac{w-z}{z} = \\frac{w}{z} - 1.\n\\]\nSince \\(|z| = 10\\), we can write \\(z = 10e^{i\\phi}\\) for some angle \\(\\phi\\). Similarly, since \\(|w| = 1\\), we can write \\(w = e^{i\\psi}\\) for some angle \\(\\psi\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the expression, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{w-z}{z} = \\frac{e^{i\\psi} - 10e^{i\\phi}}{10e^{i\\phi}} = \\frac{e^{i\\psi}}{10e^{i\\phi}} - 1 = \\frac{1}{10}e^{i(\\psi-\\phi)} - 1.\n\\]\n\nThe argument \\(\\theta\\) of this expression is the angle that the vector from \\(z\\) to \\(w\\) makes with the vector from the origin to \\(z\\). Geometrically, this is the angle between the vectors \\(w\\) and \\(z\\).\n\nTo maximize \\(\\tan^2 \\theta\\), we need to maximize \\(\\theta\\). The maximum angle \\(\\theta\\) occurs when \\(w\\) and \\(z\\) are collinear with the origin, with \\(w\\) on the unit circle and \\(z\\) on the circle of radius 10. This happens when \\(w\\) and \\(z\\) are in opposite directions, i.e., when \\(\\psi - \\phi = \\pi\\).\n\nIn this case, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{10}e^{i(\\pi)} - 1 = -\\frac{1}{10} - 1 = -\\frac{11}{10}.\n\\]\n\nThe argument of \\(-\\frac{11}{10}\\) is \\(\\pi\\), and thus \\(\\theta = \\pi\\).\n\nThe tangent of \\(\\theta\\) is undefined at \\(\\theta = \\pi\\), but we are interested in \\(\\tan^2 \\theta\\). As \\(\\theta\\) approaches \\(\\pi\\), \\(\\tan \\theta\\) approaches infinity, and thus \\(\\tan^2 \\theta\\) also approaches infinity.\n\nTherefore, the maximum possible value of \\(\\tan^2 \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\infty}\\)."
'To find the matrix that corresponds to projecting onto the \\( y \\)-axis, we need to consider how this transformation affects any point in the plane.\n\n1. **Projection Definition**: A projection onto the \\( y \\)-axis means that any point \\((x, y)\\) in the plane is mapped to the point \\((0, y)\\). This means that the \\( x \\)-coordinate is eliminated, and only the \\( y \\)-coordinate is retained.\n\n2. **Matrix Representation**: We want to represent this transformation using a matrix \\( P \\). For any vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\), the result of the projection should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n3. **Matrix Formulation**: The matrix \\( P \\) that achieves this transformation is:\n   \\[\n   P = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   This matrix has zeros in the first row, which ensures that the \\( x \\)-coordinate is projected to zero, and a one in the second row, which ensures that the \\( y \\)-coordinate remains unchanged.\n\n4. **Verification**: To verify that this matrix correctly projects onto the \\( y \\)-axis, we apply \\( P \\) to a vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n   \\[\n   P \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\cdot x + 0 \\cdot y \\\\ 0 \\cdot x + 1 \\cdot y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   This confirms that the matrix \\( P \\) correctly projects any vector onto the \\( y \\)-axis by setting the \\( x \\)-component to zero and leaving the \\( y \\)-component unchanged.\n\nTherefore, the matrix representation for the projection onto the \\( y \\)-axis is:\n\\[\nP = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\\[\n\\cos (\\alpha + \\beta) + \\sin (\\alpha - \\beta) = 0\n\\]\nand the condition:\n\\[\n\\tan \\beta = \\frac{1}{2000}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, we use the trigonometric identities for the sum and difference of angles:\n\\[\n\\cos (\\alpha + \\beta) = \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin (\\alpha - \\beta) = \\sin \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\cos \\alpha \\sin \\beta.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the given equation, we have:\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\cos \\alpha \\sin \\beta = 0.\n\\]\n\nWe can group the terms as follows:\n\\[\n(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\cos \\alpha \\sin \\beta) + (\\sin \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha (\\cos \\beta - \\sin \\beta) + \\sin \\alpha (\\cos \\beta - \\sin \\beta) = 0.\n\\]\n\nFactoring out \\((\\cos \\beta - \\sin \\beta)\\), we get:\n\\[\n(\\cos \\alpha + \\sin \\alpha)(\\cos \\beta - \\sin \\beta) = 0.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan \\beta = \\frac{1}{2000}\\), \\(\\beta\\) is not a special angle where \\(\\cos \\beta = \\sin \\beta\\), so \\(\\cos \\beta - \\sin \\beta \\neq 0\\). Therefore, we must have:\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha + \\sin \\alpha = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolving \\(\\cos \\alpha + \\sin \\alpha = 0\\), we can divide both sides by \\(\\cos \\alpha\\) (assuming \\(\\cos \\alpha \\neq 0\\)) to get:\n\\[\n1 + \\tan \\alpha = 0.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan \\alpha = -1\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\tan \\alpha\\) is \\(\\boxed{-1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 53^\\circ \\tan 81^\\circ \\tan x^\\circ = \\tan 53^\\circ + \\tan 81^\\circ + \\tan x^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the value of \\(x\\) such that \\(0 < x < 180\\).\n\nThe identity for the tangent of the sum of three angles states that:\n\n\\[\n\\tan A + \\tan B + \\tan C = \\tan A \\tan B \\tan C\n\\]\n\nif and only if \\(A + B + C = 180^\\circ\\).\n\nIn this problem, we have:\n\n- \\(A = 53^\\circ\\)\n- \\(B = 81^\\circ\\)\n- \\(C = x^\\circ\\)\n\nTo satisfy the identity, the sum of the angles must be:\n\n\\[\n53^\\circ + 81^\\circ + x^\\circ = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, let's solve for \\(x\\):\n\n\\[\nx = 180^\\circ - 53^\\circ - 81^\\circ\n\\]\n\nCalculating the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\nx = 180^\\circ - 134^\\circ = 46^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(x\\) that satisfies the given equation is \\(\\boxed{46^\\circ}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nFirst, we need to express the complex number \\(4 - 4i \\sqrt{3}\\) in polar form. A complex number \\(a + bi\\) can be expressed in polar form as \\(r(\\cos \\phi + i \\sin \\phi)\\), where \\(r = \\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}\\) is the modulus and \\(\\phi = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{b}{a}\\right)\\) is the argument.\n\nFor the complex number \\(4 - 4i \\sqrt{3}\\):\n- The modulus \\(r\\) is given by:\n  \\[\n  r = \\sqrt{4^2 + (-4\\sqrt{3})^2} = \\sqrt{16 + 48} = \\sqrt{64} = 8.\n  \\]\n\n- The argument \\(\\phi\\) is:\n  \\[\n  \\phi = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-4\\sqrt{3}}{4}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(-\\sqrt{3}).\n  \\]\n  The angle whose tangent is \\(-\\sqrt{3}\\) is \\(-60^\\circ\\) (or equivalently \\(300^\\circ\\) in the standard range of \\(0^\\circ\\) to \\(360^\\circ\\)).\n\nThus, the polar form of \\(4 - 4i \\sqrt{3}\\) is:\n\\[\n8 \\left(\\cos 300^\\circ + i \\sin 300^\\circ\\right).\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the fourth roots of this complex number. If \\(z^4 = 8 \\left(\\cos 300^\\circ + i \\sin 300^\\circ\\right)\\), then the solutions \\(z_k\\) are given by:\n\\[\nz_k = \\sqrt[4]{8} \\left(\\cos \\frac{300^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{4} + i \\sin \\frac{300^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{4}\\right),\n\\]\nwhere \\(k = 0, 1, 2, 3\\).\n\nThe modulus of each root is:\n\\[\n\\sqrt[4]{8} = 8^{1/4} = 2^{3/4} = \\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nThe angles \\(\\theta_k\\) are:\n- For \\(k = 0\\): \\(\\theta_1 = \\frac{300^\\circ}{4} = 75^\\circ\\).\n- For \\(k = 1\\): \\(\\theta_2 = \\frac{300^\\circ + 360^\\circ}{4} = \\frac{660^\\circ}{4} = 165^\\circ\\).\n- For \\(k = 2\\): \\(\\theta_3 = \\frac{300^\\circ + 720^\\circ}{4} = \\frac{1020^\\circ}{4} = 255^\\circ\\).\n- For \\(k = 3\\): \\(\\theta_4 = \\frac{300^\\circ + 1080^\\circ}{4} = \\frac{1380^\\circ}{4} = 345^\\circ\\).\n\nFinally, the sum of the angles is:\n\\[\n\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3 + \\theta_4 = 75^\\circ + 165^\\circ + 255^\\circ + 345^\\circ = 840^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of the angles \\(\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3 + \\theta_4\\) is \\(\\boxed{840^\\circ}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the conditions under which the three given planes intersect along a common line. For this to happen, the normal vectors of the planes must be linearly dependent.\n\nThe planes are given by the equations:\n1. \\(-x + cy + bz = 0\\)\n2. \\(cx - y + az = 0\\)\n3. \\(bx + ay - z = 0\\)\n\nThe normal vectors of these planes are:\n- \\((-1, c, b)\\)\n- \\((c, -1, a)\\)\n- \\((b, a, -1)\\)\n\nFor these vectors to be linearly dependent, the determinant of the matrix formed by these vectors must be zero:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix}\n-1 & c & b \\\\\nc & -1 & a \\\\\nb & a & -1\n\\end{vmatrix} = 0\n\\]\n\nCalculating this determinant, we have:\n\n\\[\n-1 \\begin{vmatrix} -1 & a \\\\ a & -1 \\end{vmatrix} - c \\begin{vmatrix} c & a \\\\ b & -1 \\end{vmatrix} + b \\begin{vmatrix} c & -1 \\\\ b & a \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating each of the 2x2 determinants:\n\n1. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} -1 & a \\\\ a & -1 \\end{vmatrix} = (-1)(-1) - a^2 = 1 - a^2\\)\n\n2. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} c & a \\\\ b & -1 \\end{vmatrix} = c(-1) - ab = -c - ab\\)\n\n3. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} c & -1 \\\\ b & a \\end{vmatrix} = ca - (-1)b = ca + b\\)\n\nSubstituting these into the determinant calculation:\n\n\\[\n= -1(1 - a^2) - c(-c - ab) + b(ca + b)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -(1 - a^2) + c^2 + abc + bca + b^2\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n= a^2 - 1 + c^2 + 2abc + b^2\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to zero for linear dependence:\n\n\\[\na^2 + b^2 + c^2 + 2abc - 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThus, the condition becomes:\n\n\\[\na^2 + b^2 + c^2 + 2abc = 1\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the only possible value of \\(a^2 + b^2 + c^2 + 2abc\\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the conditions:\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha + \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\gamma = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha + \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\gamma = 1\n\\]\n\nWe want to find the minimum value of \\(\\cos \\alpha\\).\n\nFirst, we square both equations and add them:\n\\[\n(\\cos \\alpha + \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\gamma)^2 + (\\sin \\alpha + \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\gamma)^2 = 1^2 + 1^2 = 2\n\\]\n\nExpanding both squares, we have:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\alpha + \\cos^2 \\beta + \\cos^2 \\gamma + 2(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\beta \\cos \\gamma + \\cos \\gamma \\cos \\alpha)\n\\]\n\\[\n+ \\sin^2 \\alpha + \\sin^2 \\beta + \\sin^2 \\gamma + 2(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\beta \\sin \\gamma + \\sin \\gamma \\sin \\alpha) = 2\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), we simplify:\n\\[\n3 + 2(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\beta \\cos \\gamma + \\cos \\gamma \\cos \\alpha + \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\beta \\sin \\gamma + \\sin \\gamma \\sin \\alpha) = 2\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n2(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\beta \\cos \\gamma + \\cos \\gamma \\cos \\alpha + \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta + \\sin \\beta \\sin \\gamma + \\sin \\gamma \\sin \\alpha) = -1\n\\]\n\nUsing the angle sum identity for cosine, we have:\n\\[\n\\cos(\\alpha - \\beta) = \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\\[\n\\cos(\\alpha - \\beta) + \\cos(\\beta - \\gamma) + \\cos(\\gamma - \\alpha) = -\\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nTo minimize \\(\\cos \\alpha\\), we assume \\(\\cos \\beta\\) and \\(\\cos \\gamma\\) are as large as possible. Let's assume \\(\\cos \\beta = \\cos \\gamma = x\\). Then:\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha + 2x = 1 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\cos \\alpha = 1 - 2x\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for the sine equation:\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha + 2y = 1 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\sin \\alpha = 1 - 2y\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\alpha + \\sin^2 \\alpha = 1\\), we have:\n\\[\n(1 - 2x)^2 + (1 - 2y)^2 = 1\n\\]\n\nExpanding and simplifying:\n\\[\n1 - 4x + 4x^2 + 1 - 4y + 4y^2 = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n4x^2 + 4y^2 - 4x - 4y + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n4(x^2 + y^2 - x - y) = -1\n\\]\n\nCompleting the square:\n\\[\n4((x - \\frac{1}{2})^2 + (y - \\frac{1}{2})^2) = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\n(x - \\frac{1}{2})^2 + (y - \\frac{1}{2})^2 = \\frac{1}{8}\n\\]\n\nThe minimum value of \\(\\cos \\alpha = 1 - 2x\\) occurs when \\(x\\) is maximized. The maximum \\(x\\) occurs at the center of the circle, \\(x = \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\\).\n\nThus, the minimum value of \\(\\cos \\alpha\\) is:\n\\[\n1 - 2\\left(\\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\\right) = -\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n\\]\n\nExpressing this in the form \\(-\\frac{p + \\sqrt{q}}{r}\\), we have \\(p = 0\\), \\(q = 2\\), and \\(r = 2\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(p + q + r = 0 + 2 + 2 = 4\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{4}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equations:\n\\[ x^4 - 6x^2y^2 + y^4 = 8 \\]\n\\[ x^3y - xy^3 = 2\\sqrt{3} \\]\n\nFirst, notice that the first equation can be rewritten using the identity for the difference of squares:\n\\[ (x^2 - y^2)^2 = 8 \\]\n\nThis implies:\n\\[ x^2 - y^2 = \\pm \\sqrt{8} = \\pm 2\\sqrt{2} \\]\n\nSince we are looking for positive real numbers, let's consider the case:\n\\[ x^2 - y^2 = 2\\sqrt{2} \\]\n\nNow, consider the second equation:\n\\[ x^3y - xy^3 = xy(x^2 - y^2) = 2\\sqrt{3} \\]\n\nSubstitute \\( x^2 - y^2 = 2\\sqrt{2} \\) into the second equation:\n\\[ xy(2\\sqrt{2}) = 2\\sqrt{3} \\]\n\nSolving for \\( xy \\), we get:\n\\[ xy = \\frac{2\\sqrt{3}}{2\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{2}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{3}{2}} \\]\n\nNow we have two equations:\n1. \\( x^2 - y^2 = 2\\sqrt{2} \\)\n2. \\( xy = \\sqrt{\\frac{3}{2}} \\)\n\nTo solve for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\), express \\( x^2 \\) and \\( y^2 \\) in terms of \\( xy \\) and \\( x^2 - y^2 \\):\nLet \\( x^2 = a \\) and \\( y^2 = b \\). Then:\n\\[ a - b = 2\\sqrt{2} \\]\n\\[ ab = \\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{3}{2}}\\right)^2 = \\frac{3}{2} \\]\n\nThese are the equations for a system of quadratic equations. Solving for \\( a \\) and \\( b \\), we have:\n\\[ a + b = \\text{sum of roots of } t^2 - (a+b)t + ab = 0 \\]\n\nThe roots of this quadratic are:\n\\[ t = \\frac{(a+b) \\pm \\sqrt{(a+b)^2 - 4ab}}{2} \\]\n\nSubstitute \\( a - b = 2\\sqrt{2} \\) and \\( ab = \\frac{3}{2} \\) into the quadratic:\n\\[ a + b = \\sqrt{(a-b)^2 + 4ab} = \\sqrt{(2\\sqrt{2})^2 + 4 \\cdot \\frac{3}{2}} = \\sqrt{8 + 6} = \\sqrt{14} \\]\n\nNow solve for \\( a \\) and \\( b \\):\n\\[ a = \\frac{\\sqrt{14} + 2\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\]\n\\[ b = \\frac{\\sqrt{14} - 2\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\]\n\nSince \\( x^2 = a \\), we have:\n\\[ x = \\sqrt{\\frac{\\sqrt{14} + 2\\sqrt{2}}{2}} \\]\n\nExpress \\( x \\) in the form \\( a\\cos\\theta \\):\nNotice that:\n\\[ x^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{14} + 2\\sqrt{2}}{2} = \\frac{1}{2}(\\sqrt{14} + 2\\sqrt{2}) \\]\n\nThis can be expressed as:\n\\[ x = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{2}(\\sqrt{14} + 2\\sqrt{2})} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{2} \\cdot 2\\sqrt{2} \\cdot \\cos(15^\\circ)} = \\sqrt{\\sqrt{2} \\cdot \\cos(15^\\circ)} \\]\n\nThus, \\( x = \\sqrt{2} \\cos(15^\\circ) \\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( x \\) is \\( \\boxed{\\sqrt{2} \\cos(15^\\circ)} \\)."
'To find the equation of the plane containing the line \\( L \\), which is the intersection of the planes \\( x + y + z - 6 = 0 \\) and \\( 2x + 3y + 4z + 5 = 0 \\), and the point \\( (1,1,1) \\), we proceed as follows:\n\n1. **Find the direction vector of line \\( L \\):**\n\n   The direction vector of the line \\( L \\) is perpendicular to the normal vectors of both planes. The normal vector of the first plane is \\( \\langle 1, 1, 1 \\rangle \\) and the normal vector of the second plane is \\( \\langle 2, 3, 4 \\rangle \\).\n\n   The direction vector \\( \\mathbf{d} \\) of the line \\( L \\) is given by the cross product of these two normal vectors:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{d} = \\langle 1, 1, 1 \\rangle \\times \\langle 2, 3, 4 \\rangle = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 1 & 1 & 1 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 4 \\end{vmatrix}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(1 \\cdot 4 - 1 \\cdot 3) - \\mathbf{j}(1 \\cdot 4 - 1 \\cdot 2) + \\mathbf{k}(1 \\cdot 3 - 1 \\cdot 2)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(4 - 3) - \\mathbf{j}(4 - 2) + \\mathbf{k}(3 - 2)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\langle 1, -2, 1 \\rangle\n   \\]\n\n2. **Find a point on line \\( L \\):**\n\n   To find a point on the line, solve the system of equations given by the two planes. Subtract the first equation from the second:\n   \\[\n   (2x + 3y + 4z + 5) - (x + y + z - 6) = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   x + 2y + 3z + 11 = 0\n   \\]\n   Choose \\( z = 0 \\) for simplicity:\n   \\[\n   x + 2y = -11\n   \\]\n   Substitute \\( z = 0 \\) into the first plane equation:\n   \\[\n   x + y = 6\n   \\]\n   Solve the system:\n   \\[\n   x + 2y = -11\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   x + y = 6\n   \\]\n   Subtract the second from the first:\n   \\[\n   y = -17\n   \\]\n   Substitute back to find \\( x \\):\n   \\[\n   x - 17 = 6 \\implies x = 23\n   \\]\n   Thus, a point on the line is \\( (23, -17, 0) \\).\n\n3. **Find the equation of the plane:**\n\n   The plane must contain the point \\( (1, 1, 1) \\) and have a normal vector perpendicular to the direction vector of the line \\( L \\). The normal vector of the plane can be found by taking the cross product of the direction vector \\( \\langle 1, -2, 1 \\rangle \\) and the vector from the point on the line to the given point \\( (1, 1, 1) \\), which is \\( \\langle 1 - 23, 1 + 17, 1 - 0 \\rangle = \\langle -22, 18, 1 \\rangle \\).\n\n   Calculate the cross product:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{n} = \\langle 1, -2, 1 \\rangle \\times \\langle -22, 18, 1 \\rangle = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 1 & -2 & 1 \\\\ -22 & 18 & 1 \\end{vmatrix}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}((-2)(1) - (1)(18)) - \\mathbf{j}((1)(1) - (1)(-22)) + \\mathbf{k}((1)(18) - (-2)(-22))\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(-2 - 18) - \\mathbf{j}(1 + 22) + \\mathbf{k}(18 - 44)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\langle -20, -23, -26 \\rangle\n   \\]\n\n   Simplify the normal vector by dividing by the gcd of its components, which is 1, so the normal vector remains \\( \\langle -20, -23, -26 \\rangle \\).\n\n   The equation of the plane is:\n   \\[\n   -20(x - 1) - 23(y - 1) - 26(z - 1) = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   -20x + 20 - 23y + 23 - 26z + 26 = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   -20x - 23y - 26z + 69 = 0\n   \\]\n\n   Multiply through by -1 to make \\( A > 0 \\):\n   \\[\n   20x + 23y + 26z - 69 = 0\n   \\]\n\nThus, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n20x + 23y + 26z - 69 = 0\n\\]'
"To solve the equation \n\n\\[\n\\arctan \\frac{1}{x} + \\arctan \\frac{1}{x + 2} = \\arctan \\frac{4}{x + 3},\n\\]\n\nwe can use the tangent addition formula for arctan, which states that if \\(\\arctan a + \\arctan b = \\arctan c\\), then\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a + b}{1 - ab} = c.\n\\]\n\nApplying this to our equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{1}{x} + \\frac{1}{x+2}}{1 - \\frac{1}{x} \\cdot \\frac{1}{x+2}} = \\frac{4}{x+3}.\n\\]\n\nLet's simplify the left-hand side:\n\n1. The numerator is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{x} + \\frac{1}{x+2} = \\frac{x+2 + x}{x(x+2)} = \\frac{2x+2}{x(x+2)} = \\frac{2(x+1)}{x(x+2)}.\n\\]\n\n2. The denominator is:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{1}{x} \\cdot \\frac{1}{x+2} = 1 - \\frac{1}{x(x+2)} = \\frac{x(x+2) - 1}{x(x+2)} = \\frac{x^2 + 2x - 1}{x(x+2)}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the left-hand side becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{2(x+1)}{x(x+2)}}{\\frac{x^2 + 2x - 1}{x(x+2)}} = \\frac{2(x+1)}{x^2 + 2x - 1}.\n\\]\n\nEquating this to the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2(x+1)}{x^2 + 2x - 1} = \\frac{4}{x+3}.\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n2(x+1)(x+3) = 4(x^2 + 2x - 1).\n\\]\n\nExpanding both sides:\n\n- Left-hand side: \\(2(x+1)(x+3) = 2(x^2 + 3x + x + 3) = 2(x^2 + 4x + 3) = 2x^2 + 8x + 6\\).\n- Right-hand side: \\(4(x^2 + 2x - 1) = 4x^2 + 8x - 4\\).\n\nSetting them equal:\n\n\\[\n2x^2 + 8x + 6 = 4x^2 + 8x - 4.\n\\]\n\nSubtract \\(2x^2 + 8x + 6\\) from both sides:\n\n\\[\n0 = 2x^2 - 10.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n2x^2 = 10 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x^2 = 5 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x = \\pm \\sqrt{5}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions are:\n\n\\[\nx = \\sqrt{5}, -\\sqrt{5}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the solutions to the equation are:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\sqrt{5}, -\\sqrt{5}}.\n\\]"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\frac{26}{17} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nTo find the value of \\(a\\), we use the formula for the projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto another vector \\(\\mathbf{u}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}}{\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{u}\n\\]\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}, \\quad \\mathbf{u} = \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{u} = a(-1) + 7(4) = -a + 28\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{u}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{u} = (-1)^2 + 4^2 = 1 + 16 = 17\n\\]\n\nThe projection is:\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{u}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{-a + 28}{17} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this projection equals \\(\\frac{26}{17} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Therefore, we equate the two expressions:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{-a + 28}{17} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{26}{17} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\\[\n\\frac{-a + 28}{17} = \\frac{26}{17}\n\\]\n\nSince the denominators are the same, we equate the numerators:\n\\[\n-a + 28 = 26\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(a\\):\n\\[\n-a = 26 - 28\n\\]\n\\[\n-a = -2\n\\]\n\\[\na = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(a\\) is \\(\\boxed{2}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given two matrices \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{B}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & x \\\\ y & -\\frac{9}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{5} & \\frac{1}{10} \\\\ 5 & z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to satisfy the equation \\(\\mathbf{A} + \\mathbf{B} = \\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\).\n\nFirst, let's compute \\(\\mathbf{A} + \\mathbf{B}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} + \\mathbf{B} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & x \\\\ y & -\\frac{9}{5} \\end{pmatrix} + \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{5} & \\frac{1}{10} \\\\ 5 & z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 + \\frac{12}{5} & x + \\frac{1}{10} \\\\ y + 5 & -\\frac{9}{5} + z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} + \\mathbf{B} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{17}{5} & x + \\frac{1}{10} \\\\ y + 5 & z - \\frac{9}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNext, let's compute \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & x \\\\ y & -\\frac{9}{5} \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{5} & \\frac{1}{10} \\\\ 5 & z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe product is calculated as follows:\n\n- The element in the first row, first column:\n  \\[\n  1 \\cdot \\frac{12}{5} + x \\cdot 5 = \\frac{12}{5} + 5x\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the first row, second column:\n  \\[\n  1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{10} + x \\cdot z = \\frac{1}{10} + xz\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, first column:\n  \\[\n  y \\cdot \\frac{12}{5} + \\left(-\\frac{9}{5}\\right) \\cdot 5 = \\frac{12y}{5} - 9\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, second column:\n  \\[\n  y \\cdot \\frac{1}{10} + \\left(-\\frac{9}{5}\\right) \\cdot z = \\frac{y}{10} - \\frac{9z}{5}\n  \\]\n\nThus, the product \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{5} + 5x & \\frac{1}{10} + xz \\\\ \\frac{12y}{5} - 9 & \\frac{y}{10} - \\frac{9z}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nEquating \\(\\mathbf{A} + \\mathbf{B}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\), we get the following system of equations:\n\n1. \\(\\frac{17}{5} = \\frac{12}{5} + 5x\\)\n2. \\(x + \\frac{1}{10} = \\frac{1}{10} + xz\\)\n3. \\(y + 5 = \\frac{12y}{5} - 9\\)\n4. \\(z - \\frac{9}{5} = \\frac{y}{10} - \\frac{9z}{5}\\)\n\nLet's solve these equations one by one:\n\n1. From \\(\\frac{17}{5} = \\frac{12}{5} + 5x\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{17}{5} - \\frac{12}{5} = 5x \\implies \\frac{5}{5} = 5x \\implies x = 1\n   \\]\n\n2. From \\(x + \\frac{1}{10} = \\frac{1}{10} + xz\\), substituting \\(x = 1\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   1 + \\frac{1}{10} = \\frac{1}{10} + z \\implies 1 = z \\implies z = 1\n   \\]\n\n3. From \\(y + 5 = \\frac{12y}{5} - 9\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   y + 5 = \\frac{12y}{5} - 9 \\implies 5y + 25 = 12y - 45 \\implies 70 = 7y \\implies y = 10\n   \\]\n\n4. The fourth equation \\(z - \\frac{9}{5} = \\frac{y}{10} - \\frac{9z}{5}\\) is satisfied with the values \\(y = 10\\) and \\(z = 1\\).\n\nFinally, we find \\(x + y + z\\):\n\n\\[\nx + y + z = 1 + 10 + 1 = 12\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(x + y + z\\) is \\(\\boxed{12}\\)."
'To determine the expression \\(\\frac{1}{1 - a} + \\frac{1}{1 - b} + \\frac{1}{1 - c}\\), we need to use the fact that the points \\((0,0,0)\\), \\((a,1,1)\\), \\((1,b,1)\\), and \\((1,1,c)\\) are coplanar. \n\nFor four points to be coplanar, the volume of the tetrahedron they form must be zero. The volume of a tetrahedron with vertices \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2, z_2)\\), \\((x_3, y_3, z_3)\\), and \\((x_4, y_4, z_4)\\) is given by the determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{6} \\begin{vmatrix}\nx_1 & y_1 & z_1 & 1 \\\\\nx_2 & y_2 & z_2 & 1 \\\\\nx_3 & y_3 & z_3 & 1 \\\\\nx_4 & y_4 & z_4 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSetting this determinant to zero ensures the points are coplanar. For our points, the determinant is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix}\n0 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\\\\na & 1 & 1 & 1 \\\\\n1 & b & 1 & 1 \\\\\n1 & 1 & c & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} = 0\n\\]\n\nExpanding this determinant along the first row, we have:\n\n\\[\n0 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 & 1 \\\\ b & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & c & 1 \\end{vmatrix} - 0 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} a & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & c & 1 \\end{vmatrix} + 0 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} a & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & b & 1 \\\\ 1 & 1 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} - 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} a & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & b & 1 \\\\ 1 & 1 & c \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe determinant simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n- \\begin{vmatrix} a & 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & b & 1 \\\\ 1 & 1 & c \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating this 3x3 determinant, we have:\n\n\\[\n= - \\left( a \\begin{vmatrix} b & 1 \\\\ 1 & c \\end{vmatrix} - 1 \\begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\\\ 1 & c \\end{vmatrix} + 1 \\begin{vmatrix} 1 & b \\\\ 1 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} \\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= - \\left( a(bc - 1) - (c - 1) + (1 - b) \\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= - \\left( abc - a - c + 1 + 1 - b \\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= - (abc - a - b - c + 2)\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to zero for coplanarity:\n\n\\[\nabc - a - b - c + 2 = 0\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\nabc - a - b - c = -2\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to compute \\(\\frac{1}{1-a} + \\frac{1}{1-b} + \\frac{1}{1-c}\\). Using the identity for the sum of fractions:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1-a} + \\frac{1}{1-b} + \\frac{1}{1-c} = \\frac{(1-b)(1-c) + (1-a)(1-c) + (1-a)(1-b)}{(1-a)(1-b)(1-c)}\n\\]\n\nExpanding the numerator:\n\n\\[\n= (1 - b - c + bc) + (1 - a - c + ac) + (1 - a - b + ab)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 3 - (a + b + c) + (ab + ac + bc)\n\\]\n\nUsing the coplanarity condition \\(abc - a - b - c = -2\\), we have:\n\n\\[\na + b + c = abc + 2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the numerator:\n\n\\[\n= 3 - (abc + 2) + (ab + ac + bc)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 1 - abc + ab + ac + bc\n\\]\n\nThe denominator is \\((1-a)(1-b)(1-c)\\), which expands to:\n\n\\[\n= 1 - a - b - c + ab + ac + bc - abc\n\\]\n\nUsing \\(a + b + c = abc + 2\\), the denominator becomes:\n\n\\[\n= 1 - (abc + 2) + ab + ac + bc - abc\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -1 + ab + ac + bc\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 - abc + ab + ac + bc}{-1 + ab + ac + bc} = -1\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\frac{1}{1-a} + \\frac{1}{1-b} + \\frac{1}{1-c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we can use Descartes' Circle Theorem, which relates the curvatures (reciprocals of the radii) of four mutually tangent circles. The theorem states that if four circles are tangent to each other, and their curvatures are \\( k_1, k_2, k_3, \\) and \\( k_4 \\), then:\n\n\\[\n(k_1 + k_2 + k_3 + k_4)^2 = 2(k_1^2 + k_2^2 + k_3^2 + k_4^2).\n\\]\n\nThe curvature \\( k \\) of a circle with radius \\( r \\) is given by \\( k = \\frac{1}{r} \\). For our problem, the curvatures of the circles with radii 1, 2, and 3 are:\n\n- \\( k_1 = \\frac{1}{1} = 1 \\),\n- \\( k_2 = \\frac{1}{2} = 0.5 \\),\n- \\( k_3 = \\frac{1}{3} \\approx 0.3333 \\).\n\nLet \\( k_4 = \\frac{1}{r} \\) be the curvature of the circle with the smallest radius \\( r \\).\n\nSubstituting these into Descartes' Circle Theorem, we have:\n\n\\[\n(1 + 0.5 + 0.3333 + k_4)^2 = 2(1^2 + 0.5^2 + 0.3333^2 + k_4^2).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the left side:\n\n\\[\n(1.8333 + k_4)^2 = 2(1 + 0.25 + 0.1111 + k_4^2).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the right side:\n\n\\[\n(1.8333 + k_4)^2 = 2(1.3611 + k_4^2).\n\\]\n\nExpanding the left side:\n\n\\[\n1.8333^2 + 2 \\times 1.8333 \\times k_4 + k_4^2 = 2.7222 + 3.6666k_4 + k_4^2.\n\\]\n\nEquating both sides:\n\n\\[\n2.7222 + 3.6666k_4 + k_4^2 = 2.7222 + 2k_4^2.\n\\]\n\nSubtracting \\( 2.7222 \\) and \\( k_4^2 \\) from both sides:\n\n\\[\n3.6666k_4 = k_4^2.\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\nk_4^2 - 3.6666k_4 = 0.\n\\]\n\nFactoring out \\( k_4 \\):\n\n\\[\nk_4(k_4 - 3.6666) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis gives two solutions: \\( k_4 = 0 \\) or \\( k_4 = 3.6666 \\). Since \\( k_4 = \\frac{1}{r} \\), \\( k_4 = 0 \\) is not possible because it would imply an infinite radius. Therefore, \\( k_4 = 3.6666 \\).\n\nThus, the smallest radius \\( r \\) is:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{1}{k_4} = \\frac{1}{3.6666} \\approx 0.2727.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the smallest radius \\( r \\) is approximately \\( \\boxed{0.2727} \\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven the equation for the set of vectors \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{v} = \\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the dot products, we have:\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 = 2x\n\\]\n\nRearranging the equation, we get:\n\\[\nx^2 - 2x + y^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nTo complete the square for the \\(x\\) terms, we rewrite:\n\\[\n(x^2 - 2x) + y^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nCompleting the square for \\(x\\):\n\\[\n(x - 1)^2 - 1 + y^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\\[\n(x - 1)^2 + y^2 = 1\n\\]\n\nThis is the equation of a circle centered at \\((1, 0)\\) with radius 1.\n\nThe area of a circle with radius \\(r\\) is given by the formula:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\pi r^2\n\\]\n\nFor our circle, the radius \\(r = 1\\), so the area is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\pi \\times 1^2 = \\pi\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the region contained within the curve is \\(\\boxed{\\pi}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the following conditions in triangle \\(ABC\\):\n\n1. \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{B - C}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{A}{2} = \\frac{1}{29}\\)\n2. \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{C - A}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{B}{2} = \\frac{1}{27}\\)\n\nWe need to find \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2}\\).\n\n### Step 1: Use the tangent half-angle identities\n\nFor any triangle, the tangent of the half-angle differences can be expressed in terms of the semi-perimeter \\(s\\) and the sides \\(a, b, c\\) opposite to angles \\(A, B, C\\) respectively:\n\n- \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{B - C}{2} \\right) = \\frac{s(s-b)}{(s-c)(s-a)}\\)\n- \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{C - A}{2} \\right) = \\frac{s(s-c)}{(s-a)(s-b)}\\)\n- \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) = \\frac{s(s-a)}{(s-b)(s-c)}\\)\n\n### Step 2: Express the given conditions\n\nUsing the given conditions, we can write:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( \\frac{B - C}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{A}{2} = \\frac{1}{29} \\implies \\frac{s(s-b)}{(s-c)(s-a)} \\cdot \\frac{s-a}{s} = \\frac{1}{29}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{s-b}{s-c} = \\frac{1}{29}\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for the second condition:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( \\frac{C - A}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{B}{2} = \\frac{1}{27} \\implies \\frac{s(s-c)}{(s-a)(s-b)} \\cdot \\frac{s-b}{s} = \\frac{1}{27}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{s-c}{s-a} = \\frac{1}{27}\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Find \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2}\\)\n\nWe need to find:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2} = \\frac{s(s-a)}{(s-b)(s-c)}\n\\]\n\nUsing the relations:\n\n- \\(\\frac{s-b}{s-c} = \\frac{1}{29} \\implies s-b = \\frac{s-c}{29}\\)\n- \\(\\frac{s-c}{s-a} = \\frac{1}{27} \\implies s-c = \\frac{s-a}{27}\\)\n\nSubstitute these into the expression for \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2} = \\frac{s(s-a)}{\\left(\\frac{s-c}{29}\\right)\\left(\\frac{s-a}{27}\\right)} = \\frac{29 \\times 27 \\times s(s-a)}{(s-c)(s-a)}\n\\]\n\nCanceling \\(s-a\\) from numerator and denominator, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2} = 29 \\times 27 = 783\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\tan \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right) \\tan \\frac{C}{2}\\) is \\(\\boxed{783}\\)."
"Let's think step by step:\n\n1. We start with the rectangular coordinates \\((-4, 0)\\), where \\(x = -4\\) and \\(y = 0\\).\n\n2. To convert these coordinates to polar coordinates, we need to find \\(r\\) and \\(\\theta\\).\n\n3. The formula for \\(r\\) is:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}\n   \\]\n   Substituting the values of \\(x\\) and \\(y\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{(-4)^2 + 0^2} = \\sqrt{16} = 4\n   \\]\n\n4. Next, we find \\(\\theta\\). The formula for \\(\\theta\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{y}{x}\\right)\n   \\]\n   However, since \\(y = 0\\) and \\(x = -4\\), the point lies on the negative x-axis. The angle \\(\\theta\\) for a point on the negative x-axis is \\(\\pi\\).\n\n5. Therefore, the polar coordinates are \\((r, \\theta) = (4, \\pi)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((4, \\pi)\\)."
"Let's solve the equation step by step.\n\nWe start with the equation:\n\\[ 1 - \\sin^4 x - \\cos^2 x = \\frac{1}{16}. \\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x = 1\\), we can express \\(\\cos^2 x\\) as:\n\\[ \\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x. \\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\) into the original equation:\n\\[ 1 - \\sin^4 x - (1 - \\sin^2 x) = \\frac{1}{16}. \\]\n\nSimplify the equation:\n\\[ 1 - \\sin^4 x - 1 + \\sin^2 x = \\frac{1}{16}. \\]\n\\[ -\\sin^4 x + \\sin^2 x = \\frac{1}{16}. \\]\n\nLet \\( y = \\sin^2 x \\). Then the equation becomes:\n\\[ -y^2 + y = \\frac{1}{16}. \\]\n\nRearrange the equation:\n\\[ y^2 - y + \\frac{1}{16} = 0. \\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\( y \\). Use the quadratic formula to solve for \\( y \\):\n\\[ y = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}, \\]\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = -1 \\), and \\( c = \\frac{1}{16} \\).\n\nCalculate the discriminant:\n\\[ b^2 - 4ac = (-1)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{16} = 1 - \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{3}{4}. \\]\n\nSubstitute into the quadratic formula:\n\\[ y = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{3}{4}}}{2}. \\]\n\\[ y = \\frac{1 \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}{2}. \\]\n\nThis gives two solutions for \\( y \\):\n\\[ y = \\frac{1 + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}{2} = \\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}, \\]\n\\[ y = \\frac{1 - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}{2} = \\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}. \\]\n\nSince \\( y = \\sin^2 x \\), we have:\n\\[ \\sin^2 x = \\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4} \\quad \\text{or} \\quad \\sin^2 x = \\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}. \\]\n\nFor \\(\\sin^2 x = \\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}\\), solve for \\(\\sin x\\):\n\\[ \\sin x = \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}. \\]\n\nFor \\(\\sin^2 x = \\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}\\), solve for \\(\\sin x\\):\n\\[ \\sin x = \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}. \\]\n\nNow, find the values of \\( x \\) in the interval \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{2} \\leq x \\leq \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\).\n\n1. \\(\\sin x = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\) gives \\( x = \\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right) \\).\n2. \\(\\sin x = -\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\) gives \\( x = -\\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right) \\).\n3. \\(\\sin x = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\) gives \\( x = \\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right) \\).\n4. \\(\\sin x = -\\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\) gives \\( x = -\\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right) \\).\n\nThus, the solutions are:\n\\[ x = \\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right), -\\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right), \\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right), -\\arcsin\\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\right). \\]"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the maximum angle between the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} b \\\\ c \\\\ a \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors \\(\\mathbf{u}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}}{\\|\\mathbf{u}\\| \\|\\mathbf{v}\\|}\n\\]\n\nFor the vectors \\(\\mathbf{u} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} b \\\\ c \\\\ a \\end{pmatrix}\\), the dot product is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v} = ab + bc + ca\n\\]\n\nThe magnitudes of the vectors are:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{u}\\| = \\sqrt{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{v}\\| = \\sqrt{b^2 + c^2 + a^2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the cosine of the angle is:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{ab + bc + ca}{\\sqrt{a^2 + b^2 + c^2} \\cdot \\sqrt{b^2 + c^2 + a^2}}\n\\]\n\nTo find the maximum angle, we need to minimize \\(\\cos \\theta\\). The minimum value of \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is \\(-1\\), which corresponds to an angle of \\(180^\\circ\\).\n\nThe condition for \\(\\cos \\theta = -1\\) is that the vectors are opposite, i.e.,\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = -k \\begin{pmatrix} b \\\\ c \\\\ a \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nfor some positive constant \\(k\\). This implies:\n\n1. \\(a = -kb\\)\n2. \\(b = -kc\\)\n3. \\(c = -ka\\)\n\nThese conditions can be satisfied for nonzero \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) by choosing appropriate values of \\(k\\). For example, if \\(k = -1\\), then \\(a = b = c\\) (up to a sign), which satisfies the conditions.\n\nTherefore, the maximum angle between the vectors is \\(180^\\circ\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{180^\\circ}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo perform a \\(60^\\circ\\) counter-clockwise rotation around the origin on the complex number \\(3 \\sqrt{3} - 5i\\), we multiply it by the complex number representing the rotation, which is \\(e^{i \\frac{\\pi}{3}}\\). This can be expressed as:\n\n\\[\ne^{i \\frac{\\pi}{3}} = \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{3} + i \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we multiply the given complex number \\(3 \\sqrt{3} - 5i\\) by \\(\\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\):\n\n\\[\n(3 \\sqrt{3} - 5i) \\left(\\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nLet's perform the multiplication:\n\n1. Multiply the imaginary parts:\n   \\[\n   -5i \\cdot i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = 5 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{5\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n   \\]\n\n3. Multiply the real part by the imaginary part:\n   \\[\n   3 \\sqrt{3} \\cdot i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{3 \\cdot 3}{2} i = \\frac{9}{2} i\n   \\]\n\n4. Multiply the imaginary part by the real part:\n   \\[\n   -5i \\cdot \\frac{1}{2} = -\\frac{5}{2} i\n   \\]\n\nNow, combine the results:\n\n- Real part: \\(\\frac{3 \\sqrt{3}}{2} - \\frac{5\\sqrt{3}}{2} = -\\frac{2\\sqrt{3}}{2} = -\\sqrt{3}\\)\n- Imaginary part: \\(\\frac{9}{2} i - \\frac{5}{2} i = \\frac{4}{2} i = 2i\\)\n\nThus, the resulting complex number after the rotation is:\n\n\\[\n-\\sqrt{3} + 2i\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the resulting complex number is \\(\\boxed{-\\sqrt{3} + 2i}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the smallest positive value of \\( x \\) such that the five numbers \\( \\cos(2 \\pi x) \\), \\( \\cos(4 \\pi x) \\), \\( \\cos(8 \\pi x) \\), \\( \\cos(16 \\pi x) \\), and \\( \\cos(32 \\pi x) \\) are all nonpositive, we need to ensure that each of these cosine values is nonpositive simultaneously.\n\nA cosine function is nonpositive when its argument is in the interval \\([\\pi/2 + k\\pi, 3\\pi/2 + k\\pi]\\) for integer \\( k \\). This means that for each \\( n \\), the expression \\( \\cos(2^n \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n\\[\n2^n \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k\\pi\\right]\n\\]\nfor some integer \\( k \\).\n\nLet's analyze each cosine term:\n\n1. \\( \\cos(2 \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n   \\[\n   2 \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k_1\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k_1\\pi\\right]\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   x \\in \\left[\\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{k_1}{2}, \\frac{3}{4} + \\frac{k_1}{2}\\right]\n   \\]\n\n2. \\( \\cos(4 \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n   \\[\n   4 \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k_2\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k_2\\pi\\right]\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   x \\in \\left[\\frac{1}{8} + \\frac{k_2}{4}, \\frac{3}{8} + \\frac{k_2}{4}\\right]\n   \\]\n\n3. \\( \\cos(8 \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n   \\[\n   8 \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k_3\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k_3\\pi\\right]\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   x \\in \\left[\\frac{1}{16} + \\frac{k_3}{8}, \\frac{3}{16} + \\frac{k_3}{8}\\right]\n   \\]\n\n4. \\( \\cos(16 \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n   \\[\n   16 \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k_4\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k_4\\pi\\right]\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   x \\in \\left[\\frac{1}{32} + \\frac{k_4}{16}, \\frac{3}{32} + \\frac{k_4}{16}\\right]\n   \\]\n\n5. \\( \\cos(32 \\pi x) \\) is nonpositive if:\n   \\[\n   32 \\pi x \\in \\left[\\frac{\\pi}{2} + k_5\\pi, \\frac{3\\pi}{2} + k_5\\pi\\right]\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   x \\in \\left[\\frac{1}{64} + \\frac{k_5}{32}, \\frac{3}{64} + \\frac{k_5}{32}\\right]\n   \\]\n\nTo find the smallest positive \\( x \\), we need to find a common \\( x \\) that satisfies all these conditions simultaneously. The smallest \\( x \\) will be the least common multiple of the smallest values in each interval.\n\nBy choosing \\( k_1 = 0, k_2 = 0, k_3 = 0, k_4 = 0, k_5 = 0 \\), we get:\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1}{4}, \\frac{1}{8}, \\frac{1}{16}, \\frac{1}{32}, \\frac{1}{64}\n\\]\n\nThe smallest positive \\( x \\) that satisfies all these conditions is the least common multiple of these fractions, which is:\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1}{2^6} = \\frac{1}{64}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the smallest possible positive value of \\( x \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{64}}\\)."
'To find the values of \\( x \\) such that the determinant of the matrix is zero, we need to evaluate the determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} \n1 & 1 & 1 \\\\ \nx & 7 & -2 \\\\ \nx^3 & 343 & -8 \n\\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe determinant of a \\(3 \\times 3\\) matrix \\(\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ g & h & i \\end{vmatrix}\\) is calculated as:\n\n\\[\na(ei - fh) - b(di - fg) + c(dh - eg)\n\\]\n\nApplying this formula to our matrix, we have:\n\n\\[\n1 \\cdot (7 \\cdot (-8) - (-2) \\cdot 343) - 1 \\cdot (x \\cdot (-8) - (-2) \\cdot x^3) + 1 \\cdot (x \\cdot 343 - 7 \\cdot x^3)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\n1. \\(1 \\cdot (7 \\cdot (-8) - (-2) \\cdot 343) = 1 \\cdot (-56 + 686) = 1 \\cdot 630 = 630\\)\n\n2. \\(-1 \\cdot (x \\cdot (-8) - (-2) \\cdot x^3) = -1 \\cdot (-8x + 2x^3) = 8x - 2x^3\\)\n\n3. \\(1 \\cdot (x \\cdot 343 - 7 \\cdot x^3) = 343x - 7x^3\\)\n\nCombining these, the determinant is:\n\n\\[\n630 + 8x - 2x^3 + 343x - 7x^3\n\\]\n\nSimplifying further:\n\n\\[\n630 + 351x - 9x^3\n\\]\n\nSetting the determinant equal to zero:\n\n\\[\n-9x^3 + 351x + 630 = 0\n\\]\n\nDividing the entire equation by 3 to simplify:\n\n\\[\n-3x^3 + 117x + 210 = 0\n\\]\n\nTo solve this cubic equation, we can use the Rational Root Theorem to test possible rational roots, which are factors of the constant term 210. Testing \\(x = 5\\):\n\n\\[\n-3(5)^3 + 117(5) + 210 = -375 + 585 + 210 = 0\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(x = 5\\) is a root. We can factor the cubic polynomial as \\((x - 5)(-3x^2 - 15x - 42)\\).\n\nNow, solve the quadratic \\(-3x^2 - 15x - 42 = 0\\) using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = -3\\), \\(b = -15\\), and \\(c = -42\\). Calculating the discriminant:\n\n\\[\nb^2 - 4ac = (-15)^2 - 4(-3)(-42) = 225 - 504 = -279\n\\]\n\nSince the discriminant is negative, the quadratic has no real roots. Therefore, the only real solution is \\(x = 5\\).\n\nThus, the value of \\(x\\) that makes the determinant zero is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{5}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nFirst, we express the given trigonometric expressions in terms of complex exponentials using Euler's formula:\n\\[\n\\cos 12^\\circ + i \\sin 12^\\circ = e^{i12^\\circ}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 48^\\circ + i \\sin 48^\\circ = e^{i48^\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression \\((\\cos 12^\\circ + i \\sin 12^\\circ + \\cos 48^\\circ + i \\sin 48^\\circ)^6\\) can be rewritten as:\n\\[\n(e^{i12^\\circ} + e^{i48^\\circ})^6\n\\]\n\nNext, we simplify the sum inside the parentheses:\n\\[\ne^{i12^\\circ} + e^{i48^\\circ} = e^{i12^\\circ}(1 + e^{i(48^\\circ - 12^\\circ)}) = e^{i12^\\circ}(1 + e^{i36^\\circ})\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the imaginary part of \\((e^{i12^\\circ}(1 + e^{i36^\\circ}))^6\\).\n\nFirst, calculate \\(1 + e^{i36^\\circ}\\):\n\\[\n1 + e^{i36^\\circ} = 1 + (\\cos 36^\\circ + i \\sin 36^\\circ)\n\\]\n\\[\n= (1 + \\cos 36^\\circ) + i \\sin 36^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this complex number is:\n\\[\n|1 + e^{i36^\\circ}| = \\sqrt{(1 + \\cos 36^\\circ)^2 + (\\sin 36^\\circ)^2}\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{1 + 2\\cos 36^\\circ + \\cos^2 36^\\circ + \\sin^2 36^\\circ}\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{2 + 2\\cos 36^\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThe angle of this complex number is:\n\\[\n\\theta = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{\\sin 36^\\circ}{1 + \\cos 36^\\circ}\\right)\n\\]\n\nNow, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n(e^{i12^\\circ} \\cdot |1 + e^{i36^\\circ}| e^{i\\theta})^6 = |1 + e^{i36^\\circ}|^6 e^{i(6 \\cdot 12^\\circ + 6\\theta)}\n\\]\n\nThe imaginary part of this expression is:\n\\[\n|1 + e^{i36^\\circ}|^6 \\cdot \\sin(72^\\circ + 6\\theta)\n\\]\n\nTo find the exact value, we need to evaluate \\(|1 + e^{i36^\\circ}|^6\\) and \\(\\sin(72^\\circ + 6\\theta)\\).\n\nHowever, without further simplification or numerical calculation, we can conclude that the imaginary part is determined by the sine component of the angle \\(72^\\circ + 6\\theta\\).\n\nThus, the imaginary part of the expression is \\(\\boxed{0}\\) because the symmetry and periodicity of the trigonometric functions involved lead to a cancellation of the imaginary components when raised to the sixth power."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that point \\( D \\) is on side \\( \\overline{BC} \\) of triangle \\( ABC \\), and that \\(\\angle CAD = \\angle BAD = 60^\\circ\\). This implies that \\( \\angle BAC = 120^\\circ \\) because the angles around point \\( A \\) must sum to \\( 180^\\circ \\).\n\nWe are also given that \\( AC = 3 \\) and \\( AB = 6 \\). We need to find the length of \\( AD \\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle CAD = \\angle BAD = 60^\\circ\\), triangle \\( ABD \\) and triangle \\( ACD \\) are isosceles triangles with \\( AD \\) as the common side. \n\nLet's use the Law of Cosines in triangle \\( ABC \\) to find \\( BC \\):\n\n\\[\nBC^2 = AB^2 + AC^2 - 2 \\cdot AB \\cdot AC \\cdot \\cos(120^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given values:\n\n\\[\nBC^2 = 6^2 + 3^2 - 2 \\cdot 6 \\cdot 3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\nBC^2 = 36 + 9 + 18 = 63\n\\]\n\n\\[\nBC = \\sqrt{63} = 3\\sqrt{7}\n\\]\n\nNow, since \\( \\angle CAD = \\angle BAD = 60^\\circ \\), triangles \\( ABD \\) and \\( ACD \\) are congruent by the Angle-Side-Angle (ASA) criterion. Therefore, \\( AD \\) is the same in both triangles.\n\nTo find \\( AD \\), we can use the fact that the triangles are congruent and the Law of Cosines in either triangle \\( ABD \\) or \\( ACD \\). Let's use triangle \\( ABD \\):\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = AB^2 + BD^2 - 2 \\cdot AB \\cdot BD \\cdot \\cos(60^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nSince \\( \\angle BAD = 60^\\circ \\), \\( \\cos(60^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2} \\).\n\nWe know \\( BD = \\frac{BC}{2} = \\frac{3\\sqrt{7}}{2} \\) because \\( D \\) is the midpoint of \\( BC \\) due to the symmetry of the problem.\n\nSubstituting the values:\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 6^2 + \\left(\\frac{3\\sqrt{7}}{2}\\right)^2 - 2 \\cdot 6 \\cdot \\frac{3\\sqrt{7}}{2} \\cdot \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 36 + \\frac{63}{4} - 18\\sqrt{7} \\cdot \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 36 + \\frac{63}{4} - 9\\sqrt{7}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying further:\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = \\frac{144}{4} + \\frac{63}{4} - 9\\sqrt{7}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = \\frac{207}{4} - 9\\sqrt{7}\n\\]\n\nThis expression is quite complex, so let's simplify it by considering the symmetry and congruence of the triangles. Since \\( \\angle CAD = \\angle BAD = 60^\\circ \\), and the triangles are congruent, \\( AD \\) is the same in both triangles, and the symmetry implies that \\( AD \\) is the altitude from \\( A \\) to \\( BC \\).\n\nThus, using the properties of the 30-60-90 triangle, we find that:\n\n\\[\nAD = \\frac{AB \\cdot \\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\cdot \\sqrt{3}}{2} = 3\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the length of \\( AD \\) is \\( \\boxed{3\\sqrt{3}} \\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the point where the line intersects the \\(xy\\)-plane, we need to determine the equation of the line using the given intersection points with the \\(yz\\)-plane and the \\(xz\\)-plane.\n\n1. **Identify the direction vector of the line:**\n\n   The line passes through the points \\((0, -2, -5)\\) and \\((3, 0, -1)\\). The direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) of the line can be found by subtracting the coordinates of these two points:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{d} = (3 - 0, 0 - (-2), -1 - (-5)) = (3, 2, 4).\n   \\]\n\n2. **Parameterize the line:**\n\n   We can write the parametric equations of the line using one of the points, say \\((0, -2, -5)\\), and the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d} = (3, 2, 4)\\):\n   \\[\n   x = 0 + 3t = 3t,\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   y = -2 + 2t,\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   z = -5 + 4t.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Find the intersection with the \\(xy\\)-plane:**\n\n   The \\(xy\\)-plane is defined by \\(z = 0\\). To find the intersection, set the \\(z\\)-coordinate of the line to zero and solve for \\(t\\):\n   \\[\n   -5 + 4t = 0.\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\(t\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   4t = 5 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad t = \\frac{5}{4}.\n   \\]\n\n4. **Substitute \\(t = \\frac{5}{4}\\) into the parametric equations:**\n\n   Substitute \\(t = \\frac{5}{4}\\) into the equations for \\(x\\) and \\(y\\):\n   \\[\n   x = 3 \\left(\\frac{5}{4}\\right) = \\frac{15}{4},\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   y = -2 + 2 \\left(\\frac{5}{4}\\right) = -2 + \\frac{10}{4} = -2 + \\frac{5}{2} = -\\frac{4}{2} + \\frac{5}{2} = \\frac{1}{2}.\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the point where the line intersects the \\(xy\\)-plane is \\(\\left(\\frac{15}{4}, \\frac{1}{2}, 0\\right)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\left(\\frac{15}{4}, \\frac{1}{2}, 0\\right)\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given a right triangle \\( \\triangle ADB \\) with \\( \\angle ADB = 90^\\circ \\), \\( AB = 24 \\), \\( \\sin A = \\frac{2}{3} \\), and \\( \\sin C = \\frac{3}{4} \\). We need to find the length of \\( BC \\).\n\n1. **Find \\( AD \\) and \\( BD \\) using \\( \\sin A \\):**\n\n   Since \\( \\sin A = \\frac{2}{3} \\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin A = \\frac{\\text{opposite side to } A}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{AD}{AB} = \\frac{AD}{24}\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\( AD \\), we get:\n   \\[\n   AD = 24 \\times \\frac{2}{3} = 16\n   \\]\n\n   Now, using the Pythagorean theorem in \\( \\triangle ADB \\):\n   \\[\n   AB^2 = AD^2 + BD^2\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   24^2 = 16^2 + BD^2\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   576 = 256 + BD^2\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   BD^2 = 320\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   BD = \\sqrt{320} = 8\\sqrt{5}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Find \\( DC \\) using \\( \\sin C \\):**\n\n   Since \\( \\sin C = \\frac{3}{4} \\), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin C = \\frac{\\text{opposite side to } C}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{BD}{BC}\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\( BC \\), we get:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{8\\sqrt{5}}{BC} = \\frac{3}{4}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   BC = \\frac{8\\sqrt{5} \\times 4}{3} = \\frac{32\\sqrt{5}}{3}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the length of \\( BC \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{32\\sqrt{5}}{3}}\\)."
'To solve the problem, we need to compute the expression \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot (\\mathbf{b} \\times (\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}))\\) given that \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\| = 2\\).\n\nWe can use the vector triple product identity, which states:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{u} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{w}) = (\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{w})\\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v})\\mathbf{w}.\n\\]\nIn our case, let \\(\\mathbf{u} = \\mathbf{b}\\), \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\mathbf{a}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{w} = \\mathbf{b}\\). Applying the identity, we have:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\times (\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}) = (\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})\\mathbf{a} - (\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to compute \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot (\\mathbf{b} \\times (\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}))\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\left((\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})\\mathbf{a} - (\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\\mathbf{b}\\right).\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}) - (\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}).\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\((\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) = (\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})^2\\), so the expression becomes:\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - (\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b})^2.\n\\]\n\nHowever, we need to find a specific value. Given that \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\| = 2\\), we know:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\\sin\\theta = 2,\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nThe expression \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot (\\mathbf{b} \\times (\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}))\\) is actually a known vector identity result, which simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\|^2.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot (\\mathbf{b} \\times (\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}))\\) is:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 2^2 = 4.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{4}\\).'
'To find \\(\\csc(-120^\\circ)\\), we need to follow these steps:\n\n1. **Understand the Cosecant Function**: \n   The cosecant function, \\(\\csc(\\theta)\\), is the reciprocal of the sine function. Therefore, \\(\\csc(\\theta) = \\frac{1}{\\sin(\\theta)}\\).\n\n2. **Determine the Equivalent Positive Angle**:\n   The angle \\(-120^\\circ\\) can be converted to a positive angle by adding \\(360^\\circ\\), resulting in \\(240^\\circ\\). This means \\(-120^\\circ\\) is equivalent to \\(240^\\circ\\).\n\n3. **Identify the Reference Angle**:\n   The reference angle for \\(240^\\circ\\) is \\(240^\\circ - 180^\\circ = 60^\\circ\\).\n\n4. **Determine the Sign of the Sine Function**:\n   The angle \\(-120^\\circ\\) (or equivalently \\(240^\\circ\\)) is in the third quadrant, where the sine function is negative.\n\n5. **Calculate \\(\\sin(-120^\\circ)\\)**:\n   Since the reference angle is \\(60^\\circ\\) and the sine is negative in the third quadrant, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin(-120^\\circ) = -\\sin(60^\\circ)\n   \\]\n   The sine of \\(60^\\circ\\) is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\). Therefore:\n   \\[\n   \\sin(-120^\\circ) = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n   \\]\n\n6. **Calculate \\(\\csc(-120^\\circ)\\)**:\n   \\[\n   \\csc(-120^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{\\sin(-120^\\circ)} = \\frac{1}{-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}} = -\\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\n   \\]\n\n7. **Rationalize the Denominator**:\n   To rationalize the denominator, multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(\\sqrt{3}\\):\n   \\[\n   -\\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{3}} = -\\frac{2\\sqrt{3}}{3}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\csc(-120^\\circ)\\) is \\(-\\frac{2\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) satisfies the following equations:\n\n1. \\(\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n2. \\(\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 15 \\\\ 18 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\nLet's denote the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) as:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nUsing the first equation:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the system of equations:\n\n\\[\n2a + 7b = -15\n\\]\n\\[\n2c + 7d = -6\n\\]\n\nUsing the second equation:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 15 \\\\ 18 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us another system of equations:\n\n\\[\n4a - b = 15\n\\]\n\\[\n4c - d = 18\n\\]\n\nNow, we solve these systems of equations.\n\nFrom the first system:\n\n1. \\(2a + 7b = -15\\)\n2. \\(4a - b = 15\\)\n\nMultiply the second equation by 7 to eliminate \\(b\\):\n\n\\[\n28a - 7b = 105\n\\]\n\nAdd this to the first equation:\n\n\\[\n2a + 7b + 28a - 7b = -15 + 105\n\\]\n\n\\[\n30a = 90\n\\]\n\n\\[\na = 3\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(a = 3\\) back into the second equation:\n\n\\[\n4(3) - b = 15\n\\]\n\n\\[\n12 - b = 15\n\\]\n\n\\[\nb = -3\n\\]\n\nNow, solve the second system:\n\n1. \\(2c + 7d = -6\\)\n2. \\(4c - d = 18\\)\n\nMultiply the second equation by 7 to eliminate \\(d\\):\n\n\\[\n28c - 7d = 126\n\\]\n\nAdd this to the first equation:\n\n\\[\n2c + 7d + 28c - 7d = -6 + 126\n\\]\n\n\\[\n30c = 120\n\\]\n\n\\[\nc = 4\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(c = 4\\) back into the second equation:\n\n\\[\n4(4) - d = 18\n\\]\n\n\\[\n16 - d = 18\n\\]\n\n\\[\nd = -2\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & -3 \\\\ 4 & -2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 3 & -3 \\\\ 4 & -2 \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"Let's analyze the given series for \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\) and the condition \\(\\frac{P}{Q} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{7}\\).\n\nThe series for \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\) are infinite trigonometric series. Notice that the coefficients of the trigonometric functions in both series are powers of \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\). This suggests that the series might be related to the real and imaginary parts of a complex exponential series.\n\nConsider the complex exponential series:\n\\[\nS = \\sum_{n=0}^{\\infty} \\left(\\frac{i}{2}\\right)^n e^{in\\theta}\n\\]\nThis series can be split into real and imaginary parts, which correspond to cosine and sine terms, respectively.\n\nThe real part of \\( S \\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Re}(S) = \\sum_{n=0}^{\\infty} \\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^n \\cos(n\\theta)\n\\]\nThe imaginary part of \\( S \\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Im}(S) = \\sum_{n=0}^{\\infty} \\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^n \\sin(n\\theta)\n\\]\n\nNow, let's match the given series for \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\) with these expressions. Notice that:\n- The series for \\( P \\) involves alternating cosine and sine terms with coefficients \\(\\frac{1}{2^n}\\).\n- The series for \\( Q \\) also involves alternating sine and cosine terms with coefficients \\(\\frac{1}{2^n}\\).\n\nThis suggests that \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\) can be expressed as linear combinations of the real and imaginary parts of \\( S \\).\n\nLet's express \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\) in terms of \\( S \\):\n\\[\nP = \\text{Re}(S) - \\text{Im}(S)\n\\]\n\\[\nQ = 1 - \\text{Im}(S) - \\text{Re}(S)\n\\]\n\nGiven the condition \\(\\frac{P}{Q} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{7}\\), we can substitute the expressions for \\( P \\) and \\( Q \\):\n\\[\n\\frac{\\text{Re}(S) - \\text{Im}(S)}{1 - \\text{Im}(S) - \\text{Re}(S)} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{7}\n\\]\n\nTo solve for \\(\\sin\\theta\\), we need to find the values of \\(\\text{Re}(S)\\) and \\(\\text{Im}(S)\\) that satisfy this equation. Since \\(\\theta\\) is in the range \\(\\pi \\leq \\theta < 2\\pi\\), we know that \\(\\sin\\theta\\) is non-positive.\n\nBy analyzing the symmetry and periodicity of the trigonometric functions, and considering the given ratio, we find that the solution corresponds to \\(\\theta = \\frac{5\\pi}{4}\\), where \\(\\sin\\theta = -\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sin\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step:\n\n1. The angle \\(135^\\circ\\) is located in the second quadrant of the unit circle. In the second quadrant, the tangent function is negative.\n\n2. To find the tangent of \\(135^\\circ\\), we first determine its reference angle. The reference angle for an angle in the second quadrant is calculated as \\(180^\\circ - \\text{angle}\\). Therefore, the reference angle for \\(135^\\circ\\) is:\n   \\[\n   180^\\circ - 135^\\circ = 45^\\circ\n   \\]\n\n3. We know that \\(\\tan 45^\\circ = 1\\).\n\n4. Since \\(135^\\circ\\) is in the second quadrant where the tangent is negative, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 135^\\circ = -\\tan 45^\\circ = -1\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\tan 135^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{-1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\mathbf{P}^{-1} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\mathbf{P}\n\\]\n\nThis implies that the matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is similar to the diagonal matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The eigenvalues of both matrices are the same, which are 1 and 3.\n\nThe matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is in Jordan form, which means it has a single Jordan block for each eigenvalue. The eigenvalues of this matrix are 3 and 1, with 3 being the eigenvalue of the 2x2 Jordan block.\n\nTo find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\), we need to find a matrix that transforms the diagonal matrix into the Jordan form. This means \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is the matrix of eigenvectors and generalized eigenvectors of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ -1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n1. **Find the eigenvectors and generalized eigenvectors:**\n\n   The eigenvalue 3 corresponds to the Jordan block, so we need a generalized eigenvector for it. The eigenvalue 1 corresponds to a regular eigenvector.\n\n   - For \\(\\lambda = 3\\), solve \\((A - 3I)\\mathbf{v} = 0\\):\n\n     \\[\n     \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 3 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n     \\]\n\n     This gives the eigenvector \\(\\mathbf{v}_1 = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n   - For the generalized eigenvector, solve \\((A - 3I)\\mathbf{w} = \\mathbf{v}_1\\):\n\n     \\[\n     \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 3 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n     \\]\n\n     Solving this system, we find \\(\\mathbf{w} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n   - For \\(\\lambda = 1\\), solve \\((A - I)\\mathbf{v} = 0\\):\n\n     \\[\n     \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 3 \\\\ -1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n     \\]\n\n     This gives the eigenvector \\(\\mathbf{v}_2 = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n2. **Construct the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\):**\n\n   The matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is constructed from the eigenvectors and generalized eigenvectors:\n\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 1 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Verify and calculate \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\):**\n\n   The determinant of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should be non-zero to ensure it is invertible:\n\n   \\[\n   \\det(\\mathbf{P}) = 3(-1) - 1(1) = -3 - 1 = -4 \\neq 0\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore, \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is invertible.\n\n   Calculate \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\):\n\n   \\[\n   |3| + |1| + |1| + |-1| = 3 + 1 + 1 + 1 = 6\n   \\]\n\nThus, the smallest possible value of \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\) is \\(\\boxed{6}\\)."
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) with the smallest magnitude such that \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix} \\times \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to consider the properties of the cross product and the geometry of the vectors involved.\n\n1. **Cross Product Properties**: The cross product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\) is perpendicular to both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is perpendicular to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n2. **Minimizing Magnitude**: The magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is minimized when \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is parallel to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\). This is because the cross product magnitude \\(|\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}|\\) is maximized when \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is perpendicular to \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), and minimized when \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is parallel to the direction of the cross product result.\n\n3. **Finding \\(\\mathbf{v}\\)**: We need to find \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) such that it is a scalar multiple of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and satisfies the cross product equation. Since \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) must be perpendicular to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we can express \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) as:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v} = k \\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   where \\(k\\) is a scalar.\n\n4. **Projection Method**: The smallest magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) occurs when \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is the projection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the plane perpendicular to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) onto a plane perpendicular to \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{b} - \\frac{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}}{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}} \\mathbf{a}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Calculate the Projection**:\n   - Compute \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}\\):\n     \\[\n     \\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix} = 90 \\cdot 1 + 30 \\cdot 2 + 30 \\cdot (-5) = 90 + 60 - 150 = 0\n     \\]\n   - Since the dot product is zero, \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is already perpendicular to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), meaning it lies in the plane.\n\n6. **Conclusion**: The vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) with the smallest magnitude is simply \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}\\) itself, as it is already perpendicular to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nTherefore, the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) with the smallest magnitude is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 90 \\\\ 30 \\\\ 30 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. The sine function, \\(\\sin\\), is a trigonometric function that relates the angle of a right triangle to the ratio of the length of the opposite side to the hypotenuse.\n\n2. The angle \\(90^\\circ\\) is a right angle. In the context of the unit circle, this angle corresponds to the point where the terminal side of the angle intersects the unit circle.\n\n3.
