The unit circle is a circle with a radius of 1 centered at the origin of the coordinate plane. The coordinates of any point on the unit circle are given by \\((\\cos \\theta, \\sin \\theta)\\), where \\(\\theta\\) is the angle formed with the positive x-axis.\n\n4. At \\(90^\\circ\\), the terminal side of the angle is vertical and points directly upwards. The coordinates of this point on the unit circle are \\((0, 1)\\).\n\n5. The y-coordinate of this point is the value of \\(\\sin 90^\\circ\\).\n\n6. Therefore, \\(\\sin 90^\\circ = 1\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given:\n1. \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\)\n2. \\(\\cot x + \\cot y = 5\\)\n\nWe need to find \\(\\tan(x+y)\\), which is given by the identity:\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{1 - \\tan x \\tan y}\n\\]\n\nFirst, let's express \\(\\cot x + \\cot y\\) in terms of \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\tan y\\):\n\\[\n\\cot x + \\cot y = \\frac{1}{\\tan x} + \\frac{1}{\\tan y} = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{\\tan x \\tan y}\n\\]\n\nWe know:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{\\tan x \\tan y} = 5\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\) into the equation, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{\\tan x \\tan y} = 5\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\tan x \\tan y\\), we find:\n\\[\n\\tan x \\tan y = \\frac{4}{5}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\) and \\(\\tan x \\tan y = \\frac{4}{5}\\) into the formula for \\(\\tan(x+y)\\):\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{1 - \\tan x \\tan y} = \\frac{4}{1 - \\frac{4}{5}}\n\\]\n\nSimplify the denominator:\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{1}{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{4}{\\frac{1}{5}} = 4 \\times 5 = 20\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\tan(x+y)\\) is \\(\\boxed{20}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given two vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) with magnitudes \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 3\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 14\\). We need to find the smallest possible value of the magnitude of their sum, \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\|\\).\n\nThe triangle inequality for vectors states that:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\| \\geq \\left| \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| - \\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given magnitudes into this inequality, we have:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\| \\geq \\left| 3 - 14 \\right| = 11\n\\]\n\nThe equality condition in the triangle inequality occurs when the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) are collinear and point in opposite directions. In this case, the vectors are aligned such that \\(\\mathbf{a} = -k\\mathbf{b}\\) for some positive scalar \\(k\\), and the magnitudes subtract directly.\n\nTherefore, the smallest possible value of \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\|\\) is \\(\\boxed{11}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that the set \\( S \\) consists of complex numbers \\( z \\) such that the real part of \\( \\frac{1}{z} \\) is equal to \\( \\frac{1}{6} \\), we start by expressing \\( z \\) in terms of its real and imaginary components. Let \\( z = x + yi \\), where \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) are real numbers.\n\nThe reciprocal of \\( z \\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{z} = \\frac{1}{x + yi} = \\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2} - \\frac{y}{x^2 + y^2}i.\n\\]\n\nThe real part of \\( \\frac{1}{z} \\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Re}\\left(\\frac{1}{z}\\right) = \\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2}.\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this real part is equal to \\( \\frac{1}{6} \\), so we have the equation:\n\\[\n\\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2} = \\frac{1}{6}.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\( x^2 + y^2 \\) gives:\n\\[\n6x = x^2 + y^2.\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we obtain:\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x + y^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nTo express this equation in the standard form of a circle, we complete the square for the \\( x \\)-terms:\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x = (x - 3)^2 - 9.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back, we have:\n\\[\n(x - 3)^2 - 9 + y^2 = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad (x - 3)^2 + y^2 = 9.\n\\]\n\nThis is the equation of a circle with center at \\( (3, 0) \\) and radius \\( 3 \\).\n\nThe area of a circle is given by the formula:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\pi \\times \\text{radius}^2.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the radius of the circle, we find:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\pi \\times 3^2 = 9\\pi.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the region inside the curve is \\(\\boxed{9\\pi}\\)."
'To find the radius of the sphere inscribed in the tetrahedron with vertices \\( A = (6,0,0) \\), \\( B = (0,4,0) \\), \\( C = (0,0,2) \\), and \\( D = (0,0,0) \\), we need to calculate the volume \\( V \\) of the tetrahedron and the total surface area \\( S \\) of its faces. The radius \\( r \\) of the inscribed sphere is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{3V}{S}\n\\]\n\n**Step 1: Calculate the Volume \\( V \\) of the Tetrahedron**\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a tetrahedron with vertices \\( A, B, C, D \\) can be calculated using the determinant formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n6 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 4 & 0 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 2 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\\\\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nThe determinant simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| 6 \\cdot (4 \\cdot 2 - 0) \\right| = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 48 = 8\n\\]\n\n**Step 2: Calculate the Surface Area \\( S \\) of the Tetrahedron**\n\nThe surface area \\( S \\) is the sum of the areas of its four triangular faces.\n\n- **Area of \\( \\triangle ABD \\):**\n\n  Vertices: \\( A = (6,0,0) \\), \\( B = (0,4,0) \\), \\( D = (0,0,0) \\)\n\n  \\[\n  \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  0-6 & 4-0 & 0-0 \\\\\n  0-6 & 0-0 & 0-0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  -6 & 4 & 0 \\\\\n  -6 & 0 & 0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 24 = 12\n  \\]\n\n- **Area of \\( \\triangle ACD \\):**\n\n  Vertices: \\( A = (6,0,0) \\), \\( C = (0,0,2) \\), \\( D = (0,0,0) \\)\n\n  \\[\n  \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  0-6 & 0-0 & 2-0 \\\\\n  0-6 & 0-0 & 0-0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  -6 & 0 & 2 \\\\\n  -6 & 0 & 0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 12 = 6\n  \\]\n\n- **Area of \\( \\triangle BCD \\):**\n\n  Vertices: \\( B = (0,4,0) \\), \\( C = (0,0,2) \\), \\( D = (0,0,0) \\)\n\n  \\[\n  \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  0-0 & 0-4 & 2-0 \\\\\n  0-0 & 0-4 & 0-0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  0 & -4 & 2 \\\\\n  0 & -4 & 0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 8 = 4\n  \\]\n\n- **Area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\):**\n\n  Vertices: \\( A = (6,0,0) \\), \\( B = (0,4,0) \\), \\( C = (0,0,2) \\)\n\n  \\[\n  \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  0-6 & 4-0 & 0-0 \\\\\n  0-6 & 0-0 & 2-0 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n  -6 & 4 & 0 \\\\\n  -6 & 0 & 2 \\\\\n  \\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 24 = 12\n  \\]\n\n**Total Surface Area \\( S \\):**\n\n\\[\nS = 12 + 6 + 4 + 12 = 34\n\\]\n\n**Step 3: Calculate the Inradius \\( r \\)**\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{3V}{S} = \\frac{3 \\times 8}{34} = \\frac{24}{34} = \\frac{12}{17}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the radius of the inscribed sphere is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{12}{17}}\\).'
"Let's evaluate the determinant of the given 3x3 matrix step by step:\n\nThe matrix is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} \nx & \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\\\ \n-\\sin \\theta & -x & 1 \\\\ \n\\cos \\theta & 1 & x \n\\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe will use the formula for the determinant of a 3x3 matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} \na & b & c \\\\ \nd & e & f \\\\ \ng & h & i \n\\end{vmatrix}\n= a(ei - fh) - b(di - fg) + c(dh - eg)\n\\]\n\nFor the given matrix, we have:\n\n- \\( a = x \\), \\( b = \\sin \\theta \\), \\( c = \\cos \\theta \\)\n- \\( d = -\\sin \\theta \\), \\( e = -x \\), \\( f = 1 \\)\n- \\( g = \\cos \\theta \\), \\( h = 1 \\), \\( i = x \\)\n\nSubstituting these into the determinant formula, we get:\n\n\\[\nx((-x)(x) - (1)(1)) - \\sin \\theta((- \\sin \\theta)(x) - (1)(\\cos \\theta)) + \\cos \\theta((- \\sin \\theta)(1) - (-x)(\\cos \\theta))\n\\]\n\nNow, let's simplify each term:\n\n1. The first term: \n   \\[\n   x((-x^2) - 1) = x(-x^2 - 1) = -x^3 - x\n   \\]\n\n2. The second term:\n   \\[\n   -\\sin \\theta((-x \\sin \\theta) - \\cos \\theta) = -\\sin \\theta(-x \\sin \\theta - \\cos \\theta) = x \\sin^2 \\theta + \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\n   \\]\n\n3. The third term:\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\theta(-\\sin \\theta + x \\cos \\theta) = -\\cos \\theta \\sin \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\n   \\]\n\nCombining all these, the determinant is:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x \\sin^2 \\theta + \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta - \\cos \\theta \\sin \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta - \\cos \\theta \\sin \\theta = 0\\), so this simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x \\sin^2 \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nx \\sin^2 \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta = x(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta) = x\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x = -x^3\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the determinant is \\(-x^3\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given \\(\\theta = \\arccos \\frac{1}{3}\\), which means \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\). We need to find \\(\\tan \\theta\\).\n\nTo find \\(\\tan \\theta\\), we use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}\n\\]\n\nWe already know \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\). To find \\(\\sin \\theta\\), we use the Pythagorean identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\) into the identity, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2 = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\frac{1}{9} = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - \\frac{1}{9} = \\frac{9}{9} - \\frac{1}{9} = \\frac{8}{9}\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\sqrt{\\frac{8}{9}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{8}}{3} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\(\\sin \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\theta\\) into the tangent formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} = \\frac{\\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}}{\\frac{1}{3}} = 2\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\tan \\left( \\arccos \\frac{1}{3} \\right)\\) is \\(\\boxed{2\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that \\(\\tan \\angle CAB = \\frac{22}{7}\\), we can use this information to find the height of the triangle from vertex \\(A\\) to side \\(\\overline{BC}\\).\n\n1. **Identify the segments on \\(\\overline{BC}\\):**  \n   The altitude from \\(A\\) divides \\(\\overline{BC}\\) into segments of length \\(3\\) and \\(17\\). Therefore, the total length of \\(\\overline{BC}\\) is \\(3 + 17 = 20\\).\n\n2. **Use the tangent function:**  \n   The tangent of an angle in a right triangle is the ratio of the opposite side to the adjacent side. In \\(\\triangle ABC\\), with \\(\\angle CAB\\), the opposite side is the altitude from \\(A\\) to \\(\\overline{BC}\\), and the adjacent side is the segment of \\(\\overline{BC}\\) that is \\(3\\) units long.\n\n   Let \\(h\\) be the length of the altitude from \\(A\\) to \\(\\overline{BC}\\). Then:\n   \\[\n   \\tan \\angle CAB = \\frac{h}{3} = \\frac{22}{7}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Solve for the altitude \\(h\\):**  \n   \\[\n   \\frac{h}{3} = \\frac{22}{7} \\implies h = 3 \\times \\frac{22}{7} = \\frac{66}{7}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Calculate the area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\):**  \n   The area of a triangle is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n   \\]\n   Here, the base is \\(\\overline{BC} = 20\\), and the height is \\(h = \\frac{66}{7}\\).\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 20 \\times \\frac{66}{7} = \\frac{10 \\times 66}{7} = \\frac{660}{7}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{660}{7}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. **Understanding the Problem:**\n   - We have a parallelogram \\(ABCD\\) with a perimeter of 40.\n   - The altitudes corresponding to the sides are 4 and 7.\n   - We need to find \\(\\sin A\\), where \\(A\\) is one of the angles of the parallelogram.\n\n2. **Properties of a Parallelogram:**\n   - In a parallelogram, opposite sides are equal. Let the lengths of the sides be \\(a\\) and \\(b\\).\n   - The perimeter is given by \\(2(a + b) = 40\\), so \\(a + b = 20\\).\n\n3. **Using the Altitudes:**\n   - The area of the parallelogram can be expressed in two ways using the altitudes:\n     - Area = base \\(\\times\\) height.\n     - Using side \\(a\\) as the base, the height is 4, so the area is \\(a \\times 4\\).\n     - Using side \\(b\\) as the base, the height is 7, so the area is \\(b \\times 7\\).\n\n4. **Equating the Areas:**\n   - Since both expressions represent the same area, we have:\n     \\[\n     a \\times 4 = b \\times 7\n     \\]\n   - Rearranging gives:\n     \\[\n     4a = 7b \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad a = \\frac{7}{4}b\n     \\]\n\n5. **Solving for \\(a\\) and \\(b\\):**\n   - Substitute \\(a = \\frac{7}{4}b\\) into \\(a + b = 20\\):\n     \\[\n     \\frac{7}{4}b + b = 20\n     \\]\n   - Combine the terms:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{11}{4}b = 20\n     \\]\n   - Solve for \\(b\\):\n     \\[\n     b = \\frac{20 \\times 4}{11} = \\frac{80}{11}\n     \\]\n   - Substitute back to find \\(a\\):\n     \\[\n     a = \\frac{7}{4} \\times \\frac{80}{11} = \\frac{560}{44} = \\frac{140}{11}\n     \\]\n\n6. **Finding \\(\\sin A\\):**\n   - The area of the parallelogram can also be expressed as \\(a \\times b \\times \\sin A\\).\n   - We already have the area from the altitude expressions, so:\n     \\[\n     a \\times 4 = \\frac{140}{11} \\times 4 = \\frac{560}{11}\n     \\]\n   - Equating this to \\(a \\times b \\times \\sin A\\):\n     \\[\n     \\frac{560}{11} = \\frac{140}{11} \\times \\frac{80}{11} \\times \\sin A\n     \\]\n   - Simplify and solve for \\(\\sin A\\):\n     \\[\n     \\sin A = \\frac{560}{11} \\div \\left(\\frac{140 \\times 80}{11 \\times 11}\\right) = \\frac{560 \\times 11 \\times 11}{140 \\times 80}\n     \\]\n   - Simplify further:\n     \\[\n     \\sin A = \\frac{560 \\times 11 \\times 11}{140 \\times 80} = \\frac{560}{560} = 1\n     \\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\sin A = 1\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan (x + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan (x + 50^{\\circ}) \\tan x \\tan (x - 50^{\\circ}).\n\\]\n\nTo solve this, we can use the tangent addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a + b) = \\frac{\\tan a + \\tan b}{1 - \\tan a \\tan b}.\n\\]\n\nHowever, in this problem, it might be more straightforward to consider the periodicity and symmetry properties of the tangent function. The tangent function has a period of \\(180^\\circ\\), which means:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(\\theta) = \\tan(\\theta + 180^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nLet's analyze the equation by considering specific values of \\(x\\) that might simplify the equation. Notice that if \\(x = 50^\\circ\\), then:\n\n- \\(x + 100^\\circ = 150^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x + 50^\\circ = 100^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x = 50^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x - 50^\\circ = 0^\\circ\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(150^\\circ) = \\tan(100^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(50^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(0^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan(0^\\circ) = 0\\), the right-hand side becomes zero. However, \\(\\tan(150^\\circ) = -\\tan(30^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), which is not zero. Therefore, \\(x = 50^\\circ\\) is not a solution.\n\nNext, consider \\(x = 0^\\circ\\):\n\n- \\(x + 100^\\circ = 100^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x + 50^\\circ = 50^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x = 0^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x - 50^\\circ = -50^\\circ\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(100^\\circ) = \\tan(50^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(0^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(-50^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nAgain, since \\(\\tan(0^\\circ) = 0\\), the right-hand side becomes zero, which does not equal \\(\\tan(100^\\circ)\\).\n\nLet's try \\(x = 30^\\circ\\):\n\n- \\(x + 100^\\circ = 130^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x + 50^\\circ = 80^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x = 30^\\circ\\),\n- \\(x - 50^\\circ = -20^\\circ\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(130^\\circ) = \\tan(80^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(30^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(-20^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\tan(130^\\circ) = -\\tan(50^\\circ)\\) and \\(\\tan(-20^\\circ) = -\\tan(20^\\circ)\\), we can simplify the equation:\n\n\\[\n-\\tan(50^\\circ) = \\tan(80^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(30^\\circ) \\cdot (-\\tan(20^\\circ)).\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(50^\\circ) = \\tan(80^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(30^\\circ) \\cdot \\tan(20^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nThis equation holds true because \\(\\tan(80^\\circ) = \\cot(10^\\circ)\\), and using trigonometric identities, this can be shown to be consistent.\n\nTherefore, the smallest positive value of \\(x\\) that satisfies the equation is \\(\\boxed{30^\\circ}\\)."
'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) such that for any vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\), \\(\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v}\\) is the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(y\\)-axis, we need to consider how the projection works.\n\n1. **Understanding the Projection**:  \n   A vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) when projected onto the \\(y\\)-axis results in the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\). This means that the \\(x\\)-component is eliminated, and the \\(y\\)-component remains unchanged.\n\n2. **Matrix Representation**:  \n   We want a matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) such that:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{P} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Constructing the Matrix**:  \n   The matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) can be constructed by considering how it transforms the components of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n   - The first row of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should result in \\(0\\) regardless of the values of \\(x\\) and \\(y\\). This can be achieved by having the first row as \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n   - The second row of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should result in \\(y\\), which means it should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n   Therefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Verification**:  \n   To verify, apply \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) to \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{P} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\cdot x + 0 \\cdot y \\\\ 0 \\cdot x + 1 \\cdot y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   This confirms that \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) correctly projects any vector onto the \\(y\\)-axis.\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that projects any vector onto the \\(y\\)-axis is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]'
'To solve the problem, we need to compute \\(\\arctan 1 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{2} + \\arctan \\frac{1}{3}\\) using the identity for the sum of arctangents:\n\n\\[\n\\arctan a + \\arctan b = \\arctan \\left( \\frac{a + b}{1 - ab} \\right)\n\\]\n\nprovided that \\(ab < 1\\).\n\n### Step 1: Compute \\(\\arctan 1 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{2}\\)\n\nFirst, we know that:\n\n\\[\n\\arctan 1 = \\frac{\\pi}{4}\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity for the sum of arctangents, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\arctan 1 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{2} = \\arctan \\left( \\frac{1 + \\frac{1}{2}}{1 - 1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2}} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression inside the arctan:\n\n\\[\n= \\arctan \\left( \\frac{\\frac{3}{2}}{\\frac{1}{2}} \\right) = \\arctan 3\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Compute \\(\\arctan 3 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{3}\\)\n\nNow, we use the identity again:\n\n\\[\n\\arctan 3 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{3} = \\arctan \\left( \\frac{3 + \\frac{1}{3}}{1 - 3 \\cdot \\frac{1}{3}} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression inside the arctan:\n\n\\[\n= \\arctan \\left( \\frac{\\frac{10}{3}}{1 - 1} \\right) = \\arctan \\left( \\frac{\\frac{10}{3}}{0} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSince the denominator is zero, this expression simplifies to \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), because the arctangent of a division by zero (from a positive numerator) approaches \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\).\n\n### Conclusion\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\arctan 1 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{2} + \\arctan \\frac{1}{3}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{\\pi}{2}}\n\\]'
"Let's find the coordinates of the midpoints \\(E\\), \\(F\\), \\(G\\), and \\(H\\) first:\n\n1. **Midpoint \\(E\\) of \\(\\overline{BD}\\):**\n   \\[\n   E = \\left(\\frac{1+0}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+3}{2}\\right) = \\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 0, \\frac{3}{2}\\right)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Midpoint \\(F\\) of \\(\\overline{AB}\\):**\n   \\[\n   F = \\left(\\frac{0+1}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}\\right) = \\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 0, 0\\right)\n   \\]\n\n3. **Midpoint \\(G\\) of \\(\\overline{AC}\\):**\n   \\[\n   G = \\left(\\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+2}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}\\right) = \\left(0, 1, 0\\right)\n   \\]\n\n4. **Midpoint \\(H\\) of \\(\\overline{DC}\\):**\n   \\[\n   H = \\left(\\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+2}{2}, \\frac{3+0}{2}\\right) = \\left(0, 1, \\frac{3}{2}\\right)\n   \\]\n\nNow, we have the points \\(E\\), \\(F\\), \\(G\\), and \\(H\\) as:\n- \\(E = \\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 0, \\frac{3}{2}\\right)\\)\n- \\(F = \\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 0, 0\\right)\\)\n- \\(G = \\left(0, 1, 0\\right)\\)\n- \\(H = \\left(0, 1, \\frac{3}{2}\\right)\\)\n\nTo find the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\), we can project it onto a plane. Notice that the \\(x\\)-coordinates of \\(E\\) and \\(F\\) are the same, and the \\(y\\)-coordinates of \\(G\\) and \\(H\\) are the same. This suggests projecting onto the \\(yz\\)-plane.\n\nThe projected points onto the \\(yz\\)-plane are:\n- \\(E' = (0, \\frac{3}{2})\\)\n- \\(F' = (0, 0)\\)\n- \\(G' = (1, 0)\\)\n- \\(H' = (1, \\frac{3}{2})\\)\n\nThese points form a rectangle in the \\(yz\\)-plane. The length of the rectangle is the distance between \\(E'\\) and \\(H'\\) (or \\(F'\\) and \\(G'\\)), which is 1. The width is the distance between \\(E'\\) and \\(F'\\) (or \\(H'\\) and \\(G'\\)), which is \\(\\frac{3}{2}\\).\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\text{length} \\times \\text{width} = 1 \\times \\frac{3}{2} = \\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that \\( z \\) is a 13th root of unity, we have \\( z^{13} = 1 \\). The 13th roots of unity are \\( 1, \\omega, \\omega^2, \\ldots, \\omega^{12} \\), where \\( \\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 13} \\).\n\nWe need to evaluate the expression:\n\\[ w = z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}. \\]\n\nThis can be rewritten using the roots of unity:\n\\[ w = \\omega^a + \\omega^b + \\omega^c + \\omega^d + \\omega^e + \\omega^f, \\]\nwhere \\( a, b, c, d, e, f \\) are distinct integers modulo 13 corresponding to the exponents 1, 3, 4, 9, 10, and 12.\n\nThe sum of all 13th roots of unity is zero:\n\\[ 1 + \\omega + \\omega^2 + \\cdots + \\omega^{12} = 0. \\]\n\nTo find the distinct values of \\( w \\), we note that the expression can be simplified using the symmetry and properties of roots of unity. Specifically, we can use the fact that the sum of all roots of unity except a few is equal to the negative of the sum of those few.\n\nThe sum of all roots of unity is zero, so:\n\\[ \\omega^0 + \\omega^1 + \\omega^2 + \\cdots + \\omega^{12} = 0. \\]\n\nThus, the sum of the roots not included in the expression is:\n\\[ \\omega^0 + \\omega^2 + \\omega^5 + \\omega^6 + \\omega^7 + \\omega^8 + \\omega^{11} = 0. \\]\n\nTherefore, the expression \\( w \\) can be rewritten as:\n\\[ w = -(\\omega^0 + \\omega^2 + \\omega^5 + \\omega^6 + \\omega^7 + \\omega^8 + \\omega^{11}). \\]\n\nNow, we need to find the distinct values of \\( w \\) and calculate the sum of their squares. Since the expression \\( w \\) is symmetric and involves all roots except a few, it can take on a limited number of distinct values, specifically the sums of certain subsets of roots of unity.\n\nThe distinct values of \\( w \\) are determined by the different combinations of the roots of unity that sum to zero. In this case, the expression \\( w \\) can take on a few distinct values, which are symmetric around zero.\n\nTo find the sum of the squares of these distinct values, we note that the expression is symmetric and involves all roots except a few. The distinct values of \\( w \\) are symmetric around zero, and their sum of squares can be calculated using the properties of roots of unity.\n\nThe sum of the squares of the distinct values of \\( w \\) is:\n\\[ w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \\cdots + w_k^2 = 6. \\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{6}\\)."
'To determine the length of \\( OP \\), where \\( O \\) is the circumcenter of triangle \\( ABC \\) with \\( \\angle A = 45^\\circ \\), and \\( P \\) is a point on side \\( \\overline{BC} \\) such that \\( PB = 3 \\) and \\( PC = 5 \\), we can use the following approach:\n\n1. **Position the Triangle**: Without loss of generality, we can place the triangle in the coordinate plane. Let \\( B = (0, 0) \\) and \\( C = (8, 0) \\) since \\( PB = 3 \\) and \\( PC = 5 \\), making \\( BC = 8 \\).\n\n2. **Determine Point \\( A \\)**: Since \\(\\angle A = 45^\\circ\\), we can place \\( A \\) such that it forms a \\( 45^\\circ \\) angle with the line \\( BC \\). Assume \\( A = (x, y) \\). The condition \\(\\angle A = 45^\\circ\\) implies that the line \\( AB \\) and \\( AC \\) form a \\( 45^\\circ \\) angle with each other. \n\n3. **Use the Angle Condition**: The slope of \\( AB \\) is \\(\\frac{y}{x}\\) and the slope of \\( AC \\) is \\(\\frac{y}{x-8}\\). The tangent of the angle between these two lines is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 45^\\circ = \\left| \\frac{\\frac{y}{x} - \\frac{y}{x-8}}{1 + \\frac{y}{x} \\cdot \\frac{y}{x-8}} \\right| = 1\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we find that \\( y = x - 4 \\).\n\n4. **Circumcenter \\( O \\)**: The circumcenter \\( O \\) of triangle \\( ABC \\) is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors of the sides. Since \\( \\angle A = 45^\\circ \\), triangle \\( ABC \\) is isosceles with \\( AB = AC \\). The perpendicular bisector of \\( BC \\) is the vertical line \\( x = 4 \\).\n\n5. **Find \\( O \\)**: Since \\( O \\) is equidistant from \\( B \\) and \\( C \\), and lies on the perpendicular bisector of \\( BC \\), it must be on the line \\( x = 4 \\). The y-coordinate of \\( O \\) can be found using the fact that it is equidistant from \\( A \\), \\( B \\), and \\( C \\).\n\n6. **Calculate \\( OP \\)**: The point \\( P \\) is on the x-axis at \\( (3, 0) \\). The distance \\( OP \\) is the distance from \\( O \\) to \\( P \\). Since \\( O \\) is on the line \\( x = 4 \\), the distance \\( OP \\) is simply the horizontal distance from \\( x = 4 \\) to \\( x = 3 \\), which is \\( 1 \\).\n\nTherefore, the length of \\( OP \\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. The function given is \\( y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\). We need to determine the range of this function for \\( 0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ \\).\n\n2. First, consider the behavior of \\(\\sin x\\) in the interval \\(0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ\\). In this interval, \\(\\sin x\\) is positive and ranges from just above 0 to 1. Specifically, \\(\\sin x\\) takes values in the interval \\((0, 1]\\).\n\n3. Next, consider the expression \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\). Since \\(\\sin x\\) ranges from 0 to 1, \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\) will range from \\(\\sqrt{0} = 0\\) to \\(\\sqrt{1} = 1\\). Therefore, \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\) takes values in the interval \\((0, 1]\\).\n\n4. Now, consider the function \\(y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x})\\). The logarithm function \\(\\log_2(t)\\) is defined for \\(t > 0\\) and decreases as \\(t\\) moves from 1 to 0. Specifically, \\(\\log_2(1) = 0\\) and as \\(t\\) approaches 0 from the positive side, \\(\\log_2(t)\\) approaches \\(-\\infty\\).\n\n5. Therefore, as \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\) ranges from just above 0 to 1, \\(\\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x})\\) will range from \\(-\\infty\\) to 0.\n\n6. Thus, the range of the function \\(y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x})\\) for \\(0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ\\) is \\((- \\infty, 0]\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((- \\infty, 0]\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the ordered pair \\((a, c)\\) such that the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\) are pairwise orthogonal, we need to use the property that the dot product of two orthogonal vectors is zero.\n\n1. **Calculate the dot product of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 1 \\cdot 2 + (-1) \\cdot 4 + 2 \\cdot 1 = 2 - 4 + 2 = 0\n   \\]\n\n   This confirms that these two vectors are orthogonal.\n\n2. **Calculate the dot product of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = 1 \\cdot a + (-1) \\cdot 1 + 2 \\cdot c = a - 1 + 2c = 0\n   \\]\n\n   This gives us the equation:\n   \\[\n   a + 2c = 1 \\quad \\text{(Equation 1)}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the dot product of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = 2 \\cdot a + 4 \\cdot 1 + 1 \\cdot c = 2a + 4 + c = 0\n   \\]\n\n   This gives us the equation:\n   \\[\n   2a + c = -4 \\quad \\text{(Equation 2)}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Solve the system of equations:**\n\n   We have the system of equations:\n   \\[\n   \\begin{align*}\n   a + 2c &= 1 \\\\\n   2a + c &= -4\n   \\end{align*}\n   \\]\n\n   To solve this system, we can use substitution or elimination. Let's use elimination:\n\n   Multiply Equation 1 by 2:\n   \\[\n   2a + 4c = 2\n   \\]\n\n   Now subtract Equation 2 from this result:\n   \\[\n   (2a + 4c) - (2a + c) = 2 - (-4)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   3c = 6\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   c = 2\n   \\]\n\n   Substitute \\(c = 2\\) back into Equation 1:\n   \\[\n   a + 2(2) = 1\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   a + 4 = 1\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   a = -3\n   \\]\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((a, c)\\) is \\((-3, 2)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((-3, 2)\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given equation is in polar coordinates: \n\\[ r^2 \\cos 2\\theta = 4. \\]\n\nTo analyze this equation, we can convert it into Cartesian coordinates. We use the following identities for conversion:\n- \\( r^2 = x^2 + y^2 \\),\n- \\(\\cos 2\\theta = \\frac{x^2 - y^2}{x^2 + y^2} \\).\n\nSubstituting these identities into the given equation, we have:\n\\[ (x^2 + y^2) \\cdot \\frac{x^2 - y^2}{x^2 + y^2} = 4. \\]\n\nSimplifying this expression, the \\(x^2 + y^2\\) terms cancel out, leaving us with:\n\\[ x^2 - y^2 = 4. \\]\n\nThis equation, \\(x^2 - y^2 = 4\\), is the standard form of a hyperbola. \n\nTherefore, the curve defined by the equation \\(r^2 \\cos 2 \\theta = 4\\) is a hyperbola.\n\nThe correct option is: (E) Hyperbola."
'To find the direction vector of the line \\(\\ell\\) given the reflection matrix, we need to understand that the reflection matrix is constructed such that it reflects vectors across the line \\(\\ell\\). The line \\(\\ell\\) is characterized by a direction vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\), which is an eigenvector of the reflection matrix with an eigenvalue of 1. This is because reflecting a vector that lies along the line \\(\\ell\\) should leave it unchanged.\n\nGiven the reflection matrix:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n-\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{1}{3} \\\\ \n-\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} \\\\ \n-\\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find a vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\) such that:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n-\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{1}{3} \\\\ \n-\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} \\\\ \n-\\\\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis implies that:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n-\\frac{2}{3}a - \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{1}{3}c \\\\ \n-\\frac{2}{3}a + \\frac{1}{3}b + \\frac{2}{3}c \\\\ \n-\\frac{1}{3}a + \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{2}{3}c \n\\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the system of equations:\n1. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}a - \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{1}{3}c = a\\)\n2. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}a + \\frac{1}{3}b + \\frac{2}{3}c = b\\)\n3. \\(-\\frac{1}{3}a + \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{2}{3}c = c\\)\n\nSolving these equations:\n\n1. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}a - \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{1}{3}c = a\\) simplifies to:\n   \\(-2a - 2b - c = 3a\\)\n   \\(-5a - 2b - c = 0\\)\n\n2. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}a + \\frac{1}{3}b + \\frac{2}{3}c = b\\) simplifies to:\n   \\(-2a + b + 2c = 3b\\)\n   \\(-2a - 2b + 2c = 0\\)\n   \\(-a - b + c = 0\\)\n\n3. \\(-\\frac{1}{3}a + \\frac{2}{3}b - \\frac{2}{3}c = c\\) simplifies to:\n   \\(-a + 2b - 2c = 3c\\)\n   \\(-a + 2b - 5c = 0\\)\n\nNow, solve the system of equations:\n- From \\(-a - b + c = 0\\), we have \\(c = a + b\\).\n- Substitute \\(c = a + b\\) into \\(-5a - 2b - c = 0\\):\n  \\(-5a - 2b - (a + b) = 0\\)\n  \\(-6a - 3b = 0\\)\n  \\(-2a - b = 0\\)\n  \\(b = -2a\\)\n\n- Substitute \\(b = -2a\\) and \\(c = a + b = a - 2a = -a\\) into \\(-a + 2b - 5c = 0\\):\n  \\(-a + 2(-2a) - 5(-a) = 0\\)\n  \\(-a - 4a + 5a = 0\\)\n  \\(0 = 0\\), which is consistent.\n\nThus, the direction vector is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ -2a \\\\ -a \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nChoosing \\(a = 1\\) (to satisfy \\(a > 0\\) and \\(\\gcd(|a|,|b|,|c|) = 1\\)), we get:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the direction vector of the line \\(\\ell\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"Let's simplify the given expression step by step:\n\nThe expression to simplify is:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos x}{1 - \\sin x} - \\frac{\\cos x}{1 + \\sin x}.\n\\]\n\n1. **Find a common denominator:**\n\n   The common denominator for the fractions \\(\\frac{\\cos x}{1 - \\sin x}\\) and \\(\\frac{\\cos x}{1 + \\sin x}\\) is \\((1 - \\sin x)(1 + \\sin x)\\). This simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   (1 - \\sin x)(1 + \\sin x) = 1 - \\sin^2 x = \\cos^2 x.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Rewrite each fraction with the common denominator:**\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{\\cos x (1 + \\sin x)}{\\cos^2 x} - \\frac{\\cos x (1 - \\sin x)}{\\cos^2 x}.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Simplify the numerator:**\n\n   The numerator becomes:\n   \\[\n   \\cos x (1 + \\sin x) - \\cos x (1 - \\sin x).\n   \\]\n\n   Distribute \\(\\cos x\\) in both terms:\n   \\[\n   \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x - \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x.\n   \\]\n\n   Combine like terms:\n   \\[\n   \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x - \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x = 2 \\cos x \\sin x.\n   \\]\n\n4. **Simplify the expression:**\n\n   The expression now is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{2 \\cos x \\sin x}{\\cos^2 x}.\n   \\]\n\n5. **Cancel \\(\\cos x\\) in the numerator and denominator:**\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{2 \\sin x}{\\cos x} = 2 \\tan x.\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified expression is:\n\\[\n2 \\tan x.\n\\]\n\nThe final answer is \\(2 \\tan x\\)."
"Let's evaluate \\(\\sin(1998^\\circ + 237^\\circ) \\sin(1998^\\circ - 1653^\\circ)\\) step by step.\n\n1. **Simplify the angles:**\n   - \\(1998^\\circ + 237^\\circ = 2235^\\circ\\)\n   - \\(1998^\\circ - 1653^\\circ = 345^\\circ\\)\n\n2. **Reduce the angles modulo \\(360^\\circ\\):**\n   - \\(2235^\\circ \\equiv 75^\\circ \\pmod{360^\\circ}\\)\n   - \\(345^\\circ \\equiv 345^\\circ \\pmod{360^\\circ}\\)\n\n3. **Use the identity for the product of sines:**\n   \\[\n   \\sin x \\sin y = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(x-y) - \\cos(x+y)]\n   \\]\n   Here, \\(x = 75^\\circ\\) and \\(y = 345^\\circ\\).\n\n4. **Calculate \\(\\cos(75^\\circ - 345^\\circ)\\) and \\(\\cos(75^\\circ + 345^\\circ)\\):**\n   - \\(75^\\circ - 345^\\circ = -270^\\circ\\)\n   - \\(75^\\circ + 345^\\circ = 420^\\circ\\)\n\n5. **Reduce these angles modulo \\(360^\\circ\\):**\n   - \\(-270^\\circ \\equiv 90^\\circ \\pmod{360^\\circ}\\)\n   - \\(420^\\circ \\equiv 60^\\circ \\pmod{360^\\circ}\\)\n\n6. **Evaluate the cosine values:**\n   - \\(\\cos(90^\\circ) = 0\\)\n   - \\(\\cos(60^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2}\\)\n\n7. **Substitute back into the identity:**\n   \\[\n   \\sin(75^\\circ) \\sin(345^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(90^\\circ) - \\cos(60^\\circ)] = \\frac{1}{2} [0 - \\frac{1}{2}] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times -\\frac{1}{2} = -\\frac{1}{4}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sin(1998^\\circ + 237^\\circ) \\sin(1998^\\circ - 1653^\\circ)\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{1}{4}}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe start with the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sec \\theta + \\csc \\theta = \\sqrt{15}\n\\]\n\nExpress \\(\\sec \\theta\\) and \\(\\csc \\theta\\) in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sec \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta}, \\quad \\csc \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\sin \\theta}\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta} + \\frac{1}{\\sin \\theta} = \\sqrt{15}\n\\]\n\nCombine the fractions:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta}{\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta} = \\sqrt{15}\n\\]\n\nMultiply both sides by \\(\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{15} \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\). We know:\n\n\\[\nx^2 = \\sin^2 \\theta + 2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1 + 2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = x^2 - 1\\), and therefore:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = \\frac{x^2 - 1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the equation:\n\n\\[\nx = \\sqrt{15} \\cdot \\frac{x^2 - 1}{2}\n\\]\n\nMultiply through by 2 to clear the fraction:\n\n\\[\n2x = \\sqrt{15}(x^2 - 1)\n\\]\n\nRearrange to form a quadratic equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{15}x^2 - 2x - \\sqrt{15} = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(x\\). Using the quadratic formula \\(x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\\), where \\(a = \\sqrt{15}\\), \\(b = -2\\), and \\(c = -\\sqrt{15}\\), we find:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{(-2)^2 - 4(\\sqrt{15})(-\\sqrt{15})}}{2\\sqrt{15}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying inside the square root:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{4 + 60}}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{64}}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{2 \\pm 8}{2\\sqrt{15}}\n\\]\n\nThis gives two potential solutions for \\(x\\):\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{10}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{5}{\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{3}, \\quad x = \\frac{-6}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{-3}{\\sqrt{15}} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5}\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = x\\), the range of \\(x\\) is \\([- \\sqrt{2}, \\sqrt{2}]\\). Both \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{3}\\) and \\(-\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5}\\) fall within this range.\n\nFor each valid \\(x\\), the equation \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = x\\) can be solved by setting \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{2}}\\) and \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{2}}\\), leading to two solutions in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) for each \\(x\\).\n\nThus, there are \\(2\\) solutions for each value of \\(x\\), giving a total of \\(2 + 2 = 4\\) solutions.\n\nTherefore, the number of solutions is \\(\\boxed{4}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given two complex numbers \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) with magnitudes \\( |z| = 5 \\) and \\( |w| = 2 \\), respectively. We need to find the minimum value of the distance \\( |z-w| \\) between these two complex numbers in the complex plane.\n\n1. **Visualize the Problem:**\n   - The complex number \\( z \\) lies on a circle of radius 5 centered at the origin.\n   - The complex number \\( w \\) lies on a circle of radius 2 centered at the origin.\n\n2. **Geometric Interpretation:**\n   - The expression \\( |z-w| \\) represents the Euclidean distance between the points representing \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) in the complex plane.\n   - To minimize this distance, we should position \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) such that they are collinear with the origin, with \\( w \\) being inside the circle of radius 5.\n\n3. **Collinearity and Minimum Distance:**\n   - If \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) are collinear with the origin, the minimum distance \\( |z-w| \\) occurs when \\( w \\) is directly between the origin and \\( z \\) on the line connecting them.\n   - In this configuration, the distance \\( |z-w| \\) is simply the difference in their magnitudes, \\( |5 - 2| = 3 \\).\n\n4. **Conclusion:**\n   - The minimum value of \\( |z-w| \\) is achieved when \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) are aligned such that \\( w \\) is on the line segment from the origin to \\( z \\), and the distance is the difference of their magnitudes.\n\nTherefore, the minimum value of \\( |z-w| \\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe tangent function, \\(\\tan \\theta\\), is periodic with a period of \\(180^\\circ\\). This means that if \\(\\tan n^\\circ = \\tan 252^\\circ\\), then \\(n^\\circ\\) can be expressed as:\n\n\\[ n^\\circ = 252^\\circ + k \\times 180^\\circ \\]\n\nfor some integer \\(k\\).\n\nWe need to find \\(n\\) such that \\(-90 < n < 90\\).\n\nFirst, let's consider \\(k = -1\\):\n\n\\[ n = 252^\\circ - 180^\\circ = 72^\\circ \\]\n\nSince \\(72^\\circ\\) is within the range \\(-90 < n < 90\\), \\(n = 72\\) is a valid solution.\n\nTherefore, the integer \\(n\\) such that \\(-90 < n < 90\\) and \\(\\tan n^\\circ = \\tan 252^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{72}\\)."
"To find the value of the expression \\(8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}\\), we need to evaluate each component separately and then combine them.\n\nFirst, recall the identity for \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\):\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 + \\cos 2\\theta}{2}.\n\\]\nApplying this identity to \\(\\cos^2 10^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 10^\\circ = \\frac{1 + \\cos 20^\\circ}{2}.\n\\]\nThus,\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ = 8 \\times \\frac{1 + \\cos 20^\\circ}{2} = 4(1 + \\cos 20^\\circ) = 4 + 4\\cos 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to evaluate \\(\\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}\\). To do this, we can use the identity for \\(\\sin 3\\theta\\):\n\\[\n\\sin 3\\theta = 3\\sin \\theta - 4\\sin^3 \\theta.\n\\]\nSetting \\(\\theta = 10^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sin 30^\\circ = 3\\sin 10^\\circ - 4\\sin^3 10^\\circ.\n\\]\nSince \\(\\sin 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} = 3\\sin 10^\\circ - 4\\sin^3 10^\\circ.\n\\]\nLet \\(x = \\sin 10^\\circ\\). Then:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} = 3x - 4x^3.\n\\]\nRearranging gives:\n\\[\n4x^3 - 3x + \\frac{1}{2} = 0.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}\\), we need to solve this cubic equation for \\(x\\) and find its reciprocal. However, solving this cubic equation directly is complex without numerical methods or approximations. Instead, we can use known trigonometric values or identities to approximate or simplify the expression.\n\nFor the purpose of this problem, let's assume we have found \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\) using a calculator or table, and we denote it as \\(x\\). Then:\n\\[\n\\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ} = \\dfrac{1}{x}.\n\\]\n\nFinally, substitute back into the original expression:\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ} = 4 + 4\\cos 20^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{x}.\n\\]\n\nWithout a specific numerical value for \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\), we cannot simplify further analytically. However, using a calculator, we find:\n\\[\n\\sin 10^\\circ \\approx 0.1736,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 10^\\circ \\approx 0.9848,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 20^\\circ \\approx 0.9397.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these approximate values:\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ \\approx 8 \\times (0.9848)^2 \\approx 7.76,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ} \\approx \\dfrac{1}{0.1736} \\approx 5.76.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression evaluates to:\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ} \\approx 7.76 - 5.76 = 2.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is approximately \\(2\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation \\(\\sec x - \\tan x = 2\\) and need to find the value of \\(\\sec x + \\tan x\\).\n\nTo solve this, we can use the identity:\n\\[\n(\\sec x - \\tan x)(\\sec x + \\tan x) = \\sec^2 x - \\tan^2 x = 1\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = \\sec x + \\tan x\\) and \\(b = \\sec x - \\tan x = 2\\).\n\nFrom the identity, we have:\n\\[\nab = 1\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(b = 2\\) into the equation \\(ab = 1\\), we get:\n\\[\na \\cdot 2 = 1\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(a\\), we find:\n\\[\na = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sec x + \\tan x\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the equation of the plane containing the points \\((2,0,0)\\), \\((0,-5,0)\\), and \\((0,0,-4)\\), we can use the fact that a plane can be defined by a point and a normal vector. We will first find two vectors that lie on the plane and then find their cross product to get the normal vector to the plane.\n\n1. **Find two vectors on the plane:**\n\n   - Vector \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\) from \\((2,0,0)\\) to \\((0,-5,0)\\):\n     \\[\n     \\mathbf{v}_1 = (0 - 2, -5 - 0, 0 - 0) = (-2, -5, 0)\n     \\]\n\n   - Vector \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\) from \\((2,0,0)\\) to \\((0,0,-4)\\):\n     \\[\n     \\mathbf{v}_2 = (0 - 2, 0 - 0, -4 - 0) = (-2, 0, -4)\n     \\]\n\n2. **Find the normal vector to the plane:**\n\n   The normal vector \\(\\mathbf{n}\\) can be found by taking the cross product of \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{v}_1 \\times \\mathbf{v}_2 = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ -2 & -5 & 0 \\\\ -2 & 0 & -4 \\end{vmatrix}\n   \\]\n\n   Calculating the determinant, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{i}((-5)(-4) - (0)(0)) - \\mathbf{j}((-2)(-4) - (0)(-2)) + \\mathbf{k}((-2)(0) - (-5)(-2))\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(20) - \\mathbf{j}(8) + \\mathbf{k}(10)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = (20, -8, 10)\n   \\]\n\n3. **Equation of the plane:**\n\n   The equation of the plane can be written as:\n   \\[\n   20x - 8y + 10z + D = 0\n   \\]\n\n   To find \\(D\\), substitute one of the given points, say \\((2,0,0)\\), into the equation:\n   \\[\n   20(2) - 8(0) + 10(0) + D = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   40 + D = 0 \\implies D = -40\n   \\]\n\n   So, the equation of the plane is:\n   \\[\n   20x - 8y + 10z - 40 = 0\n   \\]\n\n4. **Simplify the equation:**\n\n   We can simplify the equation by dividing all coefficients by their greatest common divisor, which is 2:\n   \\[\n   10x - 4y + 5z - 20 = 0\n   \\]\n\n   The coefficients are \\(A = 10\\), \\(B = -4\\), \\(C = 5\\), \\(D = -20\\). The greatest common divisor of \\(|10|, |4|, |5|, |20|\\) is 1, and \\(A > 0\\).\n\nThus, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{10x - 4y + 5z - 20 = 0}\n\\]"
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the complex number \\( z = re^{i\\theta} \\) and the condition:\n\n\\[\n\\left| 2z + \\frac{1}{z} \\right| = 1.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( z = re^{i\\theta} \\) and its reciprocal \\( \\frac{1}{z} = \\frac{1}{r} e^{-i\\theta} \\) into the condition, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\left| 2re^{i\\theta} + \\frac{1}{r} e^{-i\\theta} \\right| = 1.\n\\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\n\\left| 2r \\cos \\theta + \\frac{1}{r} \\cos \\theta + i \\left( 2r \\sin \\theta - \\frac{1}{r} \\sin \\theta \\right) \\right| = 1.\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this complex number is:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{\\left(2r \\cos \\theta + \\frac{1}{r} \\cos \\theta\\right)^2 + \\left(2r \\sin \\theta - \\frac{1}{r} \\sin \\theta\\right)^2} = 1.\n\\]\n\nSquaring both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\left(2r \\cos \\theta + \\frac{1}{r} \\cos \\theta\\right)^2 + \\left(2r \\sin \\theta - \\frac{1}{r} \\sin \\theta\\right)^2 = 1.\n\\]\n\nExpanding each term, we have:\n\n\\[\n(2r \\cos \\theta + \\frac{1}{r} \\cos \\theta)^2 = 4r^2 \\cos^2 \\theta + 4r \\cos^2 \\theta + \\frac{1}{r^2} \\cos^2 \\theta,\n\\]\n\n\\[\n(2r \\sin \\theta - \\frac{1}{r} \\sin \\theta)^2 = 4r^2 \\sin^2 \\theta - 4r \\sin^2 \\theta + \\frac{1}{r^2} \\sin^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nAdding these, we have:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 (\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta) + 4r (\\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta) + \\frac{1}{r^2} (\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta) = 1.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), this simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + 4r (\\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta) + \\frac{1}{r^2} = 1.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos 2\\theta\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + 4rx + \\frac{1}{r^2} = 1.\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + 4rx + \\frac{1}{r^2} - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic in \\(r\\):\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + 4rx + \\frac{1}{r^2} - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nTo find the minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\), we need to find the range of \\(x = \\cos 2\\theta\\). The expression for \\(x\\) is:\n\n\\[\nx = \\cos 2\\theta = 1 - 2\\sin^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - x}{2}\\).\n\nThe quadratic equation in \\(r\\) must have real solutions, so its discriminant must be non-negative. The discriminant \\(\\Delta\\) of the quadratic equation \\(4r^2 + 4rx + \\frac{1}{r^2} - 1 = 0\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\Delta = (4x)^2 - 16\\left(\\frac{1}{r^2} - 1\\right).\n\\]\n\nFor real \\(r\\), \\(\\Delta \\geq 0\\), which implies:\n\n\\[\n16x^2 \\geq 16\\left(\\frac{1}{r^2} - 1\\right).\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\), we find:\n\n\\[\nx^2 \\geq \\frac{1}{r^2} - 1.\n\\]\n\nThe minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\) occurs when \\(x\\) is maximized. Since \\(x = \\cos 2\\theta\\), the maximum value of \\(x\\) is 1, which occurs when \\(\\theta = 0\\) or \\(\\theta = \\pi\\). Thus, the minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - x}{2} = \\frac{1 - 1}{2} = 0.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
'To compute \\(\\tan 15^\\circ\\), we can use the tangent subtraction formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a - b) = \\frac{\\tan a - \\tan b}{1 + \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nFor \\(a = 45^\\circ\\) and \\(b = 30^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 45^\\circ = 1 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these values into the formula gives:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\tan(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ) = \\frac{\\tan 45^\\circ - \\tan 30^\\circ}{1 + \\tan 45^\\circ \\tan 30^\\circ} = \\frac{1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}{1 + 1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}{1 + \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify further, multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(\\sqrt{3}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3} \\left(1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)}{\\sqrt{3} \\left(1 + \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3} - 1}{\\sqrt{3} + 1}\n\\]\n\nTo rationalize the denominator, multiply the numerator and the denominator by the conjugate of the denominator, \\(\\sqrt{3} - 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{(\\sqrt{3} - 1)^2}{(\\sqrt{3} + 1)(\\sqrt{3} - 1)}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the denominator:\n\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{3} + 1)(\\sqrt{3} - 1) = (\\sqrt{3})^2 - 1^2 = 3 - 1 = 2\n\\]\n\nCalculate the numerator:\n\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{3} - 1)^2 = (\\sqrt{3})^2 - 2 \\cdot \\sqrt{3} \\cdot 1 + 1^2 = 3 - 2\\sqrt{3} + 1 = 4 - 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{4 - 2\\sqrt{3}}{2} = 2 - \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\tan 15^\\circ\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = 2 - \\sqrt{3}\n\\]'
'To compute the product \\(\\cos \\frac{2 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8 \\pi}{7}\\), we can use the properties of the roots of unity and some trigonometric identities.\n\n1. **Roots of Unity**: The seventh roots of unity are the solutions to the equation \\(z^7 = 1\\). These roots are given by \\(z_k = e^{2\\pi i k / 7}\\) for \\(k = 0, 1, 2, \\ldots, 6\\). These roots lie on the unit circle in the complex plane and are evenly spaced.\n\n2. **Relevant Angles**: The angles \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\), \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\), and \\(\\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\) correspond to the arguments of the complex numbers \\(z_1 = e^{2\\pi i / 7}\\), \\(z_2 = e^{4\\pi i / 7}\\), and \\(z_4 = e^{8\\pi i / 7}\\).\n\n3. **Symmetry and Identities**: Notice that \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{e^{i\\theta} + e^{-i\\theta}}{2}\\). Therefore, the cosine of these angles can be expressed in terms of the real parts of the corresponding roots of unity.\n\n4. **Product of Cosines**: The product \\(\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\) can be related to the polynomial whose roots are the real parts of these complex numbers. Specifically, the product of cosines of angles that are roots of unity can be expressed using a known identity:\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{8}.\n   \\]\n\n5. **Verification**: This result can be derived from the fact that the product of the cosines of angles that are roots of unity is related to the cyclotomic polynomial. The seventh cyclotomic polynomial is \\(z^6 + z^5 + z^4 + z^3 + z^2 + z + 1 = 0\\), and the real parts of the roots are related to the cosine values.\n\nTherefore, the product \\(\\cos \\frac{2 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8 \\pi}{7}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{8}}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\theta + \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the angle addition formula for sine, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\theta + \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\sin \\theta \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} + \\cos \\theta \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\), the equation becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} + \\cos \\theta \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} (\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta) = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\):\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\frac{2}{3\\sqrt{2}}.\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the identity:\n\\[\n(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta)^2 = \\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\\[\n(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta)^2 = 1 + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\frac{2}{3\\sqrt{2}}\\) into the equation:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{2}{3\\sqrt{2}}\\right)^2 = 1 + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\(\\left(\\frac{2}{3\\sqrt{2}}\\right)^2\\):\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{2}{3\\sqrt{2}}\\right)^2 = \\frac{4}{18} = \\frac{2}{9}.\n\\]\n\nSo, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{9} = 1 + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\(2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\):\n\\[\n2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = \\frac{2}{9} - 1 = \\frac{2}{9} - \\frac{9}{9} = -\\frac{7}{9}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = -\\frac{7}{9}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{7}{9}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe expression we want to minimize is:\n\n\\[\nE = \\frac{\\sec^4 \\alpha}{\\tan^2 \\beta} + \\frac{\\sec^4 \\beta}{\\tan^2 \\alpha}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, recall the trigonometric identities:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^2 \\theta = 1 + \\tan^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^4 \\theta = (1 + \\tan^2 \\theta)^2.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the expression, we get:\n\n\\[\nE = \\frac{(1 + \\tan^2 \\alpha)^2}{\\tan^2 \\beta} + \\frac{(1 + \\tan^2 \\beta)^2}{\\tan^2 \\alpha}.\n\\]\n\nTo simplify, let \\( x = \\tan^2 \\alpha \\) and \\( y = \\tan^2 \\beta \\). Then the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\nE = \\frac{(1 + x)^2}{y} + \\frac{(1 + y)^2}{x}.\n\\]\n\nWe want to find the minimum value of this expression. By the AM-GM inequality, for non-negative \\( a \\) and \\( b \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a}{b} + \\frac{b}{a} \\geq 2.\n\\]\n\nApplying this to our expression, let \\( a = (1 + x)^2 \\) and \\( b = (1 + y)^2 \\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{(1 + x)^2}{y} + \\frac{(1 + y)^2}{x} \\geq 2 \\sqrt{\\frac{(1 + x)^2}{y} \\cdot \\frac{(1 + y)^2}{x}}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\n2 \\sqrt{\\frac{(1 + x)^2 (1 + y)^2}{xy}} = 2 \\sqrt{\\frac{(1 + x)^2 (1 + y)^2}{xy}}.\n\\]\n\nTo find when equality holds in AM-GM, we need:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{(1 + x)^2}{y} = \\frac{(1 + y)^2}{x}.\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\nx(1 + x)^2 = y(1 + y)^2.\n\\]\n\nThis is a symmetric equation in \\( x \\) and \\( y \\), suggesting that the minimum might occur when \\( x = y \\). If \\( x = y \\), then:\n\n\\[\nx(1 + x)^2 = x(1 + x)^2,\n\\]\n\nwhich is trivially true. Substituting \\( x = y \\) into the expression:\n\n\\[\nE = \\frac{(1 + x)^2}{x} + \\frac{(1 + x)^2}{x} = 2 \\cdot \\frac{(1 + x)^2}{x}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying:\n\n\\[\nE = 2 \\left( \\frac{1 + 2x + x^2}{x} \\right) = 2 \\left( \\frac{1}{x} + 2 + x \\right).\n\\]\n\nTo minimize this, note that the expression \\( \\frac{1}{x} + x \\) is minimized when \\( x = 1 \\) (by AM-GM or calculus), giving:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{x} + x \\geq 2.\n\\]\n\nThus, the minimum value of \\( E \\) is:\n\n\\[\nE = 2(2 + 2) = 8.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the minimum value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{8}\\)."
'To find the volume of the tetrahedron with vertices \\( A = (0,1,2) \\), \\( B = (3,0,1) \\), \\( C = (4,3,6) \\), and \\( D = (2,3,2) \\), we use the determinant formula for the volume of a tetrahedron:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\nx_1 & y_1 & z_1 & 1 \\\\\nx_2 & y_2 & z_2 & 1 \\\\\nx_3 & y_3 & z_3 & 1 \\\\\nx_4 & y_4 & z_4 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of the vertices into the determinant, we have:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n0 & 1 & 2 & 1 \\\\\n3 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\\\\n4 & 3 & 6 & 1 \\\\\n2 & 3 & 2 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nTo compute this determinant, we expand along the last column:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix}\n0 & 1 & 2 & 1 \\\\\n3 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\\\\n4 & 3 & 6 & 1 \\\\\n2 & 3 & 2 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} = \n1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix}\n- 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix}\n+ 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix}\n- 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNotice that the terms cancel out, so we only need to compute the determinant of the 3x3 matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} = 3 \\left( \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 6 \\\\ 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} \\right) - 0 \\left( \\begin{vmatrix} 4 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} \\right) + 1 \\left( \\begin{vmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ 2 & 3 \\end{vmatrix} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculating the 2x2 determinants:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 6 \\\\ 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} = (3)(2) - (6)(3) = 6 - 18 = -12\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} 4 & 3 \\\\ 2 & 3 \\end{vmatrix} = (4)(3) - (3)(2) = 12 - 6 = 6\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back, we get:\n\n\\[\n3(-12) + 1(6) = -36 + 6 = -30\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant of the 4x4 matrix is \\(-30\\), and the volume of the tetrahedron is:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\times |-30| = \\frac{30}{6} = 5\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the tetrahedron is \\(\\boxed{5}\\).'
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe need to compute \\(\\tan \\left( 2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right)\\).\n\n1. **Define \\(\\theta\\):**  \n   Let \\(\\theta = \\arctan \\frac{1}{5}\\). This means \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{1}{5}\\).\n\n2. **Use the double angle formula for tangent:**  \n   The formula is \\(\\tan(2\\theta) = \\frac{2\\tan \\theta}{1 - \\tan^2 \\theta}\\).\n\n3. **Substitute \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{1}{5}\\) into the formula:**  \n   \\[\n   \\tan(2\\theta) = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\frac{1}{5}}{1 - \\left(\\frac{1}{5}\\right)^2} = \\frac{\\frac{2}{5}}{1 - \\frac{1}{25}} = \\frac{\\frac{2}{5}}{\\frac{24}{25}} = \\frac{2}{5} \\cdot \\frac{25}{24} = \\frac{10}{24} = \\frac{5}{12}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Use the tangent subtraction formula:**  \n   The formula is \\(\\tan(a - b) = \\frac{\\tan a - \\tan b}{1 + \\tan a \\tan b}\\).\n\n5. **Set \\(a = 2\\theta\\) and \\(b = \\frac{\\pi}{4}\\):**  \n   We know \\(\\tan \\frac{\\pi}{4} = 1\\).\n\n6. **Substitute into the tangent subtraction formula:**  \n   \\[\n   \\tan(2\\theta - \\frac{\\pi}{4}) = \\frac{\\tan(2\\theta) - \\tan \\frac{\\pi}{4}}{1 + \\tan(2\\theta) \\tan \\frac{\\pi}{4}}\n   \\]\n\n7. **Substitute the known values:**  \n   \\[\n   \\tan(2\\theta - \\frac{\\pi}{4}) = \\frac{\\frac{5}{12} - 1}{1 + \\frac{5}{12} \\cdot 1} = \\frac{\\frac{5}{12} - \\frac{12}{12}}{1 + \\frac{5}{12}} = \\frac{\\frac{-7}{12}}{\\frac{17}{12}} = \\frac{-7}{12} \\cdot \\frac{12}{17} = \\frac{-7}{17}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\tan \\left( 2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right)\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{7}{17}}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}] + \\mathbf{j} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}] + \\mathbf{k} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}] = \\mathbf{0}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, express the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) in terms of its components:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = v_1 \\mathbf{i} + v_2 \\mathbf{j} + v_3 \\mathbf{k}.\n\\]\n\nNow, let's evaluate each term in the equation separately.\n\n### First Term: \\(\\mathbf{i} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}]\\)\n\n1. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j})\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j} = v_1 \\mathbf{i} + (v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{j} + v_3 \\mathbf{k}.\n   \\]\n\n2. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}\\):\n   \\[\n   (v_1 \\mathbf{i} + (v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{j} + v_3 \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{i} = (v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{k} - v_3 \\mathbf{j}.\n   \\]\n\n3. Compute \\(\\mathbf{i} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}]\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{i} \\times ((v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{k} - v_3 \\mathbf{j}) = v_3 \\mathbf{k} + (v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{j}.\n   \\]\n\n### Second Term: \\(\\mathbf{j} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}]\\)\n\n1. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k})\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k} = v_1 \\mathbf{i} + v_2 \\mathbf{j} + (v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{k}.\n   \\]\n\n2. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}\\):\n   \\[\n   (v_1 \\mathbf{i} + v_2 \\mathbf{j} + (v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j} = -(v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{i} + v_1 \\mathbf{k}.\n   \\]\n\n3. Compute \\(\\mathbf{j} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}]\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{j} \\times (-(v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{i} + v_1 \\mathbf{k}) = (v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{k} - v_1 \\mathbf{i}.\n   \\]\n\n### Third Term: \\(\\mathbf{k} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}]\\)\n\n1. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i})\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i} = (v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{i} + v_2 \\mathbf{j} + v_3 \\mathbf{k}.\n   \\]\n\n2. Compute \\((\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}\\):\n   \\[\n   ((v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{i} + v_2 \\mathbf{j} + v_3 \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{k} = -(v_2) \\mathbf{i} + (v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{j}.\n   \\]\n\n3. Compute \\(\\mathbf{k} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}]\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{k} \\times (-(v_2) \\mathbf{i} + (v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{j}) = v_2 \\mathbf{j} - (v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{i}.\n   \\]\n\n### Combine All Terms\n\nNow, add all the terms together:\n\n\\[\n(v_3 \\mathbf{k} + (v_2 - 1) \\mathbf{j}) + ((v_3 - 1) \\mathbf{k} - v_1 \\mathbf{i}) + (v_2 \\mathbf{j} - (v_1 - 1) \\mathbf{i}) = \\mathbf{0}.\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\n- For \\(\\mathbf{i}\\): \\(-v_1 - (v_1 - 1) = -2v_1 + 1\\).\n- For \\(\\mathbf{j}\\): \\((v_2 - 1) + v_2 = 2v_2 - 1\\).\n- For \\(\\mathbf{k}\\): \\(v_3 + (v_3 - 1) = 2v_3 - 1\\).\n\nSet each component to zero:\n\n1. \\(-2v_1 + 1 = 0 \\Rightarrow v_1 = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n2. \\(2v_2 - 1 = 0 \\Rightarrow v_2 = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n3. \\(2v_3 - 1 = 0 \\Rightarrow v_3 = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{2} \\\\ \\frac{1}{2} \\\\ \\frac{1}{2} \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{2} \\\\ \\frac{1}{2} \\\\ \\frac{1}{2} \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the projection of the vector \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the vector \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 1 \\cdot 8 + (-2) \\cdot 1 = 8 - 2 = 6\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 8 \\cdot 8 + 1 \\cdot 1 = 64 + 1 = 65\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{6}{65} \\mathbf{b} = \\frac{6}{65} \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the scalar multiplication, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{6 \\cdot 8}{65} \\\\ \\frac{6 \\cdot 1}{65} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{48}{65} \\\\ \\frac{6}{65} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{48}{65} \\\\ \\frac{6}{65} \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
'To find the dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) in triangle \\(ABC\\) where \\(AB = 3\\), \\(AC = 5\\), and \\(O\\) is the circumcenter of the triangle, we need to use some properties of the circumcenter and the vectors involved.\n\n1. **Understanding the Circumcenter**: The circumcenter \\(O\\) of a triangle is the point equidistant from all three vertices of the triangle. This means that \\(OA = OB = OC = R\\), where \\(R\\) is the circumradius of the triangle.\n\n2. **Vector Representation**: We need to express the vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\) in terms of known quantities. Since \\(O\\) is the circumcenter, \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is simply the vector from \\(O\\) to \\(A\\).\n\n3. **Vector \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\)**: The vector \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\) can be expressed as \\(\\overrightarrow{C} - \\overrightarrow{B}\\).\n\n4. **Dot Product**: The dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC} = \\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot (\\overrightarrow{C} - \\overrightarrow{B}) = \\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{C} - \\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{B}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Using the Circumcenter Property**: Since \\(O\\) is equidistant from \\(B\\) and \\(C\\), the vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{OB}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{OC}\\) are equal in magnitude and perpendicular to \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\). Therefore, the dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) simplifies to zero because \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is perpendicular to \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\) when \\(O\\) is the circumcenter.\n\nThus, the dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) is \\(\\boxed{0}\\).'
"Let's solve the given equation step by step:\n\nThe equation is:\n\\[\n\\cos^3(3x) + \\cos^3(5x) = 8\\cos^3(4x)\\cos^3(x).\n\\]\n\nWe are tasked with finding the sum of all values of \\(x\\) within the range \\(100^\\circ < x < 200^\\circ\\) that satisfy this equation.\n\n### Step 1: Simplify the Equation\n\nFirst, notice that the equation involves trigonometric identities. We can use the identity for the sum of cubes:\n\\[\na^3 + b^3 = (a + b)(a^2 - ab + b^2).\n\\]\n\nApplying this identity to the left side:\n\\[\n\\cos^3(3x) + \\cos^3(5x) = (\\cos(3x) + \\cos(5x))(\\cos^2(3x) - \\cos(3x)\\cos(5x) + \\cos^2(5x)).\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Use Trigonometric Identities\n\nWe can use the sum-to-product identities:\n\\[\n\\cos A + \\cos B = 2 \\cos\\left(\\frac{A + B}{2}\\right) \\cos\\left(\\frac{A - B}{2}\\right).\n\\]\n\nFor \\(A = 3x\\) and \\(B = 5x\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\cos(3x) + \\cos(5x) = 2 \\cos(4x) \\cos(x).\n\\]\n\nSubstitute this into the equation:\n\\[\n2 \\cos(4x) \\cos(x) (\\cos^2(3x) - \\cos(3x)\\cos(5x) + \\cos^2(5x)) = 8 \\cos^3(4x) \\cos^3(x).\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Factor and Simplify\n\nDivide both sides by \\(2 \\cos(4x) \\cos(x)\\) (assuming \\(\\cos(4x) \\neq 0\\) and \\(\\cos(x) \\neq 0\\)):\n\\[\n\\cos^2(3x) - \\cos(3x)\\cos(5x) + \\cos^2(5x) = 4 \\cos^2(4x) \\cos^2(x).\n\\]\n\n### Step 4: Analyze the Equation\n\nThe equation is symmetric in terms of \\(3x\\) and \\(5x\\). This suggests that solutions might be symmetric around certain angles. \n\n### Step 5: Check for Specific Solutions\n\nGiven the symmetry and the range \\(100^\\circ < x < 200^\\circ\\), let's check specific angles that might satisfy the equation. \n\n1. **Check \\(x = 120^\\circ\\):**\n   - \\(\\cos(3 \\times 120^\\circ) = \\cos(360^\\circ) = 1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(5 \\times 120^\\circ) = \\cos(600^\\circ) = 1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(4 \\times 120^\\circ) = \\cos(480^\\circ) = -1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(120^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\n   Substitute into the equation:\n   \\[\n   1^3 + 1^3 = 8(-1)^3\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3.\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   2 = 8 \\times (-1) \\times \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right) = 1.\n   \\]\n   This does not satisfy the equation.\n\n2. **Check \\(x = 180^\\circ\\):**\n   - \\(\\cos(3 \\times 180^\\circ) = \\cos(540^\\circ) = -1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(5 \\times 180^\\circ) = \\cos(900^\\circ) = -1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(4 \\times 180^\\circ) = \\cos(720^\\circ) = 1\\),\n   - \\(\\cos(180^\\circ) = -1\\).\n\n   Substitute into the equation:\n   \\[\n   (-1)^3 + (-1)^3 = 8 \\times 1^3 \\times (-1)^3.\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   -2 = -8.\n   \\]\n   This does not satisfy the equation.\n\n### Step 6: Conclusion\n\nThe equation is quite complex, and without further simplification or numerical methods, it is challenging to find exact solutions analytically. However, based on the symmetry and the range, the sum of the values of \\(x\\) that satisfy the equation is likely to be symmetric around \\(150^\\circ\\).\n\nGiven the complexity and the symmetry, the sum of the values of \\(x\\) is likely to be \\(300^\\circ\\) (considering two symmetric solutions around \\(150^\\circ\\)).\n\nThus, the sum of the values of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{300}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto another vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by the formula:\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that the projection is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).
