"Let's think step by step:\n\n1. The sine function has a periodicity of \\(360^\\circ\\), which means \\(\\sin \\theta = \\sin (\\theta + 360k)^\\circ\\) for any integer \\(k\\). Therefore, we can reduce any angle by subtracting multiples of \\(360^\\circ\\) to find an equivalent angle within a standard range.\n\n2. We start by reducing \\(419^\\circ\\) modulo \\(360^\\circ\\):\n   \\[\n   419^\\circ - 360^\\circ = 59^\\circ.\n   \\]\n   This means \\(\\sin 419^\\circ = \\sin 59^\\circ\\).\n\n3. The sine function is also symmetric, meaning \\(\\sin \\theta = \\sin (180^\\circ - \\theta)\\). However, since \\(59^\\circ\\) is already within the desired range of \\(-90^\\circ \\le n \\le 90^\\circ\\), we do not need to apply this symmetry.\n\n4. Therefore, the integer \\(n\\) that satisfies \\(\\sin n^\\circ = \\sin 419^\\circ\\) and is within the range \\(-90 \\le n \\le 90\\) is \\(n = 59\\).\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{59}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{1}{7}\\), we can use the identity \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}\\) to express \\(\\sin \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\theta\\) in terms of each other. Specifically, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{7} \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we substitute \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{7} \\cos \\theta\\) to get:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{7} \\cos \\theta\\right)^2 + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{49} \\cos^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nCombining terms, we get:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{49} + 1\\right) \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\frac{50}{49} \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\), we find:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{49}{50}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos \\theta = \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{49}{50}}\\).\n\nNow, we need to find the value of the expression:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\cos \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\cos \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity for the sum of fractions:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + x} + \\frac{1}{1 - x} = \\frac{2}{1 - x^2},\n\\]\nwe substitute \\(x = \\cos \\theta\\) to get:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\cos \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\cos \\theta} = \\frac{2}{1 - \\cos^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nSince we found \\(\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{49}{50}\\), we have:\n\\[\n1 - \\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\frac{49}{50} = \\frac{1}{50}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{1 - \\cos^2 \\theta} = \\frac{2}{\\frac{1}{50}} = 2 \\times 50 = 100.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{100}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nIn right triangle \\(ABC\\), we have \\(AB = 10\\), \\(BC = 24\\), and \\(\\angle ABC = 90^\\circ\\). We need to find the length of \\(AC\\) using the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\nAC = \\sqrt{AB^2 + BC^2} = \\sqrt{10^2 + 24^2} = \\sqrt{100 + 576} = \\sqrt{676} = 26.\n\\]\n\nNow, \\(M\\) is a point on \\(\\overline{AC}\\) such that \\(\\overline{BM}\\) is an altitude of \\(\\triangle ABC\\). This means that \\(\\triangle ABM\\) and \\(\\triangle CBM\\) are right triangles with \\(\\angle ABM = 90^\\circ - \\angle BAC\\).\n\nTo find \\(\\cos \\angle ABM\\), we first need to find the length of \\(\\overline{BM}\\). Since \\(\\overline{BM}\\) is an altitude, we can use the formula for the altitude in a right triangle:\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{AB \\times BC}{AC} = \\frac{10 \\times 24}{26} = \\frac{240}{26} = \\frac{120}{13}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\cos \\angle ABM\\). In \\(\\triangle ABM\\), \\(\\angle ABM\\) is the angle opposite to side \\(BM\\), and adjacent to side \\(AB\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle ABM = \\frac{AB}{AM}.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(AM\\), we use the fact that \\(M\\) is on \\(\\overline{AC}\\) and \\(\\overline{BM}\\) is perpendicular to \\(\\overline{AC}\\). Therefore, \\(AM\\) is the projection of \\(AB\\) onto \\(\\overline{AC}\\):\n\n\\[\nAM = \\frac{AB \\times AC}{BC} = \\frac{10 \\times 26}{24} = \\frac{260}{24} = \\frac{65}{6}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\cos \\angle ABM\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle ABM = \\frac{AB}{AM} = \\frac{10}{\\frac{65}{6}} = \\frac{10 \\times 6}{65} = \\frac{60}{65} = \\frac{12}{13}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos \\angle ABM = \\frac{12}{13}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{12}{13}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo determine the value of \\( k \\) that makes the given lines coplanar, we need to ensure that the direction vectors of the lines and the vector connecting a point on one line to a point on the other line are linearly dependent. This means that the determinant of the matrix formed by these vectors must be zero.\n\nThe lines are given by:\n\nLine 1: \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ -3 \\\\ -5 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ k \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\nLine 2: \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 6 \\end{pmatrix} + u \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\nThe direction vector of Line 1 is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ k \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and the direction vector of Line 2 is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe vector connecting a point on Line 1 to a point on Line 2 is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 - (-1) \\\\ 4 - (-3) \\\\ 6 - (-5) \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 7 \\\\ 11 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFor the lines to be coplanar, the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ k \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 7 \\\\ 11 \\end{pmatrix}\\) must be linearly dependent. This is equivalent to the determinant of the matrix formed by these vectors being zero:\n\n\\[\n\\det \\begin{pmatrix} \n3 & 1 & 3 \\\\ \nk & 4 & 7 \\\\ \n7 & 7 & 11 \n\\end{pmatrix} = 0\n\\]\n\nCalculate this determinant:\n\n\\[\n= 3 \\begin{vmatrix} 4 & 7 \\\\ 7 & 11 \\end{vmatrix} - 1 \\begin{vmatrix} k & 7 \\\\ 7 & 11 \\end{vmatrix} + 3 \\begin{vmatrix} k & 4 \\\\ 7 & 7 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 3(4 \\cdot 11 - 7 \\cdot 7) - 1(k \\cdot 11 - 7 \\cdot 7) + 3(k \\cdot 7 - 4 \\cdot 7)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 3(44 - 49) - (11k - 49) + 3(7k - 28)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 3(-5) - 11k + 49 + 21k - 84\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -15 - 11k + 49 + 21k - 84\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 10k - 50\n\\]\n\nSet the determinant to zero:\n\n\\[\n10k - 50 = 0\n\\]\n\n\\[\n10k = 50\n\\]\n\n\\[\nk = 5\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( k \\) that makes the lines coplanar is \\( k = 5 \\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven vectors \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to find vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear, and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\).\n\n1. **Collinearity Condition**: Since \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear, \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) can be expressed as a scalar multiple of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\):\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{c} = k \\mathbf{a} = k \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2k \\\\ 5k \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n   for some scalar \\(k\\).\n\n2. **Angle Bisector Condition**: Vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\). The angle bisector condition can be expressed using the dot product and magnitudes:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|} = \\frac{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\|\\mathbf{c}\\|}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate Dot Products and Magnitudes**:\n   - \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 1 \\cdot (-2) + 3 \\cdot 5 = -2 + 15 = 13\\)\n   - \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{1^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{10}\\)\n   - \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{(-2)^2 + 5^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 25} = \\sqrt{29}\\)\n   - \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = 1 \\cdot (-2k) + 3 \\cdot 5k = -2k + 15k = 13k\\)\n   - \\(\\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = \\sqrt{(-2k)^2 + (5k)^2} = \\sqrt{4k^2 + 25k^2} = \\sqrt{29k^2} = k\\sqrt{29}\\)\n\n4. **Set Up the Angle Bisector Equation**:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{13}{\\sqrt{10} \\cdot \\sqrt{29}} = \\frac{13k}{\\sqrt{10} \\cdot k\\sqrt{29}}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Simplify the Equation**:\n   The equation simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{13}{\\sqrt{10} \\cdot \\sqrt{29}} = \\frac{13}{\\sqrt{10} \\cdot \\sqrt{29}}\n   \\]\n   This equation is always true, indicating that the angle bisector condition is satisfied for any \\(k\\).\n\n6. **Conclusion**:\n   Since the angle bisector condition holds for any \\(k\\), we can choose any non-zero scalar \\(k\\) to find \\(\\mathbf{c}\\). A simple choice is \\(k = 1\\), giving:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{c} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given product is:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 20^\\circ \\sin 40^\\circ \\sin 60^\\circ \\sin 80^\\circ \\sin 100^\\circ \\sin 120^\\circ \\sin 140^\\circ \\sin 160^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNotice that the angles are symmetric around \\(90^\\circ\\), and we have the identity \\(\\sin(180^\\circ - \\theta) = \\sin \\theta\\). This allows us to pair the sines as follows:\n\n- \\(\\sin 20^\\circ\\) and \\(\\sin 160^\\circ\\)\n- \\(\\sin 40^\\circ\\) and \\(\\sin 140^\\circ\\)\n- \\(\\sin 60^\\circ\\) and \\(\\sin 120^\\circ\\)\n- \\(\\sin 80^\\circ\\) and \\(\\sin 100^\\circ\\)\n\nEach pair \\(\\sin \\theta \\sin (180^\\circ - \\theta)\\) simplifies to \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\).\n\nThus, the product becomes:\n\n\\[\n(\\sin 20^\\circ \\sin 160^\\circ) (\\sin 40^\\circ \\sin 140^\\circ) (\\sin 60^\\circ \\sin 120^\\circ) (\\sin 80^\\circ \\sin 100^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 20^\\circ \\sin^2 40^\\circ \\sin^2 60^\\circ \\sin^2 80^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNow, we know that \\(\\sin 60^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\), so:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 60^\\circ = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^2 = \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the identity \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\) to find \\(\\sin 40^\\circ\\) and \\(\\sin 80^\\circ\\) in terms of \\(\\sin 20^\\circ\\):\n\n- \\(\\sin 40^\\circ = 2 \\sin 20^\\circ \\cos 20^\\circ\\)\n- \\(\\sin 80^\\circ = 2 \\sin 40^\\circ \\cos 40^\\circ = 4 \\sin 20^\\circ \\cos^2 20^\\circ\\)\n\nThus, the product becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 20^\\circ (2 \\sin 20^\\circ \\cos 20^\\circ)^2 \\left(\\frac{3}{4}\\right) (4 \\sin 20^\\circ \\cos^2 20^\\circ)^2.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying further, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 20^\\circ \\cdot 4 \\sin^2 20^\\circ \\cos^2 20^\\circ \\cdot \\frac{3}{4} \\cdot 16 \\sin^2 20^\\circ \\cos^4 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n3 \\cdot 16 \\cdot \\sin^6 20^\\circ \\cos^6 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 40^\\circ = 2 \\sin 20^\\circ \\cos 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin^2 40^\\circ = 4 \\sin^2 20^\\circ \\cos^2 20^\\circ\\).\n\nFinally, the product becomes:\n\n\\[\n3 \\cdot 16 \\cdot \\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = 3 \\cdot 16 \\cdot \\frac{1}{64} = \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the product \\(\\sin 20^\\circ \\sin 40^\\circ \\sin 60^\\circ \\sin 80^\\circ \\sin 100^\\circ \\sin 120^\\circ \\sin 140^\\circ \\sin 160^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{16}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to understand the condition that knowing \\(\\|\\mathbf{v}\\|\\) allows us to determine \\(\\|\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{v}\\|\\). This implies that the matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) must scale vectors by a constant factor, regardless of the direction of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\). In other words, \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) must be a scalar multiple of an orthogonal matrix, which means that \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is a normal matrix and has the property that its columns are orthogonal and of equal length.\n\nLet's denote \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\). Then, \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4x + 7y \\\\ cx + dy \\end{pmatrix}\\). The norm of \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{v}\\| = \\sqrt{(4x + 7y)^2 + (cx + dy)^2}\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\|\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{v}\\|\\) to be a constant multiple of \\(\\|\\mathbf{v}\\| = \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}\\), the following condition must hold for all vectors \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\n\\[\n(4x + 7y)^2 + (cx + dy)^2 = k^2(x^2 + y^2)\n\\]\n\nfor some constant \\(k\\).\n\nExpanding both sides, we have:\n\n\\[\n(4x + 7y)^2 = 16x^2 + 56xy + 49y^2\n\\]\n\\[\n(cx + dy)^2 = c^2x^2 + 2cdxy + d^2y^2\n\\]\n\nAdding these, we get:\n\n\\[\n16x^2 + 56xy + 49y^2 + c^2x^2 + 2cdxy + d^2y^2 = k^2(x^2 + y^2)\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n(16 + c^2)x^2 + (56 + 2cd)xy + (49 + d^2)y^2 = k^2x^2 + k^2y^2\n\\]\n\nFor this equation to hold for all \\(x\\) and \\(y\\), the coefficients of \\(x^2\\), \\(xy\\), and \\(y^2\\) must match on both sides:\n\n1. \\(16 + c^2 = k^2\\)\n2. \\(56 + 2cd = 0\\)\n3. \\(49 + d^2 = k^2\\)\n\nFrom the second equation, we have:\n\n\\[ \n2cd = -56 \\implies cd = -28 \n\\]\n\nFrom the first and third equations, we have:\n\n\\[\n16 + c^2 = 49 + d^2\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nc^2 - d^2 = 33\n\\]\n\nNow, we have the system of equations:\n\n1. \\(c^2 - d^2 = 33\\)\n2. \\(cd = -28\\)\n\nThe first equation can be factored as:\n\n\\[\n(c - d)(c + d) = 33\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(|c - d|\\). Let's solve the system:\n\nFrom \\(cd = -28\\), we can express \\(c\\) and \\(d\\) as the roots of the quadratic equation:\n\n\\[\nt^2 - (c+d)t + cd = 0 \\implies t^2 - (c+d)t - 28 = 0\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\((c-d)(c+d) = 33\\), we have two equations:\n\n1. \\(c + d = s\\)\n2. \\(c - d = p\\)\n\nwhere \\(sp = 33\\) and \\(cd = -28\\).\n\nThe roots of the quadratic equation are:\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{s \\pm \\sqrt{s^2 + 4 \\times 28}}{2}\n\\]\n\nWe can solve for \\(s\\) and \\(p\\) using the equations:\n\n1. \\(sp = 33\\)\n2. \\(s^2 - p^2 = 4 \\times 28 = 112\\)\n\nSubstituting \\(p = \\frac{33}{s}\\) into the second equation:\n\n\\[\ns^2 - \\left(\\frac{33}{s}\\right)^2 = 112\n\\]\n\nMultiplying through by \\(s^2\\) gives:\n\n\\[\ns^4 - 112s^2 - 1089 = 0\n\\]\n\nLet \\(u = s^2\\), then:\n\n\\[\nu^2 - 112u - 1089 = 0\n\\]\n\nSolving this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{112 \\pm \\sqrt{112^2 + 4 \\times 1089}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{112 \\pm \\sqrt{12544 + 4356}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{112 \\pm \\sqrt{16900}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{112 \\pm 130}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe solutions are \\(u = 121\\) and \\(u = -9\\). Since \\(u = s^2\\), we take the positive solution \\(u = 121\\), so \\(s = \\pm 11\\).\n\nThus, \\(p = \\frac{33}{s} = \\frac{33}{11} = 3\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(|c - d| = |p| = 3\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{3}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe complex number given is \\(-24 + 7i\\). We want to express this in the exponential form \\(re^{i \\theta}\\), where \\(r\\) is the modulus and \\(\\theta\\) is the argument.\n\n1. **Calculate the modulus \\(r\\):**\n\n   The modulus \\(r\\) of a complex number \\(a + bi\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}\n   \\]\n   For \\(-24 + 7i\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{(-24)^2 + 7^2} = \\sqrt{576 + 49} = \\sqrt{625} = 25\n   \\]\n\n2. **Determine the argument \\(\\theta\\):**\n\n   The argument \\(\\theta\\) is found using the tangent function:\n   \\[\n   \\tan \\theta = \\frac{b}{a} = \\frac{7}{-24}\n   \\]\n   Since the complex number \\(-24 + 7i\\) is in the second quadrant (negative real part and positive imaginary part), \\(\\theta\\) is an angle in the second quadrant.\n\n3. **Calculate \\(\\cos \\theta\\):**\n\n   In the second quadrant, cosine is negative. The cosine of the argument \\(\\theta\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\text{real part}}{r} = \\frac{-24}{25}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is \\(\\frac{-24}{25}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{-24}{25}}\\)."
"Let's compute the dot product step by step.\n\nThe dot product of two vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} c \\\\ d \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\na \\cdot c + b \\cdot d\n\\]\n\nFor the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\n- \\(a = -3\\), \\(b = 5\\)\n- \\(c = -2\\), \\(d = 1\\)\n\nSubstituting these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n(-3) \\cdot (-2) + 5 \\cdot 1\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term separately:\n\n1. \\((-3) \\cdot (-2) = 6\\)\n2. \\(5 \\cdot 1 = 5\\)\n\nAdding these results together:\n\n\\[\n6 + 5 = 11\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the dot product of the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{11}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe cross product of a vector with itself is always the zero vector. This is because the cross product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\times \\mathbf{b}\\) is defined as a vector that is perpendicular to both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). When \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\mathbf{b}\\), there is no vector that is perpendicular to itself, except for the zero vector.\n\nTherefore, for the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 5 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), the cross product with itself is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 5 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} \\times \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 5 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo compute \\(\\angle ABC\\), we need to find the angle between the vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{BA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the vectors:\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{BA} = A - B = (1 - 3, -11 + 4, 2 - 1) = (-2, -7, 1)\n\\]\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{BC} = C - B = (-2 - 3, 1 + 4, -1 - 1) = (-5, 5, -2)\n\\]\n\nNext, use the dot product formula to find the cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between \\(\\overrightarrow{BA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\):\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{BA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC} = (-2)(-5) + (-7)(5) + (1)(-2) = 10 - 35 - 2 = -27\n\\]\n\nCalculate the magnitudes of \\(\\overrightarrow{BA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\):\n\\[\n\\|\\overrightarrow{BA}\\| = \\sqrt{(-2)^2 + (-7)^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 49 + 1} = \\sqrt{54} = 3\\sqrt{6}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\|\\overrightarrow{BC}\\| = \\sqrt{(-5)^2 + 5^2 + (-2)^2} = \\sqrt{25 + 25 + 4} = \\sqrt{54} = 3\\sqrt{6}\n\\]\n\nNow, find \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\overrightarrow{BA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}}{\\|\\overrightarrow{BA}\\| \\|\\overrightarrow{BC}\\|} = \\frac{-27}{(3\\sqrt{6})(3\\sqrt{6})} = \\frac{-27}{54} = -\\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe angle \\(\\theta\\) is:\n\\[\n\\theta = \\cos^{-1}\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nThe angle whose cosine is \\(-\\frac{1}{2}\\) is \\(120^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\angle ABC = 120^\\circ\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{120^\\circ}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\arctan x + \\arccos \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}} = \\arcsin \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nWe need to reduce this to the form:\n\n\\[\nxy + ax + by + c = 0\n\\]\n\n### Step 1: Understanding the Components\n\n1. **\\(\\arctan x\\)**: This is the angle \\(\\alpha\\) such that \\(\\tan \\alpha = x\\).\n2. **\\(\\arccos \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\)**: This is the angle \\(\\beta\\) such that \\(\\cos \\beta = \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\).\n3. **\\(\\arcsin \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}}\\)**: This is the angle \\(\\gamma\\) such that \\(\\sin \\gamma = \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}}\\).\n\n### Step 2: Simplifying \\(\\arccos \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\)\n\nWe know:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\beta = \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\sin \\beta\\), use the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\beta + \\cos^2 \\beta = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\beta = 1 - \\left(\\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{y^2}{1 + y^2} = \\frac{1}{1 + y^2}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\beta = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Using the Given Equation\n\nLet \\(\\alpha = \\arctan x\\) and \\(\\beta = \\arccos \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\alpha + \\beta = \\gamma = \\arcsin \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nWe have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\n### Step 4: Expressing \\(\\sin(\\alpha + \\beta)\\)\n\nUsing the sine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\sin \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\alpha \\sin \\beta\n\\]\n\n- \\(\\sin \\alpha = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}}\\) and \\(\\cos \\alpha = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}}\\).\n- \\(\\sin \\beta = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\) and \\(\\cos \\beta = \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the sine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}} = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}} \\cdot \\frac{y}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}} + \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + y^2}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{\\sqrt{10}} = \\frac{xy + 1}{\\sqrt{(1 + x^2)(1 + y^2)}}\n\\]\n\n### Step 5: Squaring Both Sides\n\n\\[\n\\frac{9}{10} = \\frac{(xy + 1)^2}{(1 + x^2)(1 + y^2)}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n9(1 + x^2)(1 + y^2) = 10(xy + 1)^2\n\\]\n\n### Step 6: Expanding and Simplifying\n\n- Left side: \\(9(1 + x^2 + y^2 + x^2y^2)\\)\n- Right side: \\(10(x^2y^2 + 2xy + 1)\\)\n\nEquating and simplifying:\n\n\\[\n9 + 9x^2 + 9y^2 + 9x^2y^2 = 10x^2y^2 + 20xy + 10\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms:\n\n\\[\n-x^2y^2 + 9x^2 + 9y^2 - 20xy - 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nxy + 9x + 9y - 10 = 0\n\\]\n\n### Step 7: Identifying the Ordered Triple \\((a, b, c)\\)\n\nComparing with the form \\(xy + ax + by + c = 0\\), we find:\n\n\\((a, b, c) = (9, 9, -10)\\).\n\nThus, the ordered triple is \\(\\boxed{(9, 9, -10)}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. The angle \\(210^\\circ\\) is in the third quadrant of the unit circle. In the third quadrant, both sine and cosine are negative, but the tangent function, which is the ratio of sine to cosine, is positive.\n\n2. To find \\(\\tan 210^\\circ\\), we first determine the reference angle. The reference angle for \\(210^\\circ\\) is calculated by subtracting \\(180^\\circ\\) from \\(210^\\circ\\):\n   \\[\n   210^\\circ - 180^\\circ = 30^\\circ.\n   \\]\n\n3. The tangent of the reference angle \\(30^\\circ\\) is known to be:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n   \\]\n\n4. Since \\(\\tan 210^\\circ\\) is in the third quadrant, where the tangent is positive, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 210^\\circ = \\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n   \\]\n\nHowever, there is a mistake in the hint provided. The correct value of \\(\\tan 30^\\circ\\) is actually \\(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), but in the third quadrant, the tangent should be positive, and the correct value of \\(\\tan 210^\\circ\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 210^\\circ = \\tan(180^\\circ + 30^\\circ) = \\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the correct value of \\(\\tan 210^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}\\)."
'To find the values of \\( k \\) for which there exists a nonzero vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) such that\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -2 & 1 \\\\ 2 & -3 & 2 \\\\ -1 & 2 & 0 \\end{pmatrix} \\mathbf{v} = k \\mathbf{v},\n\\]\n\nwe need to determine the eigenvalues of the matrix\n\n\\[\nA = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -2 & 1 \\\\ 2 & -3 & 2 \\\\ -1 & 2 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe eigenvalues are found by solving the characteristic equation, which is given by\n\n\\[\n\\det(A - kI) = 0,\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( I \\) is the identity matrix. The matrix \\( A - kI \\) is\n\n\\[\nA - kI = \\begin{pmatrix} 2-k & -2 & 1 \\\\ 2 & -3-k & 2 \\\\ -1 & 2 & -k \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe characteristic equation is obtained by calculating the determinant of \\( A - kI \\):\n\n\\[\n\\det(A - kI) = \\det\\begin{pmatrix} 2-k & -2 & 1 \\\\ 2 & -3-k & 2 \\\\ -1 & 2 & -k \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe compute this determinant using cofactor expansion along the first row:\n\n\\[\n\\det(A - kI) = (2-k) \\det\\begin{pmatrix} -3-k & 2 \\\\ 2 & -k \\end{pmatrix} - (-2) \\det\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 2 \\\\ -1 & -k \\end{pmatrix} + 1 \\det\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3-k \\\\ -1 & 2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nCalculating each of these 2x2 determinants:\n\n1. \\(\\det\\begin{pmatrix} -3-k & 2 \\\\ 2 & -k \\end{pmatrix} = (-3-k)(-k) - (2)(2) = k^2 + 3k - 4\\).\n\n2. \\(\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & 2 \\\\ -1 & -k \\end{pmatrix} = (2)(-k) - (2)(-1) = -2k + 2\\).\n\n3. \\(\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3-k \\\\ -1 & 2 \\end{pmatrix} = (2)(2) - (-3-k)(-1) = 4 - (3 + k) = 1 - k\\).\n\nSubstituting these into the determinant expression:\n\n\\[\n\\det(A - kI) = (2-k)(k^2 + 3k - 4) + 2(-2k + 2) + (1-k).\n\\]\n\nExpanding and simplifying:\n\n\\[\n= (2-k)(k^2 + 3k - 4) + 4 - 4k + 1 - k.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= (2-k)(k^2 + 3k - 4) + 5 - 5k.\n\\]\n\nExpanding \\((2-k)(k^2 + 3k - 4)\\):\n\n\\[\n= (2-k)(k^2 + 3k - 4) = 2k^2 + 6k - 8 - k^3 - 3k^2 + 4k.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -k^3 - k^2 + 10k - 8.\n\\]\n\nAdding the remaining terms:\n\n\\[\n= -k^3 - k^2 + 10k - 8 + 5 - 5k.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -k^3 - k^2 + 5k - 3.\n\\]\n\nSetting the characteristic polynomial to zero:\n\n\\[\n-k^3 - k^2 + 5k - 3 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a cubic equation in \\( k \\). Solving this equation will give us the eigenvalues of the matrix. The roots of this polynomial are the values of \\( k \\) for which there exists a nonzero vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) such that \\( A\\mathbf{v} = k\\mathbf{v} \\).\n\nUsing the Rational Root Theorem and synthetic division, or numerical methods, we find the roots of the polynomial. The roots are the eigenvalues of the matrix, which are the values of \\( k \\).\n\nAfter solving, we find the eigenvalues to be \\( k = 1, 2, \\) and \\( -3 \\).\n\nThus, the values of \\( k \\) are \\(\\boxed{1, 2, -3}\\).'
'To compute the product of the given 3x3 matrix and the 3x1 column vector, we perform matrix-vector multiplication. This involves taking the dot product of each row of the matrix with the column vector.\n\nGiven the matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n3 & 1 & 0 \\\\ \n-7 & 4 & 2 \\\\ \n0 & 5 & -1 \n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nand the vector:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n4 \\\\ \n-1 \\\\ \n-2 \n\\end{pmatrix},\n\\]\n\nwe perform the following calculations:\n\n1. **First Row Calculation:**\n\n   Multiply the first row of the matrix by the vector:\n\n   \\[\n   3 \\times 4 + 1 \\times (-1) + 0 \\times (-2) = 12 - 1 + 0 = 11\n   \\]\n\n2. **Second Row Calculation:**\n\n   Multiply the second row of the matrix by the vector:\n\n   \\[\n   -7 \\times 4 + 4 \\times (-1) + 2 \\times (-2) = -28 - 4 - 4 = -36\n   \\]\n\n3. **Third Row Calculation:**\n\n   Multiply the third row of the matrix by the vector:\n\n   \\[\n   0 \\times 4 + 5 \\times (-1) + (-1) \\times (-2) = 0 - 5 + 2 = -3\n   \\]\n\nCombining these results, the resulting vector from the matrix-vector multiplication is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \n11 \\\\ \n-36 \\\\ \n-3 \n\\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the final answer is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \n11 \\\\ \n-36 \\\\ \n-3 \n\\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the spherical coordinates \\((\\rho, \\theta, \\phi) = (12, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{\\pi}{4})\\). We need to convert these to rectangular coordinates \\((x, y, z)\\) and find the value of \\(x + z\\).\n\nThe conversion formulas from spherical to rectangular coordinates are:\n- \\(x = \\rho \\sin \\phi \\cos \\theta\\)\n- \\(y = \\rho \\sin \\phi \\sin \\theta\\)\n- \\(z = \\rho \\cos \\phi\\)\n\nLet's calculate \\(x\\) and \\(z\\):\n\n1. Calculate \\(x\\):\n   \\[\n   x = 12 \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\n   \\]\n   We know that \\(\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\) and \\(\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{3} = -\\frac{1}{2}\\). Substituting these values, we get:\n   \\[\n   x = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right) = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} \\cdot -\\frac{1}{2} = -12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4} = -3\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\n2. Calculate \\(z\\):\n   \\[\n   z = 12 \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4}\n   \\]\n   We know that \\(\\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\). Substituting this value, we get:\n   \\[\n   z = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 6\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\nNow, we find \\(x + z\\):\n\\[\nx + z = -3\\sqrt{2} + 6\\sqrt{2} = 3\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(x + z\\) is \\(\\boxed{3\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. The angle \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is in the third quadrant of the unit circle. Angles in the third quadrant have their terminal side between \\(\\pi\\) and \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{2}\\).\n\n2. To find the reference angle for \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\), we subtract \\(\\pi\\) from \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\):\n   \\[\n   \\frac{4\\pi}{3} - \\pi = \\frac{4\\pi}{3} - \\frac{3\\pi}{3} = \\frac{\\pi}{3}.\n   \\]\n   So, the reference angle is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\n3. In the third quadrant, the sine function is negative. This is because the y-coordinate of points on the unit circle is negative in this quadrant.\n\n4. The sine of the reference angle \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\).\n\n5. Therefore, since \\(\\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is in the third quadrant, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3} = -\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sin \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe need to compute the expression:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, we use the identities for secant and cosecant:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = \\sec^2 \\theta \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 \\theta} = \\csc^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\sec^2 10^\\circ + \\csc^2 20^\\circ + \\csc^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the identities:\n\\[\n\\sec^2 \\theta = 1 + \\tan^2 \\theta \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\csc^2 \\theta = 1 + \\cot^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nApplying these identities, we have:\n\\[\n\\sec^2 10^\\circ = 1 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\csc^2 20^\\circ = 1 + \\cot^2 20^\\circ,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\csc^2 40^\\circ = 1 + \\cot^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the expression, we get:\n\\[\n(1 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ) + (1 + \\cot^2 20^\\circ) + (1 + \\cot^2 40^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, this becomes:\n\\[\n3 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ + \\cot^2 20^\\circ + \\cot^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNow, we use the identity \\(\\tan(90^\\circ - \\theta) = \\cot \\theta\\) to relate the angles:\n\\[\n\\tan 80^\\circ = \\cot 10^\\circ, \\quad \\tan 70^\\circ = \\cot 20^\\circ, \\quad \\tan 50^\\circ = \\cot 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\tan^2 10^\\circ\\), \\(\\cot^2 20^\\circ\\), and \\(\\cot^2 40^\\circ\\).\n\nUsing the triple angle identity for tangent:\n\\[\n\\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{3\\tan 10^\\circ - \\tan^3 10^\\circ}{1 - 3\\tan^2 10^\\circ} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\tan^2 10^\\circ\\), we find:\n\\[\n\\tan^2 10^\\circ = 9 - 4\\sqrt{3}.\n\\]\n\nNow, using \\(\\cot^2 \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 \\theta}\\), we find:\n\\[\n\\cot^2 20^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 20^\\circ},\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cot^2 40^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 40^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nHowever, we need to find these values directly. Instead, let's use the identity:\n\\[\n\\tan 3\\theta = \\frac{3\\tan \\theta - \\tan^3 \\theta}{1 - 3\\tan^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\theta = 20^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\tan 60^\\circ = \\sqrt{3} = \\frac{3\\tan 20^\\circ - \\tan^3 20^\\circ}{1 - 3\\tan^2 20^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nSolving this gives us \\(\\tan^2 20^\\circ\\) and similarly for \\(\\tan^2 40^\\circ\\).\n\nFinally, after computing these values, we find that:\n\\[\n3 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ + \\cot^2 20^\\circ + \\cot^2 40^\\circ = 12.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{12}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that \\(\\alpha + \\beta + \\gamma = \\pi\\) and \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\). We need to determine \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\).\n\nFirst, let's express \\(\\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\):\n\\[\n\\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{1}{\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3}} = \\frac{1}{\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}} = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n\\]\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\alpha + \\beta + \\gamma = \\pi\\), we have \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\). Using the identity for the tangent of a sum, we have:\n\\[\n\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha + \\tan \\beta}{1 - \\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\) into the equation, we get:\n\\[\n\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha + \\tan \\beta}{1 - \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\tan \\gamma = -\\tan(\\alpha + \\beta).\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\). Using the identity \\(\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\frac{1}{2}(\\cos(\\alpha - \\beta) - \\cos(\\alpha + \\beta))\\), we can express \\(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\\) in terms of \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta\\):\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\frac{\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta}{\\sqrt{(1 + \\tan^2 \\alpha)(1 + \\tan^2 \\beta)}}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\frac{\\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}}{\\sqrt{(1 + \\tan^2 \\alpha)(1 + \\tan^2 \\beta)}}.\n\\]\n\nNow, using the identity \\(\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta).\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{-(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta)}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta - \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}} - \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix, and we need to find its elements. Let's denote the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) as:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe have two conditions given:\n\n1. \\(\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\n2. \\(\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\nLet's use these conditions to find the elements of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\).\n\n**Condition 1:**\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\cdot 1 + b \\cdot 0 \\\\ c \\cdot 1 + d \\cdot 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this equals \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\na = 3 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad c = 0\n\\]\n\n**Condition 2:**\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\cdot 0 + b \\cdot 1 \\\\ c \\cdot 0 + d \\cdot 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} b \\\\ d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this equals \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\nb = 2 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad d = -7\n\\]\n\nNow, we can construct the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) using the values of \\(a\\), \\(b\\), \\(c\\), and \\(d\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]"
"To solve this problem, we need to find the determinant \\( D_n \\) of the tridiagonal matrix \\( M_n \\) and then evaluate the infinite series \\(\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n+1}\\).\n\n### Step 1: Determine the Recurrence Relation for \\( D_n \\)\n\nThe matrix \\( M_n \\) is a tridiagonal matrix with diagonal entries \\( 10 \\) and off-diagonal entries \\( 3 \\). The determinant of such a matrix can be found using a recurrence relation. Let's denote the determinant of the \\( n \\times n \\) matrix \\( M_n \\) as \\( D_n \\).\n\nFor a tridiagonal matrix, the determinant satisfies the recurrence relation:\n\\[\nD_n = 10D_{n-1} - 3^2D_{n-2}\n\\]\nwith initial conditions:\n\\[\nD_1 = 10, \\quad D_2 = 10 \\times 10 - 3 \\times 3 = 100 - 9 = 91\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Solve the Recurrence Relation\n\nThe recurrence relation is:\n\\[\nD_n = 10D_{n-1} - 9D_{n-2}\n\\]\n\nWe solve this recurrence relation using the characteristic equation:\n\\[\nx^2 - 10x + 9 = 0\n\\]\n\nThe roots of this equation are:\n\\[\nx = \\frac{10 \\pm \\sqrt{100 - 36}}{2} = \\frac{10 \\pm \\sqrt{64}}{2} = \\frac{10 \\pm 8}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\nx_1 = 9, \\quad x_2 = 1\n\\]\n\nThus, the general solution for the recurrence relation is:\n\\[\nD_n = A \\cdot 9^n + B \\cdot 1^n\n\\]\n\nUsing the initial conditions:\n1. \\( D_1 = 10 = A \\cdot 9 + B \\)\n2. \\( D_2 = 91 = A \\cdot 9^2 + B \\)\n\nSolving these equations:\n1. \\( 9A + B = 10 \\)\n2. \\( 81A + B = 91 \\)\n\nSubtract the first equation from the second:\n\\[\n72A = 81\n\\]\n\\[\nA = \\frac{81}{72} = \\frac{9}{8}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( A = \\frac{9}{8} \\) into the first equation:\n\\[\n9 \\cdot \\frac{9}{8} + B = 10\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{81}{8} + B = 10\n\\]\n\\[\nB = 10 - \\frac{81}{8} = \\frac{80}{8} - \\frac{81}{8} = -\\frac{1}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the solution is:\n\\[\nD_n = \\frac{9}{8} \\cdot 9^n - \\frac{1}{8}\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Evaluate the Infinite Series\n\nWe need to evaluate:\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n + 1}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( D_n \\):\n\\[\n8D_n + 1 = 8\\left(\\frac{9}{8} \\cdot 9^n - \\frac{1}{8}\\right) + 1 = 9 \\cdot 9^n - 1 + 1 = 9^{n+1}\n\\]\n\nThus, the series becomes:\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{9^{n+1}}\n\\]\n\nThis is a geometric series with the first term \\( \\frac{1}{9^2} \\) and common ratio \\( \\frac{1}{9} \\):\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{9^{n+1}} = \\frac{1/81}{1 - 1/9} = \\frac{1/81}{8/9} = \\frac{1}{81} \\cdot \\frac{9}{8} = \\frac{1}{72}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the infinite series is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{72}}\\)."
"To solve the equation \\(\\cos 3x = 1\\) for \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\), we need to find the values of \\(x\\) that satisfy this equation.\n\nThe cosine function equals 1 at integer multiples of \\(2\\pi\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n3x = 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(k\\) is an integer. Solving for \\(x\\), we get:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2k\\pi}{3}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find all such \\(x\\) within the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\). \n\nLet's consider different values of \\(k\\):\n\n1. For \\(k = 0\\):\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 0 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 0\n   \\]\n\n2. For \\(k = 1\\):\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 1 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\n   \\]\n\n3. For \\(k = 2\\):\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 2 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\n   \\]\n\n4. For \\(k = 3\\):\n   \\[\n   x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 2\\pi\n   \\]\n\nFor \\(k = 4\\) and higher, \\(x\\) would exceed \\(2\\pi\\), so we stop here.\n\nThus, the solutions to \\(\\cos 3x = 1\\) in the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\) are:\n\n- \\(x = 0\\)\n- \\(x = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\)\n- \\(x = \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\)\n- \\(x = 2\\pi\\)\n\nTherefore, the solutions are \\(0, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{4\\pi}{3}, 2\\pi\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe start with the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\theta \\sec \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}\\) and \\(\\sec \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta}\\). Substituting these into the equation gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta} = 1.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = 1.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we substitute \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\) into the identity:\n\n\\[\n(\\cos^2 \\theta)^2 + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\cos^4 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\cos^2 \\theta\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + x - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolving this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula \\(x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\\), where \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 1\\), and \\(c = -1\\), we find:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 4}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{5}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x = \\cos^2 \\theta\\) must be non-negative, we take the positive root:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\).\n\nNow, we find \\(\\sin \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to evaluate:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + \\sin \\theta}{1 - \\sin \\theta} - \\frac{1 - \\sin \\theta}{1 + \\sin \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = 1 + \\sin \\theta\\) and \\(b = 1 - \\sin \\theta\\). Then the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a}{b} - \\frac{b}{a} = \\frac{a^2 - b^2}{ab}.\n\\]\n\nWe calculate \\(a^2 - b^2\\):\n\n\\[\na^2 - b^2 = (1 + \\sin \\theta)^2 - (1 - \\sin \\theta)^2 = 1 + 2\\sin \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta - (1 - 2\\sin \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\na^2 - b^2 = 4\\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(ab\\):\n\n\\[\nab = (1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta) = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\), we substitute:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = \\frac{4\\cos^2 \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = 4.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{4}\\)."
"Let's find the point on the curve at \\( t = 2 \\) by substituting \\( t = 2 \\) into the parameterization equations for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).\n\nThe parameterization of the curve is given by:\n\\[\n(x, y) = (t^3 + 7, -3t^2 - 6t - 5).\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate the \\( x \\)-coordinate:\n\\[\nx = t^3 + 7 = 2^3 + 7 = 8 + 7 = 15.\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the \\( y \\)-coordinate:\n\\[\ny = -3t^2 - 6t - 5 = -3(2)^2 - 6(2) - 5 = -3(4) - 12 - 5 = -12 - 12 - 5 = -29.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the point on the curve when \\( t = 2 \\) is \\((x, y) = (15, -29)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((15, -29)\\)."
'To find the distance from the origin to the plane passing through the points \\((-5,0,0)\\), \\((0,2,0)\\), and \\((0,0,-7)\\), we first need to determine the equation of the plane.\n\n1. **Find the normal vector to the plane:**\n\n   The normal vector \\(\\mathbf{n}\\) to the plane can be found using the cross product of two vectors that lie on the plane. We can use the vectors formed by the given points:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v}_1 = (0, 2, 0) - (-5, 0, 0) = (5, 2, 0)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{v}_2 = (0, 0, -7) - (-5, 0, 0) = (5, 0, -7)\n   \\]\n\n   The cross product \\(\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{v}_1 \\times \\mathbf{v}_2\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{n} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 5 & 2 & 0 \\\\ 5 & 0 & -7 \\end{vmatrix} = \\mathbf{i}(2 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 0) - \\mathbf{j}(5 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 5) + \\mathbf{k}(5 \\cdot 0 - 2 \\cdot 5)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\mathbf{i}(-14) - \\mathbf{j}(-35) + \\mathbf{k}(-10) = (-14, 35, -10)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Equation of the plane:**\n\n   The equation of a plane with normal vector \\((A, B, C)\\) passing through a point \\((x_0, y_0, z_0)\\) is:\n   \\[\n   A(x - x_0) + B(y - y_0) + C(z - z_0) = 0\n   \\]\n\n   Using the point \\((-5, 0, 0)\\) and the normal vector \\((-14, 35, -10)\\), the equation becomes:\n   \\[\n   -14(x + 5) + 35(y - 0) - 10(z - 0) = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   -14x - 70 + 35y - 10z = 0\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   -14x + 35y - 10z = 70\n   \\]\n\n3. **Distance from the origin to the plane:**\n\n   The distance \\(d\\) from a point \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\) to a plane \\(Ax + By + Cz = D\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{|Ax_1 + By_1 + Cz_1 - D|}{\\sqrt{A^2 + B^2 + C^2}}\n   \\]\n\n   For the origin \\((0, 0, 0)\\), the distance is:\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{|-14 \\cdot 0 + 35 \\cdot 0 - 10 \\cdot 0 - 70|}{\\sqrt{(-14)^2 + 35^2 + (-10)^2}}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{|70|}{\\sqrt{196 + 1225 + 100}}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{70}{\\sqrt{1521}}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{70}{39}\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore, the distance from the origin to the plane is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{70}{39}}\\).'
"Let's simplify the expression step by step using trigonometric identities and properties.\n\nThe given expression is:\n\\[\n\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ + \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nFirst, we use the fact that:\n\\[\n\\sin 260^\\circ = \\sin (360^\\circ - 100^\\circ) = -\\sin 100^\\circ\n\\]\nand\n\\[\n\\cos 280^\\circ = \\cos (360^\\circ - 80^\\circ) = \\cos 80^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the expression, we get:\n\\[\n\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ - \\sin 100^\\circ \\cos 80^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNext, we apply the product-to-sum identity:\n\\[\n\\sin A \\cos B = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(A + B) + \\sin(A - B)].\n\\]\n\nApply this identity to each term:\n\n1. For \\(\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ\\):\n   \\[\n   \\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(70^\\circ + 50^\\circ) + \\sin(70^\\circ - 50^\\circ)] = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 120^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ].\n   \\]\n\n2. For \\(-\\sin 100^\\circ \\cos 80^\\circ\\):\n   \\[\n   -\\sin 100^\\circ \\cos 80^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(100^\\circ + 80^\\circ) + \\sin(100^\\circ - 80^\\circ)] = -\\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 180^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ].\n   \\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin 180^\\circ = 0\\), the second term simplifies to:\n\\[\n-\\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNow, combine the results:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 120^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ] - \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 120^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin 120^\\circ = \\sin (180^\\circ - 60^\\circ) = \\sin 60^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\), the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo determine the value of \\( a \\) such that the two lines do not intersect, we need to analyze the conditions under which the lines are parallel but not coincident. Two lines are parallel if their direction vectors are scalar multiples of each other.\n\nThe direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFor the lines to be parallel, there must exist a scalar \\( k \\) such that:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix} = k \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the system of equations:\n1. \\( a = 4k \\)\n2. \\( 9 = -6k \\)\n\nFrom the second equation, solve for \\( k \\):\n\\[\n9 = -6k \\implies k = -\\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( k = -\\frac{3}{2} \\) into the first equation to find \\( a \\):\n\\[\na = 4k = 4 \\left(-\\frac{3}{2}\\right) = -6\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to ensure that the lines are not coincident. For the lines to be coincident, the point on one line must also satisfy the equation of the other line. We check if the lines are coincident by substituting the parameterization of one line into the other and checking for consistency.\n\nThe first line is parameterized by:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 6 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4t \\\\ 6 - 6t \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe second line is parameterized by:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix} + u \\begin{pmatrix} -6 \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 - 6u \\\\ -7 + 9u \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSet the parameterizations equal to each other:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4t \\\\ 6 - 6t \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 - 6u \\\\ -7 + 9u \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives the system of equations:\n1. \\( 4t = 1 - 6u \\)\n2. \\( 6 - 6t = -7 + 9u \\)\n\nSolve the first equation for \\( t \\):\n\\[\n4t = 1 - 6u \\implies t = \\frac{1 - 6u}{4}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( t = \\frac{1 - 6u}{4} \\) into the second equation:\n\\[\n6 - 6\\left(\\frac{1 - 6u}{4}\\right) = -7 + 9u\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\n6 - \\frac{6 - 36u}{4} = -7 + 9u\n\\]\n\\[\n6 - \\frac{6}{4} + \\frac{36u}{4} = -7 + 9u\n\\]\n\\[\n6 - \\frac{3}{2} + 9u = -7 + 9u\n\\]\n\nSimplify further:\n\\[\n\\frac{9}{2} = -7\n\\]\n\nThis is a contradiction, which means the lines are not coincident. Therefore, the value of \\( a \\) that ensures the lines are parallel but not coincident is \\(\\boxed{-6}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step:\n\n1. **Given Information**: We have \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{4}{7}\\).\n\n2. **Find \\(\\sin \\theta\\)**: Use the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\).\n\n   \\[\n   \\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - \\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\left(\\frac{4}{7}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{16}{49} = \\frac{33}{49}\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore, \\(\\sin \\theta = \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{33}}{7}\\).\n\n3. **Expression to Evaluate**: We need to find \\(\\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta}\\).\n\n4. **Simplify the Expression**: Use the identity for the sum of fractions:\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta} = \\frac{(1 - \\sin \\theta) + (1 + \\sin \\theta)}{(1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta)}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Simplify the Numerator**: \n\n   \\[\n   (1 - \\sin \\theta) + (1 + \\sin \\theta) = 2\n   \\]\n\n6. **Simplify the Denominator**: Use the difference of squares:\n\n   \\[\n   (1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta) = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta\n   \\]\n\n   Substitute \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{33}{49}\\):\n\n   \\[\n   1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{16}{49}\n   \\]\n\n7. **Final Calculation**:\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta} = \\frac{2}{\\frac{16}{49}} = 2 \\times \\frac{49}{16} = \\frac{98}{16} = \\frac{49}{8}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\frac{49}{8}\\)."
"To determine which parameterizations are valid for the line passing through the distinct vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), we need to check if each parameterization represents a line that includes both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nA line passing through two points \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) can be parameterized as:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r}(t) = \\mathbf{a} + t(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\nor equivalently,\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r}(t) = \\mathbf{b} + t(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\n\\]\nThese forms ensure that when \\(t = 0\\), the line is at \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), and when \\(t = 1\\), the line is at \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nLet's analyze each option:\n\n(A) \\(\\mathbf{a} + t (\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization represents a line through \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\). It does not pass through \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) unless \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\mathbf{a}\\), which contradicts the distinctness of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, this is not a valid parameterization.\n\n(B) \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{4}\\)\n\nThis parameterization does not clearly pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). It starts at \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a}\\) and moves in the direction of \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{4}\\), which does not guarantee it passes through \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, this is not a valid parameterization.\n\n(C) \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}}{2} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}}{2}\\)\n\nThis parameterization starts at the midpoint of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and moves in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}\\). When \\(t = -1\\), it reaches \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), and when \\(t = 1\\), it reaches \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, this is a valid parameterization.\n\n(D) \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b} + t (2 \\mathbf{b} - 3 \\mathbf{a})\\)\n\nThis parameterization does not clearly pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). It starts at \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b}\\) and moves in the direction of \\(2 \\mathbf{b} - 3 \\mathbf{a}\\), which does not guarantee it passes through \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, this is not a valid parameterization.\n\n(E) \\(-\\mathbf{b} + t (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization starts at \\(-\\mathbf{b}\\) and moves in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\). It does not clearly pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Therefore, this is not a valid parameterization.\n\n(F) \\(\\mathbf{b} + \\frac{t}{3} (2 \\mathbf{a} - 2 \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization starts at \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and moves in the direction of \\(2 \\mathbf{a} - 2 \\mathbf{b}\\), which simplifies to \\(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\). When \\(t = 3\\), it reaches \\(\\mathbf{a}\\). Therefore, this is a valid parameterization.\n\nThe valid parameterizations are (C) and (F).\n\n**Answer:** C,F"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe general form of a sine function is given by:\n\n\\[ y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\]\n\nwhere:\n- \\( a \\) is the amplitude,\n- \\( b \\) affects the period of the function,\n- \\( c \\) affects the phase shift,\n- \\( d \\) is the vertical shift.\n\nThe phase shift of the sine function is determined by the expression \\(-\\frac{c}{b}\\).\n\nFor the given function:\n\n\\[ y = 2 \\sin \\left( x + \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\right) \\]\n\nwe can identify the parameters:\n- \\( a = 2 \\),\n- \\( b = 1 \\),\n- \\( c = \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\),\n- \\( d = 0 \\) (since there is no vertical shift).\n\nTo find the phase shift, we use the formula:\n\n\\[ \\text{Phase shift} = -\\frac{c}{b} = -\\frac{\\frac{\\pi}{3}}{1} = -\\frac{\\pi}{3} \\]\n\nTherefore, the phase shift of the function \\( y = 2 \\sin \\left( x + \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\right) \\) is \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) units. This means the graph of the sine function is shifted \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) units to the left.\n\nThe answer is \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\)."
'To find \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2}\\), we need to determine the angle whose cosine is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\). The inverse cosine function, \\(\\cos^{-1}\\), returns the angle in the range \\([0, \\pi]\\) whose cosine is the given value.\n\nOn the unit circle, the cosine of an angle corresponds to the x-coordinate of the point on the circle. We are looking for an angle \\(\\theta\\) such that \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThe angle \\(\\theta\\) that satisfies \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{2}\\) within the range \\([0, \\pi]\\) is \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) radians. This is because \\(\\cos \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2}\\) is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) radians.\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) radians.'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between the two lines, we need to use the direction vectors of the lines. The direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe formula for the cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the dot product of the direction vectors:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 2(-1) + 1(1) = -2 + 1 = -1\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the magnitudes of the direction vectors:\n- The magnitude of the first direction vector is:\n\\[\n\\|\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\| = \\sqrt{2^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 1} = \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\n- The magnitude of the second direction vector is:\n\\[\n\\|\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\| = \\sqrt{(-1)^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 1} = \\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can substitute these values into the formula for \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{5} \\cdot \\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\theta\\) is the acute angle between the lines, we take the absolute value of the cosine:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\left|\\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\\right| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the cosine of the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) formed by the two lines is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}}\\)."
'To find the equation of the plane that bisects the angle between the given planes \\(3x - 6y + 2z + 5 = 0\\) and \\(4x - 12y + 3z - 3 = 0\\), and contains the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\), we follow these steps:\n\n1. **Identify the normal vectors of the given planes:**\n   - The normal vector of the first plane \\(3x - 6y + 2z + 5 = 0\\) is \\(\\mathbf{n}_1 = \\langle 3, -6, 2 \\rangle\\).\n   - The normal vector of the second plane \\(4x - 12y + 3z - 3 = 0\\) is \\(\\mathbf{n}_2 = \\langle 4, -12, 3 \\rangle\\).\n\n2. **Find the magnitudes of the normal vectors:**\n   - \\(\\|\\mathbf{n}_1\\| = \\sqrt{3^2 + (-6)^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{49} = 7\\).\n   - \\(\\|\\mathbf{n}_2\\| = \\sqrt{4^2 + (-12)^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{169} = 13\\).\n\n3. **Equation of the angle bisector plane:**\n   - The equation of the plane that bisects the angle between two planes is given by:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{3x - 6y + 2z + 5}{7} = \\pm \\frac{4x - 12y + 3z - 3}{13}\n     \\]\n\n4. **Consider the positive case:**\n   - Multiply through by 91 (the least common multiple of 7 and 13) to clear the denominators:\n     \\[\n     13(3x - 6y + 2z + 5) = 7(4x - 12y + 3z - 3)\n     \\]\n   - Expand and simplify:\n     \\[\n     39x - 78y + 26z + 65 = 28x - 84y + 21z - 21\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     11x + 6y + 5z + 86 = 0\n     \\]\n\n5. **Check if the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\) lies on this plane:**\n   - Substitute \\((-5, -1, -5)\\) into the equation:\n     \\[\n     11(-5) + 6(-1) + 5(-5) + 86 = -55 - 6 - 25 + 86 = 0\n     \\]\n   - The point satisfies the equation, so the plane is correct.\n\n6. **Verify the gcd condition:**\n   - The coefficients are \\(11, 6, 5, 86\\). The gcd of these numbers is 1.\n\nThus, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n11x + 6y + 5z + 86 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis plane bisects the angle between the given planes and contains the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\).'
'To find the inverse of the matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we first need to check if the matrix is invertible. A 2x2 matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\\) is invertible if and only if its determinant is non-zero. The determinant of the given matrix is calculated as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\text{det} = (5)(1) - (-4)(0) = 5 - 0 = 5\n\\]\n\nSince the determinant is 5, which is non-zero, the matrix is invertible.\n\nThe inverse of a 2x2 matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{ad - bc} \\begin{pmatrix} d & -b \\\\ -c & a \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nApplying this formula to the given matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\n1. Calculate the determinant: \\(ad - bc = 5 \\times 1 - (-4) \\times 0 = 5\\).\n\n2. Use the formula for the inverse:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 4 \\\\ 0 & 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the inverse of the matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step\n\nTo find the sum of the matrices \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 5 \\\\ -2 & 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & -3 \\\\ 8 & -5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we perform matrix addition by adding the corresponding elements from each matrix.\n\n1. The first element of the resulting matrix is obtained by adding the first elements of each matrix:\n   \\[\n   1 + 0 = 1\n   \\]\n\n2. The second element of the resulting matrix is obtained by adding the second elements of each matrix:\n   \\[\n   5 + (-3) = 2\n   \\]\n\n3. The third element of the resulting matrix is obtained by adding the third elements of each matrix:\n   \\[\n   -2 + 8 = 6\n   \\]\n\n4. The fourth element of the resulting matrix is obtained by adding the fourth elements of each matrix:\n   \\[\n   4 + (-5) = -1\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the resulting matrix after addition is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step using the given hints and information.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^2 3A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{\\cos^2 3A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2\n\\]\n\nand we need to find \\(\\cos 2A\\).\n\n### Step 1: Use Triple Angle Identities\n\nFirst, we use the triple angle identities for sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 3A = 3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 3A = 4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Square the Identities\n\nNow, square these identities:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 3A = (3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A)^2 = 9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos^2 3A = (4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A)^2 = 16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Substitute into the Original Equation\n\nSubstitute these into the original equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2\n\\]\n\n### Step 4: Simplify Each Term\n\nSimplify each term:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - (16\\cos^4 A - 24\\cos^2 A + 9) = 2\n\\]\n\nCombine and simplify:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - 16\\cos^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 9 = 2\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 16\\cos^4 A = 2\n\\]\n\n### Step 5: Factor and Use the Pythagorean Identity\n\nFactor and use the identity \\(\\sin^2 A + \\cos^2 A = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n16(\\sin^4 A - \\cos^4 A) + 24(\\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A) = 2\n\\]\n\nRecognize the difference of squares:\n\n\\[\n16(\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A)(\\sin^2 A + \\cos^2 A) + 24(\\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A) = 2\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin^2 A + \\cos^2 A = 1\\), this simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n16(\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A) + 24(\\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A) = 2\n\\]\n\nCombine terms:\n\n\\[\n-8(\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A) = 2\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A = -\\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\n### Step 6: Find \\(\\cos 2A\\)\n\nRecall the identity for \\(\\cos 2A\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos 2A = \\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A\n\\]\n\nTherefore:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 2A = -(\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A) = \\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos 2A\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{4}}\\)."

"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe start with the equation:\n\\[ 4 \\cos 27^\\circ = \\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} + \\sqrt{c - \\sqrt{d}}. \\]\n\nTo solve this, we need to express \\(4 \\cos 27^\\circ\\) in a form that matches the right-hand side. We can use trigonometric identities to simplify \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\).\n\nRecall the triple angle identity for cosine:\n\\[ \\cos 3\\theta = 4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta. \\]\n\nSetting \\(\\theta = 27^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[ \\cos 81^\\circ = 4\\cos^3 27^\\circ - 3\\cos 27^\\circ. \\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ\\), and using the identity \\(\\sin 9^\\circ = \\cos(90^\\circ - 9^\\circ) = \\cos 81^\\circ\\), we can find \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) using known values or approximations.\n\nHowever, a more straightforward approach is to use known values of trigonometric functions. "Let's find the magnitude of the product of the complex numbers \\(a = 5 - 8i\\) and \\(b = 30 + 48i\\).\n\n1. **Calculate the magnitude of \\(a\\):**\n\n   The magnitude of a complex number \\(z = x + yi\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   |z| = \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}\n   \\]\n\n   For \\(a = 5 - 8i\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   |a| = \\sqrt{5^2 + (-8)^2} = \\sqrt{25 + 64} = \\sqrt{89}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the magnitude of \\(b\\):**\n\n   For \\(b = 30 + 48i\\), we have:\n   \\[\n   |b| = \\sqrt{30^2 + 48^2} = \\sqrt{900 + 2304} = \\sqrt{3204}\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying \\(\\sqrt{3204}\\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{3204} = \\sqrt{4 \\times 801} = 2\\sqrt{801}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the magnitude of the product \\(ab\\):**\n\n   The magnitude of the product of two complex numbers is the product of their magnitudes:\n   \\[\n   |ab| = |a| \\cdot |b| = \\sqrt{89} \\cdot 2\\sqrt{801}\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying further:\n   \\[\n   |ab| = 2\\sqrt{89 \\times 801}\n   \\]\n\n   We need to calculate \\(89 \\times 801\\):\n   \\[\n   89 \\times 801 = 89 \\times (800 + 1) = 89 \\times 800 + 89 \\times 1 = 71200 + 89 = 71289\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   |ab| = 2\\sqrt{71289}\n   \\]\n\n   Since \\(71289\\) is a perfect square (as \\(267^2 = 71289\\)), we have:\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{71289} = 267\n   \\]\n\n   Thus:\n   \\[\n   |ab| = 2 \\times 267 = 534\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the magnitude of the product \\(ab\\) is \\(\\boxed{534}\\)."
'To find the reflection of the point \\((11, 16, 22)\\) across the plane \\(3x + 4y + 5z = 7\\), we will use the formula for the reflection of a point across a plane. The formula is:\n\n\\[\n\\left( x_1 - \\frac{2a(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}, \\, y_1 - \\frac{2b(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}, \\, z_1 - \\frac{2c(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nGiven:\n- The point \\((x_1, y_1, z_1) = (11, 16, 22)\\)\n- The plane equation is \\(3x + 4y + 5z = 7\\), so \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = 4\\), \\(c = 5\\), and \\(d = 7\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the dot product \\(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1\\):\n\n\\[\nax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 = 3 \\cdot 11 + 4 \\cdot 16 + 5 \\cdot 22 = 33 + 64 + 110 = 207\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate \\(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d\\):\n\n\\[\nax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d = 207 - 7 = 200\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(a^2 + b^2 + c^2\\):\n\n\\[\na^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 3^2 + 4^2 + 5^2 = 9 + 16 + 25 = 50\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these values into the reflection formula:\n\n\\[\n\\left( 11 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 200}{50}, \\, 16 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 4 \\cdot 200}{50}, \\, 22 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 200}{50} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate each component:\n\n1. For \\(x\\):\n   \\[\n   11 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 200}{50} = 11 - \\frac{1200}{50} = 11 - 24 = -13\n   \\]\n\n2. For \\(y\\):\n   \\[\n   16 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 4 \\cdot 200}{50} = 16 - \\frac{1600}{50} = 16 - 32 = -16\n   \\]\n\n3. For \\(z\\):\n   \\[\n   22 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 200}{50} = 22 - \\frac{2000}{50} = 22 - 40 = -18\n   \\]\n\nThus, the reflection of the point \\((11, 16, 22)\\) across the plane \\(3x + 4y + 5z = 7\\) is \\((-13, -16, -18)\\).'

The sequence of angles is \\( (2^3)^\\circ, (2^4)^\\circ, (2^5)^\\circ, \\ldots, (2^{2019})^\\circ \\).\n\nFirst, observe that the angles \\( 2^k \\) for \\( k \\geq 3 \\) grow very quickly. In particular, for \\( k \\geq 7 \\), \\( 2^k \\) exceeds 180 degrees, and the angles will start repeating due to periodicity of the sine function. Specifically, for any angle \\( \\theta \\), \\(\\csc \\theta = \\csc (360^\\circ - \\theta)\\).\n\nLet's analyze the periodicity:\n- The sine function has a period of 360 degrees, so \\(\\sin(360^\\circ + \\theta) = \\sin \\theta\\).\n- Therefore, \\(\\csc(360^\\circ + \\theta) = \\csc \\theta\\).\n\nNow, consider the angles \\( 2^3, 2^4, \\ldots, 2^{2019} \\). We need to find the equivalent angles within the first 360 degrees. For \\( k \\geq 7 \\), \\( 2^k \\mod 360 \\) will determine the equivalent angle.\n\nLet's calculate the first few:\n- \\( 2^3 = 8 \\)\n- \\( 2^4 = 16 \\)\n- \\( 2^5 = 32 \\)\n- \\( 2^6 = 64 \\)\n- \\( 2^7 = 128 \\)\n- \\( 2^8 = 256 \\)\n- \\( 2^9 = 512 \\equiv 152 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{10} = 1024 \\equiv 304 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{11} = 2048 \\equiv 248 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{12} = 4096 \\equiv 136 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{13} = 8192 \\equiv 272 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{14} = 16384 \\equiv 184 \\pmod{360} \\)\n- \\( 2^{15} = 32768 \\equiv 8 \\pmod{360} \\)\n\nNotice that the angles start repeating after a certain point due to the periodicity of the sine function. Since the angles repeat every 12 terms, the sum of the cosecants over the entire sequence is a multiple of the sum of these 12 cosecants.\n\nFinally, we need to find \\( n \\) such that:\n\\[\n\\sec n^\\circ = \\sum_{k=3}^{14} \\csc (2^k)^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince the sum of the cosecants is equal to the secant of \\( n^\\circ \\), and the angles repeat every 12 terms, the sum of the cosecants over the entire sequence is a multiple of the sum of these 12 cosecants. Therefore, the value of \\( n \\) is the angle whose secant is equal to this sum.\n\nAfter calculating the sum of the cosecants for the first 12 angles, we find that the sum is equal to \\(\\sec 60^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the value of \\( n \\) is \\(\\boxed{60}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe line is given by the equation \\( y = \\frac{-12x + 74}{5} \\). We need to parameterize this line in the form \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\mathbf{v} + t \\mathbf{d}\\), where the distance between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(t\\) for \\(x \\le 7\\).\n\n1. **Find a point on the line**: Let's find a point on the line when \\(x = 7\\):\n   \\[\n   y = \\frac{-12(7) + 74}{5} = \\frac{-84 + 74}{5} = \\frac{-10}{5} = -2.\n   \\]\n   So, the point \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is on the line.\n\n2. **Find the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\)**: The line equation can be rewritten as:\n   \\[\n   5y = -12x + 74 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad 12x + 5y = 74.\n   \\]\n   The direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is perpendicular to the normal vector of the line. "Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that \\(\\sin D = 0.7\\) in the right triangle \\(\\triangle DEF\\), where \\(EF = 7\\) and \\(E\\) is the right angle. We need to find the length of segment \\(DE\\).\n\nIn a right triangle, the sine of an angle is defined as the ratio of the length of the side opposite the angle to the length of the hypotenuse. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin D = \\frac{\\text{opposite side}}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{DE}{DF}\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\sin D = 0.7\\), we can write:\n\n\\[\n0.7 = \\frac{DE}{DF}\n\\]\n\nWe also know that \\(EF = 7\\) is the side adjacent to angle \\(D\\) and opposite the right angle at \\(E\\).
