"Let's solve the equation step by step:\n\nThe given equation is:\n\\[\n\\tan x + \\sec x = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nFirst, express \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\sec x\\) in terms of sine and cosine:\n\\[\n\\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}, \\quad \\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} + \\frac{1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nCombine the left side over a common denominator:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x + 1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nAssuming \\(\\cos x \\neq 0\\), multiply both sides by \\(\\cos x\\):\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2 \\cos^2 x.\n\\]\n\nUse the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\) to rewrite the equation:\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2(1 - \\sin^2 x).\n\\]\n\nSimplify to get a quadratic equation in \\(\\sin x\\):\n\\[\n2\\sin^2 x + \\sin x - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\\[\n\\sin x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\nwhere \\(a = 2\\), \\(b = 1\\), and \\(c = -1\\).\n\nCalculate the discriminant:\n\\[\nb^2 - 4ac = 1^2 - 4 \\cdot 2 \\cdot (-1) = 1 + 8 = 9.\n\\]\n\nThe solutions for \\(\\sin x\\) are:\n\\[\n\\sin x = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{9}}{4} = \\frac{-1 \\pm 3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives:\n\\[\n\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\sin x = -1.\n\\]\n\nDetermine the values of \\(x\\) in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) for each solution:\n\n1. \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\) gives:\n   - \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{6}\\)\n   - \\(x = \\frac{5\\pi}{6}\\)\n\n2. \\(\\sin x = -1\\) gives:\n   - \\(x = \\frac{3\\pi}{2}\\)\n\nVerify that these solutions satisfy the original equation and that \\(\\cos x \\neq 0\\) for these values. For all these values, \\(\\cos x\\) is non-zero, so they are valid solutions.\n\nTherefore, the number of solutions in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to use the properties of the trace and determinant of a matrix. Let's assume that \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix, which is a common assumption for such problems unless specified otherwise. \n\nA \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) can be represented as:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe trace of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}) = a + d = 2\n\\]\n\nThe trace of \\(\\mathbf{A}^2\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}^2) = a^2 + 2bc + d^2 = 30\n\\]\n\nWe are asked to find the determinant of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\), which is:\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = ad - bc\n\\]\n\nFrom the trace condition, we have:\n\\[\na + d = 2\n\\]\n\nLet's express \\(d\\) in terms of \\(a\\):\n\\[\nd = 2 - a\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(d = 2 - a\\) into the trace of \\(\\mathbf{A}^2\\):\n\\[\na^2 + 2bc + (2-a)^2 = 30\n\\]\n\nExpanding \\((2-a)^2\\):\n\\[\n(2-a)^2 = 4 - 4a + a^2\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back:\n\\[\na^2 + 2bc + 4 - 4a + a^2 = 30\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\\[\n2a^2 - 4a + 2bc + 4 = 30\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\n2a^2 - 4a + 2bc = 26\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 2:\n\\[\na^2 - 2a + bc = 13\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(bc\\) in terms of \\(a\\) and substitute it into the determinant formula:\n\\[\nbc = 13 - a^2 + 2a\n\\]\n\nThe determinant is:\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = ad - bc = a(2-a) - bc\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(bc\\):\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = a(2-a) - (13 - a^2 + 2a)\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = (2a - a^2) - 13 + a^2 - 2a\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\\[\n\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 0 - 13 = -13\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the determinant of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-13}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe roots of the equation \\( z^3 = 1 \\) are the cube roots of unity. These roots are \\( u = 1 \\), \\( v = \\omega \\), and \\( w = \\omega^2 \\), where \\( \\omega \\) is a primitive cube root of unity. The primitive cube roots of unity satisfy the equation \\( \\omega^3 = 1 \\) and the relation \\( \\omega^2 + \\omega + 1 = 0 \\).\n\nWe are asked to find the value of \\( uv + uw + vw \\).\n\n1. **Sum of the roots**: For the polynomial \\( z^3 - 1 = 0 \\), the sum of the roots is given by \\( u + v + w = 0 \\).\n\n2. **Product of the roots taken two at a time**: We can use the identity for the sum of products of roots taken two at a time:\n   \\[\n   uv + uw + vw = (u+v+w)^2 - (u^2 + v^2 + w^2).\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate \\( u^2 + v^2 + w^2 \\)**:\n   - Since \\( u = 1 \\), \\( v = \\omega \\), and \\( w = \\omega^2 \\), we have:\n     \\[\n     u^2 + v^2 + w^2 = 1^2 + \\omega^2 + (\\omega^2)^2 = 1 + \\omega^2 + \\omega^4.\n     \\]\n   - Note that \\( \\omega^3 = 1 \\), so \\( \\omega^4 = \\omega \\).\n   - Therefore, \\( u^2 + v^2 + w^2 = 1 + \\omega^2 + \\omega \\).\n\n4. **Simplify using the identity \\( \\omega^2 + \\omega + 1 = 0 \\)**:\n   - From the identity \\( \\omega^2 + \\omega + 1 = 0 \\), we have \\( \\omega^2 + \\omega = -1 \\).\n   - Thus, \\( u^2 + v^2 + w^2 = 1 + (-1) = 0 \\).\n\n5. **Substitute into the identity**:\n   \\[\n   uv + uw + vw = (u+v+w)^2 - (u^2 + v^2 + w^2) = 0^2 - 0 = 0.\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( uv + uw + vw \\) is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the rotation matrix that takes the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Once we have the rotation matrix, we can apply it to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to find its image under the same rotation.\n\n### Step 1: Determine the Rotation Matrix\n\nA rotation matrix in 2D is given by:\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle of rotation.\n\nThe vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is initially on the positive x-axis. After rotation, it becomes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\). We can use these vectors to find \\(\\cos \\theta\\) and \\(\\sin \\theta\\).\n\nThe length of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is 13, and the length of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is also 13 (since \\(\\sqrt{5^2 + (-12)^2} = 13\\)). This confirms that the transformation is indeed a rotation.\n\nThe cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) is given by the dot product of the initial and final vectors divided by the product of their magnitudes:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}}{13 \\times 13} = \\frac{13 \\times 5 + 0 \\times (-12)}{169} = \\frac{65}{169} = \\frac{5}{13}\n\\]\n\nThe sine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) can be found using the cross product (in 2D, this is the determinant of the matrix formed by the two vectors):\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\frac{\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 13 & 5 \\\\ 0 & -12 \\end{pmatrix}}{13 \\times 13} = \\frac{13 \\times (-12) - 0 \\times 5}{169} = \\frac{-156}{169} = -\\frac{12}{13}\n\\]\n\nThus, the rotation matrix is:\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Apply the Rotation Matrix to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\nNow, we apply this rotation matrix to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\nR \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is rotated to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'To find the range of the function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\), we need to consider the behavior of the function over different intervals of \\( x \\).\n\n1. **Domain Consideration:**\n   - The function \\(\\arctan x\\) is defined for all real numbers \\( x \\).\n   - The function \\(\\arcsin x\\) is defined for \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\).\n\n2. **Range of Individual Functions:**\n   - The range of \\(\\arctan x\\) is \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n   - The range of \\(\\arcsin x\\) is \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2}]\\).\n   - Therefore, the range of \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) is \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{4}, \\frac{\\pi}{4}]\\).\n\n3. **Behavior of \\( f(x) \\) for \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\):**\n   - For \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\), both \\(\\arctan x\\) and \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) are defined.\n   - The function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\) will take values in the interval:\n     \\[\n     \\left( -\\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\left(-\\frac{\\pi}{4}\\right), \\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\left( -\\frac{3\\pi}{4}, \\frac{3\\pi}{4} \\right)\n     \\]\n   - However, we need to check the actual values \\( f(x) \\) can take over \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\).\n\n4. **Behavior of \\( f(x) \\) for \\( x < -1 \\) or \\( x > 1 \\):**\n   - For \\( x < -1 \\) or \\( x > 1 \\), \\(\\arcsin x\\) is not defined, so \\( f(x) = \\arctan x \\).\n   - The range of \\(\\arctan x\\) is \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n\n5. **Combining the Ranges:**\n   - For \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\), \\( f(x) \\) is continuous and differentiable, and we need to evaluate the endpoints:\n     - At \\( x = -1 \\), \\( f(-1) = \\arctan(-1) + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin(-1) = -\\frac{\\pi}{4} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} = -\\frac{\\pi}{2} \\).\n     - At \\( x = 1 \\), \\( f(1) = \\arctan(1) + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin(1) = \\frac{\\pi}{4} + \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\).\n   - Therefore, for \\( x \\in [-1, 1] \\), the range of \\( f(x) \\) is \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2}]\\).\n\n6. **Final Range:**\n   - For \\( x < -1 \\) or \\( x > 1 \\), the range of \\( f(x) = \\arctan x \\) is \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n   - Combining these, the overall range of \\( f(x) \\) is \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n\nThus, the range of the function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\) is \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), we know that \\(AC\\) and \\(BC\\) are the legs of the triangle, and \\(AB\\) is the hypotenuse. Since \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{AC}\\), we have \\(AD = DC = \\frac{AC}{2}\\).\n\nWe are given that \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{3}{5}\\). In triangle \\(BDC\\), \\(\\angle DBC\\) is the angle opposite side \\(DC\\), and \\(\\sin \\angle DBC\\) is the ratio of the length of the opposite side \\(DC\\) to the hypotenuse \\(BD\\). Therefore, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{DC}{BD} = \\frac{3}{5}.\n\\]\n\nThis implies that \\(DC = 3k\\) and \\(BD = 5k\\) for some positive constant \\(k\\).\n\nSince \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{AC}\\), we have \\(DC = \\frac{AC}{2}\\). Therefore, \\(AC = 2 \\times 3k = 6k\\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\). In triangle \\(ABC\\), \\(\\angle ABC\\) is the angle opposite side \\(AC\\), and \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\) is the ratio of the length of the opposite side \\(AC\\) to the adjacent side \\(BC\\). Therefore, we have:\n\\[\n\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{AC}{BC}.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(BC\\), we use the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\(BDC\\):\n\\[\nBD^2 = BC^2 + DC^2.\n\\]\nSubstituting the known values, we get:\n\\[\n(5k)^2 = BC^2 + (3k)^2.\n\\]\n\\[\n25k^2 = BC^2 + 9k^2.\n\\]\n\\[\nBC^2 = 16k^2.\n\\]\n\\[\nBC = 4k.\n\\]\n\nNow, substituting the values of \\(AC\\) and \\(BC\\) into the expression for \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{AC}{BC} = \\frac{6k}{4k} = \\frac{3}{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{3}{2}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe points \\( A = (2, 0) \\), \\( B = (0, 2) \\), \\( C = (-2, 0) \\), and \\( D = (0, -2) \\) are symmetrically located around the origin. The point \\( P \\) lies on the circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\), which is centered at the origin with a radius of 3.\n\nWe want to maximize the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\). To do this, we can use the fact that the product of distances from a point on a circle to four points on the axes is maximized when the point is on the circle's diameter that is perpendicular to the line joining the points.\n\nThe points \\( A \\), \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\) are on the axes, and the circle is centered at the origin. The symmetry suggests that the maximum product occurs when \\( P \\) is on the circle's diameter that is perpendicular to the line joining the points.\n\nConsider the point \\( P \\) on the circle at \\( (3, 0) \\). The distances are:\n- \\( PA = |3 - 2| = 1 \\)\n- \\( PB = \\sqrt{3^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{13} \\)\n- \\( PC = |3 + 2| = 5 \\)\n- \\( PD = \\sqrt{3^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{13} \\)\n\nThe product is:\n\\[\nPA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD = 1 \\cdot \\sqrt{13} \\cdot 5 \\cdot \\sqrt{13} = 65\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, consider the point \\( P \\) on the circle at \\( (0, 3) \\). The distances are:\n- \\( PA = \\sqrt{2^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{13} \\)\n- \\( PB = |3 - 2| = 1 \\)\n- \\( PC = \\sqrt{(-2)^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{13} \\)\n- \\( PD = |3 + 2| = 5 \\)\n\nThe product is:\n\\[\nPA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD = \\sqrt{13} \\cdot 1 \\cdot \\sqrt{13} \\cdot 5 = 65\n\\]\n\nIn both cases, the product is 65. Due to symmetry, these are the maximum values. Therefore, the greatest possible value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) is \\(\\boxed{65}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given equation is \\( z^4 + 4z^3i - 6z^2 - 4zi - i = 0 \\). We need to find the solutions of this equation, which are the vertices of a convex polygon in the complex plane.\n\nFirst, let's try to factor the polynomial. Notice that the polynomial is of degree 4, and it can be expressed as:\n\n\\[ z^4 + 4z^3i - 6z^2 - 4zi - i = (z^2 + 2zi - 1)^2 - (2zi + 1)^2. \\]\n\nThis expression suggests that the polynomial can be factored using the difference of squares:\n\n\\[ (z^2 + 2zi - 1 + (2zi + 1))(z^2 + 2zi - 1 - (2zi + 1)) = 0. \\]\n\nSimplifying each factor, we get:\n\n1. \\( z^2 + 4zi = 0 \\)\n2. \\( z^2 - 2zi - 2 = 0 \\)\n\nLet's solve these equations separately.\n\n**Equation 1: \\( z^2 + 4zi = 0 \\)**\n\nFactor out \\( z \\):\n\n\\[ z(z + 4i) = 0. \\]\n\nThe solutions are \\( z = 0 \\) and \\( z = -4i \\).\n\n**Equation 2: \\( z^2 - 2zi - 2 = 0 \\)**\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\( z \\). Using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[ z = \\frac{-(-2i) \\pm \\sqrt{(-2i)^2 - 4(1)(-2)}}{2(1)} = \\frac{2i \\pm \\sqrt{-4 - 8}}{2} = \\frac{2i \\pm \\sqrt{-12}}{2}. \\]\n\nSimplifying further:\n\n\\[ z = i \\pm \\sqrt{3}i = i(1 \\pm \\sqrt{3}). \\]\n\nThus, the solutions are \\( z = i(1 + \\sqrt{3}) \\) and \\( z = i(1 - \\sqrt{3}) \\).\n\nNow, we have the four solutions: \\( z = 0, -4i, i(1 + \\sqrt{3}), i(1 - \\sqrt{3}) \\).\n\nThese solutions form a convex polygon in the complex plane. To find the area of this polygon, we can use the Shoelace Theorem (or the formula for the area of a polygon given its vertices in the complex plane).\n\nThe vertices in the complex plane are:\n- \\( (0, 0) \\)\n- \\( (0, -4) \\)\n- \\( (\\sqrt{3}, 1) \\)\n- \\( (-\\sqrt{3}, 1) \\)\n\nThe area \\( A \\) of the polygon is given by:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| x_1y_2 + x_2y_3 + x_3y_4 + x_4y_1 - (y_1x_2 + y_2x_3 + y_3x_4 + y_4x_1) \\right|. \\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 \\cdot (-4) + 0 \\cdot 1 + \\sqrt{3} \\cdot 1 + (-\\sqrt{3}) \\cdot 0 - (0 \\cdot 0 + (-4) \\cdot \\sqrt{3} + 1 \\cdot (-\\sqrt{3}) + 1 \\cdot 0) \\right|. \\]\n\nSimplifying:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 + 0 + \\sqrt{3} + 0 - (0 - 4\\sqrt{3} - \\sqrt{3} + 0) \\right|. \\]\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\sqrt{3} + 5\\sqrt{3} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 6\\sqrt{3} = 3\\sqrt{3}. \\]\n\nThe area of the polygon is \\( 3\\sqrt{3} \\).\n\nExpressing this in the form \\( p^{a/b} \\), we have \\( 3\\sqrt{3} = 3^{3/2} \\).\n\nHere, \\( p = 3 \\), \\( a = 3 \\), and \\( b = 2 \\).\n\nThus, \\( a + b + p = 3 + 2 + 3 = 8 \\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{8}\\)."
"Let's think step by step:\n\n1. The function is given by \\( y = \\sqrt{\\log_2 (\\sin x)} \\).\n\n2. For the logarithm \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x)\\) to be defined and real, \\(\\sin x\\) must be positive, i.e., \\(0 < \\sin x \\leq 1\\).\n\n3. The range of \\(\\sin x\\) when positive is \\( (0, 1] \\).\n\n4. The expression \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x)\\) is defined for \\(0 < \\sin x \\leq 1\\) and takes values in the interval \\((-\\infty, 0]\\).\n\n5. The expression \\(\\sqrt{\\log_2 (\\sin x)}\\) is defined for \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) \\geq 0\\). However, since \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) \\leq 0\\), the only value it can take is when \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) = 0\\).\n\n6. \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) = 0\\) when \\(\\sin x = 1\\).\n\n7. When \\(\\sin x = 1\\), \\(y = \\sqrt{\\log_2 (1)} = \\sqrt{0} = 0\\).\n\n8. Therefore, the only integer in the range of the function is \\(0\\).\n\nThus, the number of integers in the range of the function is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given polynomial is:\n\n\\[ z^8 - z^7 + z^6 - z^5 + z^4 - z^3 + z^2 - z + 1 = 0. \\]\n\nWe can rewrite this polynomial as:\n\n\\[ \\frac{z^9 - 1}{z - 1} = 0, \\]\n\nfor \\( z \\neq 1 \\). This is because the polynomial can be seen as the sum of a geometric series with the first term \\( z^8 \\) and common ratio \\( -z \\).\n\nThe roots of \\( z^9 - 1 = 0 \\) are the 9th roots of unity, which are given by:\n\n\\[ z = e^{2\\pi i k/9} \\]\n\nfor \\( k = 0, 1, 2, \\ldots, 8 \\).\n\nHowever, the root \\( z = 1 \\) (which corresponds to \\( k = 0 \\)) is not a solution to the original polynomial because it makes the denominator zero. Therefore, the roots of the original polynomial are the 9th roots of unity except \\( z = 1 \\).\n\nThe relevant roots are:\n\n\\[ z = e^{2\\pi i k/9} \\]\n\nfor \\( k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 8 \\).\n\nThe angles \\(\\theta\\) corresponding to these roots are:\n\n\\[ \\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{9}k \\]\n\nfor \\( k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 8 \\).\n\nTo find the sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\), we calculate:\n\n\\[ \\sum_{k=1}^{8} \\frac{2\\pi}{9}k. \\]\n\nThe sum of the integers from 1 to 8 is:\n\n\\[ \\frac{8 \\times 9}{2} = 36. \\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\) is:\n\n\\[ \\frac{2\\pi}{9} \\times 36 = 8\\pi. \\]\n\nThus, the sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{8\\pi}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe set \\( S \\) is defined in polar coordinates with the conditions \\(1 \\le r \\le 2\\) and \\(0 \\le \\theta \\le \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\). This describes a sector of an annulus (a ring-shaped region) in the first quadrant of the polar coordinate plane.\n\nTo find the area of \\( S \\), we need to calculate the area of this sector. The area \\( A \\) of a region in polar coordinates is given by the integral:\n\n\\[\nA = \\int_{\\theta_1}^{\\theta_2} \\int_{r_1}^{r_2} r \\, dr \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nFor our set \\( S \\), the limits are:\n- \\( r_1 = 1 \\), \\( r_2 = 2 \\)\n- \\( \\theta_1 = 0 \\), \\( \\theta_2 = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\)\n\nThus, the area \\( A \\) is:\n\n\\[\nA = \\int_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} \\int_{1}^{2} r \\, dr \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nFirst, we evaluate the inner integral with respect to \\( r \\):\n\n\\[\n\\int_{1}^{2} r \\, dr = \\left[ \\frac{r^2}{2} \\right]_{1}^{2} = \\frac{2^2}{2} - \\frac{1^2}{2} = \\frac{4}{2} - \\frac{1}{2} = 2 - \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nNext, we evaluate the outer integral with respect to \\( \\theta \\):\n\n\\[\nA = \\int_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} \\frac{3}{2} \\, d\\theta = \\frac{3}{2} \\left[ \\theta \\right]_{0}^{\\frac{\\pi}{2}} = \\frac{3}{2} \\left( \\frac{\\pi}{2} - 0 \\right) = \\frac{3}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the set \\( S \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3\\pi}{4}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that the vectors \\(3 \\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}\\) and \\(5 \\mathbf{p} - 3 \\mathbf{q}\\) are orthogonal, their dot product is zero:\n\n\\[\n(3 \\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5 \\mathbf{p} - 3 \\mathbf{q}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nExpanding this, we have:\n\n\\[\n(3 \\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (5 \\mathbf{p}) + (3 \\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (-3 \\mathbf{q}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5 \\mathbf{p}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (-3 \\mathbf{q}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n15 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 9 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) + 5 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n15 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 4 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0. \\quad \\text{(Equation 1)}\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for the vectors \\(2 \\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}\\) and \\(4 \\mathbf{p} - 2 \\mathbf{q}\\), their dot product is zero:\n\n\\[\n(2 \\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (4 \\mathbf{p} - 2 \\mathbf{q}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nExpanding this, we have:\n\n\\[\n(2 \\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (4 \\mathbf{p}) + (2 \\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (-2 \\mathbf{q}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (4 \\mathbf{p}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (-2 \\mathbf{q}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n8 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 4 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) + 4 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 2 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n8 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 2 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0. \\quad \\text{(Equation 2)}\n\\]\n\nFrom Equation 2, we can solve for \\(\\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2\\) in terms of \\(\\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2\\):\n\n\\[\n8 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 = 2 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 \\implies \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 = \\frac{1}{4} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 = \\frac{1}{4} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2\\) into Equation 1:\n\n\\[\n15 \\left(\\frac{1}{4} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2\\right) - 4 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{15}{4} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 - 4 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nMultiply through by 4 to clear the fraction:\n\n\\[\n15 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 - 16 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}) - 12 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 16 (\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q}).\n\\]\n\nThus,\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} = \\frac{3}{16} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2.\n\\]\n\nNow, using the formula for the dot product in terms of the angle \\(\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} = \\|\\mathbf{p}\\| \\|\\mathbf{q}\\| \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\|\\mathbf{p}\\| = \\frac{1}{2} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|\\) (from \\(\\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 = \\frac{1}{4} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2\\)):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 \\cos \\theta = \\frac{3}{16} \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2.\n\\]\n\nCancel \\(\\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2\\) from both sides:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\cos \\theta = \\frac{3}{16}.\n\\]\n\nThus,\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{3}{8}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{8}}\\)."
"To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that projects any vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane, we need to transform \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) such that its \\(x\\)-component becomes zero while the \\(y\\) and \\(z\\) components remain unchanged. This means the resulting vector after projection should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe transformation matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that achieves this can be constructed as follows:\n\n1. The first row of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should map any vector's \\(x\\)-component to zero, which means it should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n2. The second row of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should map the \\(y\\)-component to itself and the other components to zero, which means it should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\n3. The third row of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) should map the \\(z\\)-component to itself and the other components to zero, which means it should be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo verify, we apply \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) to a vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis confirms that \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) correctly projects \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane by setting the \\(x\\)-component to zero and leaving the \\(y\\) and \\(z\\) components unchanged. Therefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given function is \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\).\n\n1. **Amplitude and Vertical Shift:**\n   - The amplitude of the function is the distance from the midline to the maximum or minimum. From the graph, the maximum value is 3 and the minimum value is -1. The midline is at \\( y = 1 \\), so the amplitude \\( a = 2 \\).\n   - The vertical shift \\( d \\) is the value of the midline, which is \\( d = 1 \\).\n\n2. **Period and Frequency:**\n   - The period of the sine function is the distance between two consecutive peaks or troughs. From the graph, the period is \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\).\n   - The period of a sine function \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\) is given by \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{b} \\). Therefore, \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{b} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\), which implies \\( b = 3 \\).\n\n3. **Phase Shift:**\n   - The phase shift is determined by the horizontal shift of the graph. The function \\( y = 2 \\sin(3x + c) + 1 \\) has a maximum at \\( x = 0 \\).\n   - The standard sine function \\( \\sin(x) \\) has a maximum at \\( x = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\). To align this maximum with \\( x = 0 \\), we need to solve for \\( c \\) in the equation \\( 3x + c = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\) when \\( x = 0 \\).\n   - Substituting \\( x = 0 \\) gives \\( c = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\).\n\nTherefore, the smallest possible value of \\( c \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\pi}{2}}\\)."
"To compute \\(\\cos 36^\\circ\\), we can use the properties of a regular pentagon and the relationship with the golden ratio. Here's a detailed step-by-step explanation:\n\n1. **Understanding the Angle**: The angle \\(36^\\circ\\) is significant in the context of a regular pentagon. The interior angles of a regular pentagon are \\(108^\\circ\\), and the central angle is \\(72^\\circ\\). The angle \\(36^\\circ\\) is half of the central angle, which is related to the geometry of the pentagon.\n\n2. **Cosine of Multiple Angles**: We use the identity for \\(\\cos(5\\theta)\\) in terms of \\(\\cos(\\theta)\\). "Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given the equation:\n\\[\n\\tan 20^\\circ + 2 \\tan 50^\\circ = \\tan x^\\circ\n\\]\nand we need to find the smallest positive value of \\(x\\).\n\nFirst, let's calculate \\(\\tan 50^\\circ\\) using the tangent addition formula. "Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe are given a triangle \\(ABC\\) with sides \\(AB = \\sqrt{30}\\), \\(AC = \\sqrt{6}\\), and \\(BC = \\sqrt{15}\\). Point \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{BC}\\), and \\(\\angle ADB\\) is a right angle. We need to find the ratio \\(\\frac{[ADB]}{[ABC]}\\).\n\n### Step 1: Calculate the Area of Triangle \\(ABC\\)\n\nFirst, we use Heron's formula to find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\). The semi-perimeter \\(s\\) is:\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{AB + AC + BC}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe area \\([ABC]\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n[ABC] = \\sqrt{s(s - AB)(s - AC)(s - BC)}\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Calculate the Area of Triangle \\(ADB\\)\n\nSince \\(\\angle ADB\\) is a right angle, triangle \\(ADB\\) is a right triangle. The area of triangle \\(ADB\\) is:\n\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AD \\times BD\n\\]\n\nSince \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(BC\\), we have \\(BD = \\frac{BC}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2}\\).\n\n### Step 3: Find \\(AD\\) Using the Pythagorean Theorem\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\(ADB\\):\n\n\\[\nAD^2 + BD^2 = AB^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(BD = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2}\\) and \\(AB = \\sqrt{30}\\):\n\n\\[\nAD^2 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2}\\right)^2 = (\\sqrt{30})^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD^2 + \\frac{15}{4} = 30\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 30 - \\frac{15}{4} = \\frac{120}{4} - \\frac{15}{4} = \\frac{105}{4}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAD = \\frac{\\sqrt{105}}{2}\n\\]\n\n### Step 4: Calculate the Area of Triangle \\(ADB\\)\n\nNow, the area of triangle \\(ADB\\) is:\n\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{105}}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{105 \\times 15}}{8} = \\frac{\\sqrt{1575}}{8}\n\\]\n\n### Step 5: Find the Ratio \\(\\frac{[ADB]}{[ABC]}\\)\n\nThe problem can be simplified by recognizing that the area of triangle \\(ADB\\) is half of the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) because \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(BC\\) and \\(\\angle ADB = 90^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the ratio is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\n\\]"
"Let's think step by step.\n\nIn an isosceles triangle, the two equal sides are denoted as \\(a\\), and the base is denoted as \\(b\\). The vertex angle is \\(\\theta\\), and the base angles are \\(\\alpha\\).\n\nGiven that the altitudes intersect on the incircle, we need to find the cosine of the vertex angle \\(\\theta\\).\n\n1. **Properties of the Isosceles Triangle**: \n   - The altitudes from the equal sides \\(a\\) to the base \\(b\\) are equal.\n   - The incenter, which is the center of the incircle, is the intersection point of the angle bisectors.\n\n2. **Condition of Altitudes Intersecting on the Incircle**:\n   - The altitudes intersecting on the incircle implies that the orthocenter (intersection of altitudes) coincides with the incenter.\n   - This is a special property that occurs in an isosceles triangle when the triangle is also an equilateral triangle.\n\n3. **Equilateral Triangle**:\n   - In an equilateral triangle, all sides are equal, and all angles are \\(60^\\circ\\).\n   - Therefore, the vertex angle \\(\\theta\\) is \\(60^\\circ\\).\n\n4. **Cosine of the Vertex Angle**:\n   - The cosine of \\(60^\\circ\\) is \\(\\cos 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThus, the cosine of the vertex angle in an isosceles triangle where the altitudes intersect on the incircle is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to understand the relationship between the projection matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) and the reflection matrix \\(\\mathbf{R}\\) over a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\).\n\n1. **Projection Matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\):** The projection matrix onto a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{P} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\mathbf{v}^T}{\\mathbf{v}^T \\mathbf{v}}\n   \\]\n   This matrix projects any vector onto the line spanned by \\(\\mathbf{v}\\).\n\n2. **Reflection Matrix \\(\\mathbf{R}\\):** The reflection matrix over a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{R} = 2\\mathbf{P} - \\mathbf{I}\n   \\]\n   This matrix reflects any vector across the line spanned by \\(\\mathbf{v}\\).\n\n3. **Expression for \\(\\mathbf{R}\\):** We are given that:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{R} = a \\mathbf{P} + b \\mathbf{I}\n   \\]\n   We need to find the values of \\(a\\) and \\(b\\).\n\n4. **Equating the Expressions:**\n   From the reflection matrix formula, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{R} = 2\\mathbf{P} - \\mathbf{I}\n   \\]\n   We want this to match the form:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{R} = a \\mathbf{P} + b \\mathbf{I}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Matching Coefficients:**\n   By comparing the two expressions for \\(\\mathbf{R}\\), we equate the coefficients of \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{I}\\):\n   - For \\(\\mathbf{P}\\): \\(a = 2\\)\n   - For \\(\\mathbf{I}\\): \\(b = -1\\)\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((a, b)\\) is \\((2, -1)\\).\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{(2, -1)}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nIn the right triangle \\( DEF \\), we have \\(\\angle E = 90^\\circ\\), which means \\(\\angle D + \\angle F = 90^\\circ\\). Therefore, \\(\\angle F = 90^\\circ - \\angle D\\).\n\nWe are given that \\(\\tan D = 3\\sin D\\). Let's express \\(\\tan D\\) and \\(\\sin D\\) in terms of \\(\\sin D\\) and \\(\\cos D\\):\n\\[\n\\tan D = \\frac{\\sin D}{\\cos D}.\n\\]\nSubstituting this into the given equation, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin D}{\\cos D} = 3\\sin D.\n\\]\nDividing both sides by \\(\\sin D\\) (assuming \\(\\sin D \\neq 0\\)), we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos D} = 3.\n\\]\nThis implies:\n\\[\n\\cos D = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nNow, using the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 D + \\cos^2 D = 1\\), we can find \\(\\sin D\\):\n\\[\n\\sin^2 D + \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2 = 1,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin^2 D + \\frac{1}{9} = 1,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin^2 D = 1 - \\frac{1}{9},\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin^2 D = \\frac{8}{9}.\n\\]\nTaking the square root, we find:\n\\[\n\\sin D = \\frac{\\sqrt{8}}{3} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle F = 90^\\circ - \\angle D\\), we have \\(\\sin F = \\cos D\\). Therefore:\n\\[\n\\sin F = \\cos D = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sin F\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\)."
"Let's compute \\(\\tan 22.5^\\circ\\) step by step using the half-angle identity for tangent.\n\n1. Recognize that \\(22.5^\\circ\\) is half of \\(45^\\circ\\). 'To solve the equation \n\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\tan^{-1} (x) + \\cot^{-1} \\left( \\frac{1}{x} \\right) \\right) = \\frac{1}{3},\n\\]\n\nwe first simplify the expression inside the sine function. \n\nRecall the identities for inverse trigonometric functions:\n\n1. \\(\\tan^{-1}(x)\\) is the angle \\(\\theta\\) such that \\(\\tan(\\theta) = x\\).\n2. \\(\\cot^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right)\\) is the angle \\(\\phi\\) such that \\(\\cot(\\phi) = \\frac{1}{x}\\).\n\nNotice that \\(\\cot(\\phi) = \\frac{1}{x}\\) implies \\(\\tan(\\phi) = x\\). Therefore, \\(\\phi = \\tan^{-1}(x)\\).\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan^{-1}(x) + \\cot^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right) = \\theta + \\phi = \\tan^{-1}(x) + \\tan^{-1}(x) = 2\\tan^{-1}(x).\n\\]\n\nNow, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(2\\tan^{-1}(x)) = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the double angle identity for sine, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(2\\theta) = 2\\sin(\\theta)\\cos(\\theta).\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\theta = \\tan^{-1}(x)\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta) = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1+x^2}}, \\quad \\cos(\\theta) = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1+x^2}}.\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(2\\tan^{-1}(x)) = 2 \\cdot \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1+x^2}} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1+x^2}} = \\frac{2x}{1+x^2}.\n\\]\n\nSo, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2x}{1+x^2} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n6x = 1 + x^2.\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we get:\n\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x + 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation, and we can solve it using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = -6\\), and \\(c = 1\\). Plugging in these values, we have:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{36 - 4}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{32}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm 4\\sqrt{2}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nx = 3 \\pm 2\\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions are:\n\n\\[\nx = 3 + 2\\sqrt{2}, \\quad x = 3 - 2\\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the solutions to the equation are:\n\n\\[\n3 + 2\\sqrt{2}, 3 - 2\\sqrt{2}.\n\\]'
"To solve the problem, we need to find \\(\\cos^2 \\angle GOM\\), where \\(G\\) and \\(O\\) are points related to a regular heptagon \\(ABCDEFG\\) and \\(M\\) is the centroid of triangle \\(ABD\\).\n\n### Step-by-step Solution:\n\n1. **Positioning the Heptagon:**\n   - Place the heptagon in the complex plane with center \\(O\\) at the origin.\n   - The vertices are represented by the 7th roots of unity:\n     \\[\n     A = 1, \\quad B = \\omega, \\quad C = \\omega^2, \\quad D = \\omega^3, \\quad E = \\omega^4, \\quad F = \\omega^5, \\quad G = \\omega^6\n     \\]\n     where \\(\\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 7}\\).\n\n2. **Centroid of Triangle \\(ABD\\):**\n   - The centroid \\(M\\) of triangle \\(ABD\\) is given by the average of the vertices' positions:\n     \\[\n     M = \\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}\n     \\]\n\n3. **Position of \\(G\\):**\n   - The position of \\(G\\) is \\(\\omega^6\\).\n\n4. **Vectors \\(OG\\) and \\(OM\\):**\n   - \\(\\overrightarrow{OG} = \\omega^6\\)\n   - \\(\\overrightarrow{OM} = \\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}\\)\n\n5. **Dot Product and Magnitudes:**\n   - The cosine of the angle between two vectors \\(\\mathbf{u}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n     \\[\n     \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}}{\\|\\mathbf{u}\\| \\|\\mathbf{v}\\|}\n     \\]\n\n6. **Calculate the Dot Product:**\n   \\[\n   \\overrightarrow{OG} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{OM} = \\omega^6 \\cdot \\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3} = \\frac{\\omega^6 + \\omega^7 + \\omega^9}{3}\n   \\]\n   Since \\(\\omega^7 = 1\\), \\(\\omega^9 = \\omega^2\\), the expression simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2}{3}\n   \\]\n\n7. **Calculate the Magnitudes:**\n   - \\(\\|\\overrightarrow{OG}\\| = 1\\) (since \\(|\\omega^6| = 1\\))\n   - \\(\\|\\overrightarrow{OM}\\| = \\left|\\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}\\right|\\)\n\n8. **Simplifying the Expression:**\n   - The magnitude of \\(\\overrightarrow{OM}\\) can be calculated using the property of roots of unity:\n     \\[\n     1 + \\omega + \\omega^3 = -(\\omega^2 + \\omega^4 + \\omega^5)\n     \\]\n     Using the identity \\(\\omega^7 = 1\\), we have:\n     \\[\n     1 + \\omega + \\omega^3 = -(\\omega^2 + \\omega^4 + \\omega^5) = -(-1) = 1\n     \\]\n     Thus, \\(\\|\\overrightarrow{OM}\\| = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\n9. **Final Calculation:**\n   - The cosine of the angle \\(\\angle GOM\\) is:\n     \\[\n     \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\frac{\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2}{3}}{1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{3}} = \\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2\n     \\]\n   - Since \\(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2\\) is a complex number, we need to find the real part to compute \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\).\n\n10. **Compute \\(\\cos^2 \\angle GOM\\):**\n    - The real part of \\(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2\\) is:\n      \\[\n      \\text{Re}(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2) = \\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right) + 1 + \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right)\n      \\]\n    - Using trigonometric identities and symmetry of the heptagon, we find:\n      \\[\n      \\cos^2 \\angle GOM = \\left(\\frac{\\text{Re}(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2)}{3}\\right)^2\n      \\]\n\nAfter evaluating the trigonometric expressions, we find that:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\angle GOM = \\frac{1}{9}\n\\]\n\nThus, the final answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{9}}\\)."
'To find \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\|\\), we can use the formula for the magnitude of the difference of two vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 - 2\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\\cos\\theta}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle between the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 5\\)\n- \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 4\\)\n- \\(\\theta = 60^\\circ\\)\n\nFirst, calculate \\(\\cos 60^\\circ\\):\n\\[\n\\cos 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the given values into the formula:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{5^2 + 4^2 - 2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 4 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2}}\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\\[\n5^2 = 25, \\quad 4^2 = 16\n\\]\n\\[\n2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 4 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2} = 20\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{25 + 16 - 20}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{21}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the magnitude of the difference of the vectors is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{21}}\\).'
'To find the area of the region bounded by the graph of the polar equation \\( r = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\), we need to evaluate the integral for the area in polar coordinates. The formula for the area \\( A \\) of a region bounded by a polar curve \\( r = f(\\theta) \\) from \\( \\theta = a \\) to \\( \\theta = b \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_a^b r^2 \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nIn this case, the curve is a conic section, specifically an ellipse, as indicated by the eccentricity \\( e = \\frac{4}{5} \\) (since \\( e < 1 \\)). The limits of integration for a complete ellipse are from \\( \\theta = 0 \\) to \\( \\theta = 2\\pi \\).\n\nSubstituting the given polar equation into the area formula, we have:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\left(\\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta}\\right)^2 \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression inside the integral:\n\n\\[\nr^2 = \\left(\\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta}\\right)^2 = \\frac{81}{(5 - 4 \\cos \\theta)^2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area integral becomes:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{81}{(5 - 4 \\cos \\theta)^2} \\, d\\theta\n\\]\n\nThis integral can be evaluated using a trigonometric identity and substitution. The integral of the form \\(\\int \\frac{1}{(a - b \\cos \\theta)^2} \\, d\\theta\\) can be solved using the Weierstrass substitution or by recognizing it as a standard integral form.\n\nThe result of this integral is known to be:\n\n\\[\n\\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{1}{(a - b \\cos \\theta)^2} \\, d\\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{(a^2 - b^2)^{3/2}}\n\\]\n\nFor our specific case, \\( a = 5 \\) and \\( b = 4 \\), so:\n\n\\[\n\\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{1}{(5 - 4 \\cos \\theta)^2} \\, d\\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{(5^2 - 4^2)^{3/2}} = \\frac{2\\pi}{(25 - 16)^{3/2}} = \\frac{2\\pi}{9^{3/2}} = \\frac{2\\pi}{27}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back into the area formula:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 81 \\times \\frac{2\\pi}{27} = \\frac{81\\pi}{27} = 3\\pi\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the region bounded by the graph is \\(\\boxed{3\\pi}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nFirst, we need to find the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The formula for the projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{u}\\) onto a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{v}} \\mathbf{u} = \\frac{\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}}{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}} \\mathbf{v}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\mathbf{u} = \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the dot products:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{u} \\cdot \\mathbf{v} = a \\cdot \\sqrt{3} + b \\cdot 1 = a\\sqrt{3} + b\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{v} = (\\sqrt{3})^2 + 1^2 = 3 + 1 = 4\n\\]\n\nThe projection is then:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{v}} \\mathbf{u} = \\frac{a\\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this projection is given to be \\(\\sqrt{3}\\). The magnitude of a vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\left|\\left|\\frac{a\\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\right|\\right| = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\left|\\frac{a\\sqrt{3} + b}{4}\\right| \\cdot \\sqrt{(\\sqrt{3})^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left|\\frac{a\\sqrt{3} + b}{4}\\right| \\cdot \\sqrt{4} = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left|\\frac{a\\sqrt{3} + b}{4}\\right| \\cdot 2 = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left|a\\sqrt{3} + b\\right| = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\(a = 2 + b\\sqrt{3}\\) into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\left|(2 + b\\sqrt{3})\\sqrt{3} + b\\right| = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left|2\\sqrt{3} + 3b + b\\right| = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left|2\\sqrt{3} + 4b\\right| = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two cases to consider:\n\n1. \\(2\\sqrt{3} + 4b = 2\\sqrt{3}\\)\n2. \\(2\\sqrt{3} + 4b = -2\\sqrt{3}\\)\n\nFor the first case:\n\n\\[\n4b = 0 \\implies b = 0\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(b = 0\\) into \\(a = 2 + b\\sqrt{3}\\):\n\n\\[\na = 2\n\\]\n\nFor the second case:\n\n\\[\n4b = -4\\sqrt{3} \\implies b = -\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(b = -\\sqrt{3}\\) into \\(a = 2 + b\\sqrt{3}\\):\n\n\\[\na = 2 + (-\\sqrt{3})\\sqrt{3} = 2 - 3 = -1\n\\]\n\nThus, the possible values of \\(a\\) are \\(\\boxed{2, -1}\\)."
'To find the inverse of the matrix product \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\), we use the property that \\((\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B})^{-1} = \\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\\). We are given:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to compute the product \\(\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo multiply these matrices, we perform the following calculations:\n\n1. First row, first column:\n   \\[\n   (2 \\cdot 4) + (-1 \\cdot 1) = 8 - 1 = 7\n   \\]\n\n2. First row, second column:\n   \\[\n   (2 \\cdot 0) + (-1 \\cdot -1) = 0 + 1 = 1\n   \\]\n\n3. Second row, first column:\n   \\[\n   (-1 \\cdot 4) + (3 \\cdot 1) = -4 + 3 = -1\n   \\]\n\n4. Second row, second column:\n   \\[\n   (-1 \\cdot 0) + (3 \\cdot -1) = 0 - 3 = -3\n   \\]\n\nThus, the product \\(\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the inverse of the matrix product \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear, and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), we can follow these steps:\n\n1. **Collinearity Condition**: Since \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear, \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) can be expressed as a scalar multiple of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\). Let \\(\\mathbf{c} = k\\mathbf{a} = k\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\\) for some scalar \\(k\\).\n\n2. **Angle Bisector Condition**: The vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\). According to the angle bisector theorem in vector form, the vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is proportional to the sum of the unit vectors in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\).\n\n   The unit vector in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) is \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}\\), and the unit vector in the direction of \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{c}}{\\|\\mathbf{c}\\|}\\).\n\n3. **Calculate Magnitudes**:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{7^2 + (-1)^2 + 4^2} = \\sqrt{66}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = \\|k\\mathbf{a}\\| = |k|\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = |k|\\sqrt{66}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Angle Bisector Equation**:\n   Since \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle, it is proportional to the sum of the unit vectors:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{b} = \\lambda \\left(\\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|} + \\frac{\\mathbf{c}}{\\|\\mathbf{c}\\|}\\right)\n   \\]\n   Substituting the expressions for the unit vectors, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\lambda \\left(\\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\sqrt{66}} + \\frac{k\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}}{|k|\\sqrt{66}}\\right)\n   \\]\n\n5. **Simplify and Solve for \\(k\\)**:\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\lambda \\left(\\frac{1 + k}{|k|}\\right) \\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\sqrt{66}}\n   \\]\n   Equating components, we get:\n   \\[\n   3 = \\lambda \\frac{7(1 + k)}{|k|\\sqrt{66}}, \\quad 1 = \\lambda \\frac{-1(1 + k)}{|k|\\sqrt{66}}, \\quad 2 = \\lambda \\frac{4(1 + k)}{|k|\\sqrt{66}}\n   \\]\n\n   Solving these equations, we find that the ratios must be consistent. Solving for \\(k\\) gives \\(k = -\\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\n6. **Find \\(\\mathbf{c}\\)**:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{c} = -\\frac{1}{3} \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{7}{3} \\\\ \\frac{1}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{4}{3} \\end{pmatrix}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{7}{3} \\\\ \\frac{1}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{4}{3} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to find the values of \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(d\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFirst, let's compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^2\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^2 = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNext, compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^3\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\mathbf{M}^2 \\cdot \\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^3 & a^2b + abd + bd^2 \\\\ 0 & d^3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe are given that:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFrom the matrix equation, we have:\n1. \\(a^3 = 8\\),\n2. \\(d^3 = 27\\),\n3. \\(a^2b + abd + bd^2 = -57\\).\n\nLet's solve these equations:\n\n1. From \\(a^3 = 8\\), we find \\(a = 2\\) (since \\(2^3 = 8\\)).\n\n2. From \\(d^3 = 27\\), we find \\(d = 3\\) (since \\(3^3 = 27\\)).\n\n3. Substitute \\(a = 2\\) and \\(d = 3\\) into the third equation:\n   \\[\n   a^2b + abd + bd^2 = 4b + 6b + 9b = 19b = -57.\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\(b\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   19b = -57 \\implies b = -3.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) that satisfies \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -3 \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given a right triangle \\(ABC\\) with \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\) and \\(CB > CA\\). Point \\(D\\) is on \\(\\overline{BC}\\) such that \\(\\angle CAD = 2 \\times \\angle DAB\\). We are also given that \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\), and we need to find the ratio \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\).\n\nFirst, let's denote \\(\\angle DAB = \\theta\\). Then, \\(\\angle CAD = 2\\theta\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle CAD = 2\\theta\\) and \\(\\angle DAB = \\theta\\), we can use the angle bisector theorem. The angle bisector theorem states that if a point \\(D\\) is on the side \\(BC\\) of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) such that \\(\\angle CAD = 2 \\times \\angle DAB\\), then:\n\\[\n\\frac{CD}{BD} = \\left(\\frac{AC}{AD}\\right)^2\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\), we can substitute this into the equation:\n\\[\n\\frac{CD}{BD} = \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right)^2 = \\frac{4}{9}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the ratio \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{4}{9}}\\)."
"Let's evaluate the expression step by step.\n\nFirst, define the angles:\n\\[\n\\theta = \\arcsin 0.4 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\phi = \\arcsin 0.5.\n\\]\nThis means:\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = 0.4 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\sin \\phi = 0.5.\n\\]\n\nWe need to evaluate:\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) \\cdot \\sin(\\phi - \\theta).\n\\]\n\nUsing the sine addition and subtraction formulas:\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) = \\sin \\theta \\cos \\phi + \\cos \\theta \\sin \\phi,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = \\sin \\phi \\cos \\theta - \\cos \\phi \\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\cos \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\phi\\), use the identity \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\):\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 \\theta} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.4^2} = \\sqrt{0.84},\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos \\phi = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 \\phi} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.5^2} = \\sqrt{0.75}.\n\\]\n\nNow substitute these into the expressions for \\(\\sin(\\theta + \\phi)\\) and \\(\\sin(\\phi - \\theta)\\):\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) = 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75} + \\sqrt{0.84} \\cdot 0.5,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84} - \\sqrt{0.75} \\cdot 0.4.\n\\]\n\nCalculate each:\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) = 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75} + 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84},\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84} - 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75}.\n\\]\n\nNow, multiply these results:\n\\[\n\\sin(\\theta + \\phi) \\cdot \\sin(\\phi - \\theta) = (0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75} + 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84}) \\cdot (0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84} - 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75}).\n\\]\n\nNotice that this is in the form of a difference of squares:\n\\[\n(a + b)(a - b) = a^2 - b^2,\n\\]\nwhere \\(a = 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84}\\) and \\(b = 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75}\\).\n\nCalculate \\(a^2\\) and \\(b^2\\):\n\\[\na^2 = (0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84})^2 = 0.25 \\cdot 0.84 = 0.21,\n\\]\n\\[\nb^2 = (0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75})^2 = 0.16 \\cdot 0.75 = 0.12.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\na^2 - b^2 = 0.21 - 0.12 = 0.09.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the original expression is \\(\\boxed{0.09}\\)."
'To compute \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\), we can use the angle difference identity for cosine. The angle \\(15^\\circ\\) can be expressed as the difference of two common angles: \\(45^\\circ\\) and \\(30^\\circ\\). The cosine of the difference of two angles identity is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a - b) = \\cos a \\cos b + \\sin a \\sin b\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\), we set \\(a = 45^\\circ\\) and \\(b = 30^\\circ\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\cos(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ) = \\cos 45^\\circ \\cos 30^\\circ + \\sin 45^\\circ \\sin 30^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the known trigonometric values for these angles:\n\n- \\(\\cos 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\)\n- \\(\\cos 30^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\)\n- \\(\\sin 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\)\n- \\(\\sin 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\)\n\nSubstituting these values into the expression, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right) + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this expression:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{4} + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nCombining the terms, we have:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe given vector equation is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix} + s \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nFor this equation to have infinitely many solutions in \\(t\\) and \\(s\\), the direction vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\) must be parallel. This means that one vector is a scalar multiple of the other.\n\nLet's check if \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is a scalar multiple of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5}{-15} = \\frac{2}{-6} = -\\frac{1}{3}\n\\]\n\nBoth ratios are equal, confirming that the vectors are indeed parallel.\n\nNext, the initial points \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix}\\) must also be collinear with the direction vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\). This means that the vector from \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix}\\) must be a scalar multiple of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe vector from \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 8 - 4 \\\\ k + 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ k + 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nFor collinearity, we need:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{k + 1}{2}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n4 \\cdot 2 = 5 \\cdot (k + 1)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n8 = 5k + 5\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(k\\), we subtract 5 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n3 = 5k\n\\]\n\nDivide by 5:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{3}{5}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(k\\) that makes the equation have infinitely many solutions is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{5}}\\)."
'To determine the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\), we need to find the least common multiple (LCM) of the periods of the individual cosine terms.\n\n1. The period of \\(\\cos x\\) is \\(2\\pi\\).\n2. The period of \\(\\cos 2x\\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{2} = \\pi\\).\n3. The period of \\(\\cos 3x\\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe period of the sum of these cosine functions is the LCM of their individual periods: \\(2\\pi\\), \\(\\pi\\), and \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nTo find the LCM, we express each period in terms of a common denominator:\n\n- \\(2\\pi = \\frac{6\\pi}{3}\\)\n- \\(\\pi = \\frac{3\\pi}{3}\\)\n- \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\)\n\nThe LCM of \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{3}\\), \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{3}\\), and \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{3} = 2\\pi\\).\n\nTherefore, the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\) is \\(2\\pi\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(2\\pi\\).'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe need to find the two least positive real values of \\( x \\) for which \\(\\sin(x \\text{ degrees}) = \\sin(x \\text{ radians})\\).\n\n1. **Convert Degrees to Radians:**\n   - If \\( x \\) is in degrees, then in radians it is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{180}x\\).\n\n2. **Set Up the Equation:**\n   - We need \\(\\sin(x) = \\sin\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180}x\\right)\\).\n\n3. **Use the Sine Identity:**\n   - The sine function is periodic with period \\(2\\pi\\). Therefore, \\(\\sin(a) = \\sin(b)\\) implies \\(a = b + 2k\\pi\\) or \\(a = \\pi - b + 2k\\pi\\) for some integer \\(k\\).\n\n4. **Consider the First Case:**\n   - \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{180}x + 2k\\pi\\).\n   - Solving for \\(x\\), we get:\n     \\[\n     x - \\frac{\\pi}{180}x = 2k\\pi \\implies x\\left(1 - \\frac{\\pi}{180}\\right) = 2k\\pi \\implies x = \\frac{2k\\pi}{1 - \\frac{\\pi}{180}}.\n     \\]\n   - Simplifying, we have:\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{2k\\pi \\cdot 180}{180 - \\pi}.\n     \\]\n\n5. **Consider the Second Case:**\n   - \\(x = \\pi - \\frac{\\pi}{180}x + 2k\\pi\\).\n   - Solving for \\(x\\), we get:\n     \\[\n     x + \\frac{\\pi}{180}x = \\pi + 2k\\pi \\implies x\\left(1 + \\frac{\\pi}{180}\\right) = \\pi(1 + 2k).\n     \\]\n   - Simplifying, we have:\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{\\pi(1 + 2k)}{1 + \\frac{\\pi}{180}}.\n     \\]\n\n6. **Find the Two Least Positive Values:**\n   - For the first case, set \\(k = 1\\) to get the smallest positive \\(x\\):\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{2\\pi \\cdot 180}{180 - \\pi}.\n     \\]\n   - For the second case, set \\(k = 0\\) to get the smallest positive \\(x\\):\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{\\pi}{1 + \\frac{\\pi}{180}} = \\frac{180\\pi}{180 + \\pi}.\n     \\]\n\n7. **Identify \\(m\\), \\(n\\), \\(p\\), and \\(q\\):**\n   - From the first expression \\(\\frac{2\\pi \\cdot 180}{180 - \\pi}\\), we have \\(m = 360\\) and \\(n = 180\\).\n   - From the second expression \\(\\frac{180\\pi}{180 + \\pi}\\), we have \\(p = 180\\) and \\(q = 180\\).\n\n8. **Calculate the Sum:**\n   - The sum \\(m + n + p + q = 360 + 180 + 180 + 180 = 900\\).\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{900}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given two conditions involving the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n1. \\(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal, which implies:\n   \\[\n   (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0.\n   \\]\n   Expanding this, we get:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0.\n   \\]\n   This simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0.\n   \\]\n   Therefore, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2.\n   \\]\n\n2. \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) are orthogonal, which implies:\n   \\[\n   (\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0.\n   \\]\n   Expanding this, we get:\n   \\[\n   \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0.\n   \\]\n   This simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) = 0.\n   \\]\n   Substituting \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\\) from the first condition, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(-\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2) = 0.\n   \\]\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0.\n   \\]\n   Therefore, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 = 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2.\n   \\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\\). Taking the square root of both sides of the equation \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 = 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{2}\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|.\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio is:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the determinants of two matrices, \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{B}\\):\n- \\(\\det \\mathbf{A} = -7\\)\n- \\(\\det \\mathbf{B} = 3\\)\n\nWe need to find the determinant of the product of these two matrices, \\(\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A})\\).\n\nA key property of determinants is that the determinant of the product of two matrices is equal to the product of their determinants. Mathematically, this is expressed as:\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = \\det \\mathbf{B} \\cdot \\det \\mathbf{A}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given values into this formula, we have:\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = 3 \\cdot (-7)\n\\]\n\nCalculating the product, we get:\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = -21\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the determinant of the product \\(\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-21}\\)."
'To solve the equation \\(\\sin 6x + \\cos 4x = 0\\) for \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\), we start by rewriting the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 6x = -\\cos 4x.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos(90^\\circ - \\theta)\\), we can express \\(\\sin 6x\\) as:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 6x = \\cos(90^\\circ - 6x).\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(90^\\circ - 6x) = -\\cos 4x.\n\\]\n\nThis implies that the angles \\(90^\\circ - 6x\\) and \\(4x\\) are supplementary or differ by \\(180^\\circ\\), leading to two cases:\n\n**Case 1:**\n\n\\[\n90^\\circ - 6x = 180^\\circ - 4x + k \\cdot 360^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n-6x + 4x = 180^\\circ - 90^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ,\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-2x = 90^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ,\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = -45^\\circ - k \\cdot 180^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\), we test values of \\(k\\):\n\n- For \\(k = 0\\), \\(x = -45^\\circ\\) (not in range).\n- For \\(k = -1\\), \\(x = 135^\\circ\\) (valid).\n\n**Case 2:**\n\n\\[\n90^\\circ - 6x = -180^\\circ + 4x + k \\cdot 360^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n-6x - 4x = -180^\\circ - 90^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ,\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-10x = -270^\\circ + k \\cdot 360^\\circ,\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = 27^\\circ - k \\cdot 36^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nTesting values of \\(k\\):\n\n- For \\(k = 0\\), \\(x = 27^\\circ\\) (valid).\n- For \\(k = 1\\), \\(x = -9^\\circ\\) (not in range).\n- For \\(k = -1\\), \\(x = 63^\\circ\\) (valid).\n\nThus, the solutions are:\n\n\\[\nx = 27^\\circ, 63^\\circ, 135^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the angles \\(x\\) that satisfy the equation \\(\\sin 6x + \\cos 4x = 0\\) in the range \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\) are:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{27^\\circ, 63^\\circ, 135^\\circ}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe position of the particle at time \\( t \\) is given by the parametric equations:\n\\[\nx(t) = 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4}, \\quad y(t) = 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4}\n\\]\n\nTo find the speed of the particle, we need to determine the magnitude of the velocity vector, which is the derivative of the position vector with respect to time \\( t \\).\n\nFirst, we find the derivatives of \\( x(t) \\) and \\( y(t) \\) with respect to \\( t \\):\n\n1. The derivative of \\( x(t) = 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{dx}{dt} = \\frac{d}{dt} \\left( 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right) = 3 \\cdot \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}\n   \\]\n\n2. The derivative of \\( y(t) = 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{dy}{dt} = \\frac{d}{dt} \\left( 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right) = -3 \\cdot \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4}\n   \\]\n\nThe velocity vector is \\(\\left( \\frac{dx}{dt}, \\frac{dy}{dt} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}, -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right)\\).\n\nThe speed of the particle is the magnitude of the velocity vector:\n\\[\n\\text{Speed} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2 + \\left( -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression for speed:\n\\[\n\\text{Speed} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4}}\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\left( \\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4} \\right)}\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\text{Speed} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\right)^2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{3}{4}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the speed of the particle is constant and equal to \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\) units of distance per unit of time.\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\)."
"To simplify the expression \\(4 \\sin x \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x)\\), we can use trigonometric identities to transform it into a simpler form. Let's go through the steps:\n\n1. **Product-to-Sum Identity**: We use the identity for the product of sines:\n   \\[\n   \\sin A \\sin B = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(A - B) - \\cos(A + B)].\n   \\]\n\n2. **Apply Identity**: Apply this identity to \\(\\sin (60^\\circ - x)\\) and \\(\\sin (60^\\circ + x)\\):\n   \\[\n   \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos((60^\\circ - x) - (60^\\circ + x)) - \\cos((60^\\circ - x) + (60^\\circ + x))].\n   \\]\n\n3. **Simplify Cosine Arguments**:\n   - \\((60^\\circ - x) - (60^\\circ + x) = -2x\\),\n   - \\((60^\\circ - x) + (60^\\circ + x) = 120^\\circ\\).\n\n   So, the expression becomes:\n   \\[\n   \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(-2x) - \\cos(120^\\circ)].\n   \\]\n\n4. **Evaluate Cosine Terms**:\n   - \\(\\cos(-2x) = \\cos(2x)\\) (since cosine is an even function),\n   - \\(\\cos(120^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\n   Therefore:\n   \\[\n   \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}].\n   \\]\n\n5. **Substitute Back**: Substitute this result back into the original expression:\n   \\[\n   4 \\sin x \\left(\\frac{1}{2} \\left[\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}\\right]\\right) = 2 \\sin x \\left[\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}\\right].\n   \\]\n\n6. **Distribute and Simplify**:\n   - Distribute \\(2 \\sin x\\): \n     \\[\n     2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) + \\sin x.\n     \\]\n   - Use the identity \\(2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) = \\sin(2x)\\).\n\n7. **Final Simplified Form**: The expression simplifies to:\n   \\[\n   \\sin(2x) + \\sin x.\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified form of the original expression is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\sin(2x) + \\sin x}.\n\\]"
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe start with the equation \\( z^6 + z^3 + 1 = 0 \\). To simplify this, we make the substitution \\( w = z^3 \\). This transforms the equation into:\n\n\\[\nw^2 + w + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation, and we can solve it using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 1 \\), and \\( c = 1 \\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 1}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{-3}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the roots are:\n\n\\[\nw_1 = \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2}, \\quad w_2 = \\frac{-1 - i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThese roots are complex numbers with modulus 1, as they lie on the unit circle in the complex plane. We can express these roots in polar form. The modulus of each root is:\n\n\\[\n|w_1| = \\left| \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right| = \\frac{\\sqrt{(-1)^2 + (\\sqrt{3})^2}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{1 + 3}}{2} = 1\n\\]\n\nThe argument of \\( w_1 \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_1) = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{-1}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(-\\sqrt{3})\n\\]\n\nSince the point \\((-1, \\sqrt{3})\\) is in the second quadrant, the argument is:\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_1) = 120^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for \\( w_2 \\):\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_2) = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-\\sqrt{3}}{-1}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(\\sqrt{3})\n\\]\n\nSince the point \\((-1, -\\sqrt{3})\\) is in the third quadrant, the argument is:\n\n\\[\n\\arg(w_2) = 240^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\( z \\) such that \\( z^3 = w_1 \\) or \\( z^3 = w_2 \\). We focus on \\( w_1 \\) because its argument is \\(120^\\circ\\), which is between \\(90^\\circ\\) and \\(180^\\circ\\).\n\nThe argument of \\( z \\) is \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\) of the argument of \\( w_1 \\):\n\n\\[\n\\arg(z) = \\frac{120^\\circ}{3} = 40^\\circ\n\\]\n\nHowever, this is not between \\(90^\\circ\\) and \\(180^\\circ\\). We need to consider the other cube roots of \\( w_1 \\). The cube roots of \\( w_1 \\) are given by:\n\n\\[\nz_k = |w_1|^{1/3} \\left( \\cos\\left(\\frac{120^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3}\\right) + i \\sin\\left(\\frac{120^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3}\\right) \\right)\n\\]\n\nfor \\( k = 0, 1, 2 \\).
