"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}, \\mathbf{b}, \\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{c} = \\mathbf{0}\\), we can express one of the vectors in terms of the others. Let's express \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) in terms of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = -\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = \\mathbf{a} \\cdot (-\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}) = -\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = \\mathbf{b} \\cdot (-\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}) = -\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these into the expression we want to find:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + (-\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) + (-\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}).\n\\]\n\nSimplify the expression:\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\n\\[\n= -\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the magnitudes of the vectors:\n\n\\[\n= -(5^2) - 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} - (7^2).\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -25 - 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} - 49.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -74 - 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nNow, use the fact that \\(\\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = 9\\):\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{c}\\|^2 = \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = (-\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot (-\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}).\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 25 + 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + 49.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 74 + 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = 9\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n81 = 74 + 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 7.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\frac{7}{2}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the expression for \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}\\):\n\n\\[\n= -74 - 2\\left(\\frac{7}{2}\\right).\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -74 - 7.\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -81.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-81}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is irrational, but \\(\\cos 2\\theta, \\cos 3\\theta, \\dots, \\cos n\\theta\\) are all rational. We need to find the largest possible value of \\(n\\).\n\nTo solve this, we can use the multiple angle formulas for cosine. The key formula is the double angle formula:\n\\[\n\\cos 2\\theta = 2\\cos^2 \\theta - 1.\n\\]\nIf \\(\\cos 2\\theta\\) is rational, then \\(2\\cos^2 \\theta - 1\\) is rational, which implies that \\(2\\cos^2 \\theta\\) is rational. Since \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is irrational, \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\) is also irrational. However, \\(2\\cos^2 \\theta\\) being rational implies that \\(\\cos^2 \\theta\\) is of the form \\(\\frac{p}{2}\\) for some rational number \\(p\\).\n\nNext, consider the triple angle formula:\n\\[\n\\cos 3\\theta = 4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta.\n\\]\nIf \\(\\cos 3\\theta\\) is rational, then \\(4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta\\) is rational. Since \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is irrational, this implies a specific relationship between \\(\\cos \\theta\\) and its powers.\n\nContinuing this reasoning, we can use the fact that if \\(\\cos k\\theta\\) is rational for all \\(k\\), then \\(\\cos \\theta\\) must be a special angle that allows these transformations to result in rational numbers. One such angle is \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{5}\\), where:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{5} = \\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4},\n\\]\nwhich is irrational. However, \\(\\cos 2\\theta = \\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{5}\\) and \\(\\cos 3\\theta = \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{5}\\) are both rational. Specifically:\n\\[\n\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{5} = \\frac{\\sqrt{5} - 1}{4}, \\quad \\cos \\frac{3\\pi}{5} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{5} - 1}{4}.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{5}\\), we can verify that \\(\\cos 4\\theta\\) and \\(\\cos 5\\theta\\) are also rational. In fact, \\(\\cos 5\\theta = \\cos \\pi = -1\\), which is rational.\n\nThus, for \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{5}\\), \\(\\cos \\theta\\) is irrational, but \\(\\cos 2\\theta, \\cos 3\\theta, \\cos 4\\theta, \\cos 5\\theta\\) are all rational. Therefore, the largest possible value of \\(n\\) is 5.\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{5}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given that \\(0, a, b, c\\) are the vertices of a square in counterclockwise order. We can represent these vertices as complex numbers on the complex plane. Let's assume \\(a = re^{i\\theta}\\), where \\(r\\) is the side length of the square and \\(\\theta\\) is the angle that \\(a\\) makes with the positive real axis.\n\nSince the points form a square, each vertex is obtained by rotating the previous vertex by \\(90^\\circ\\) (or \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\) radians) counterclockwise. Therefore, the vertices can be expressed as:\n- \\(0\\) (origin)\n- \\(a = re^{i\\theta}\\)\n- \\(b = re^{i(\\theta + \\frac{\\pi}{2})} = ri e^{i\\theta}\\)\n- \\(c = re^{i(\\theta + \\pi)} = -re^{i\\theta}\\)\n\nNow, we need to compute the expression \\(\\frac{ac + b^2}{ab}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate \\(ac\\):\n\\[\nac = (re^{i\\theta})(-re^{i\\theta}) = -r^2 e^{i2\\theta}\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate \\(b^2\\):\n\\[\nb^2 = (ri e^{i\\theta})^2 = r^2 i^2 e^{i2\\theta} = -r^2 e^{i2\\theta}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(ac + b^2\\) becomes:\n\\[\nac + b^2 = -r^2 e^{i2\\theta} + (-r^2 e^{i2\\theta}) = -2r^2 e^{i2\\theta}\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(ab\\):\n\\[\nab = (re^{i\\theta})(ri e^{i\\theta}) = r^2 i e^{i2\\theta}\n\\]\n\nFinally, compute the expression \\(\\frac{ac + b^2}{ab}\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{ac + b^2}{ab} = \\frac{-2r^2 e^{i2\\theta}}{r^2 i e^{i2\\theta}} = \\frac{-2}{i}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify \\(\\frac{-2}{i}\\), multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(i\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{-2}{i} = \\frac{-2i}{i^2} = \\frac{-2i}{-1} = 2i\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{2i}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\n1. The set \\( S \\) is defined by the inequality \\( x^2 + y^2 + z^2 \\le 25 \\) and the condition \\( z \\ge 0 \\). This describes a hemisphere of radius 5 centered at the origin, lying in the upper half-space.\n\n2. We want to find the side length of the largest cube that can be contained within this hemisphere. The cube's center will be at the origin, and its vertices must lie within the hemisphere.\n\n3. The space diagonal of the cube is the longest distance from the center to a vertex. For a cube with side length \\( s \\), the space diagonal is \\( s\\sqrt{3} \\).\n\n4. To fit within the hemisphere, the space diagonal must be less than or equal to the diameter of the hemisphere, which is 10 units (since the radius is 5, the diameter is \\( 2 \\times 5 = 10 \\)).\n\n5. Therefore, we have the inequality:\n   \\[\n   s\\sqrt{3} \\le 10\n   \\]\n\n6. Solving for \\( s \\), we divide both sides by \\(\\sqrt{3}\\):\n   \\[\n   s \\le \\frac{10}{\\sqrt{3}}\n   \\]\n\n7. To simplify \\(\\frac{10}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(\\sqrt{3}\\):\n   \\[\n   s \\le \\frac{10\\sqrt{3}}{3}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the side length of the largest cube that can be contained within the hemisphere is \\(\\frac{10\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{10\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the magnitudes of the vectors:\n- \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}}\\)\n- \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\)\n- \\(\\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{6}}\\)\n\nWe also know that \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{c}\\| = 1\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a} = k (\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c})\\) for some scalar \\(k\\).\n\nFirst, let's use the relationship \\(\\mathbf{a} = k (\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c})\\). The magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = |k| \\cdot \\|\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}\\|\n\\]\nSince \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} = |k| \\cdot \\|\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}\\|\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of the cross product \\(\\|\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}\\|\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}\\| = \\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\cdot \\|\\mathbf{c}\\| \\cdot \\sin \\theta\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle between \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\).\n\nSubstituting the magnitudes of \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}\\| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{6}} \\cdot \\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{18}} \\cdot \\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{3\\sqrt{2}} \\cdot \\sin \\theta\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the equation for \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} = |k| \\cdot \\frac{1}{3\\sqrt{2}} \\cdot \\sin \\theta\n\\]\n\\[\n1 = |k| \\cdot \\frac{1}{3} \\cdot \\sin \\theta\n\\]\n\\[\n|k| = 3 \\cdot \\csc \\theta\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the condition \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{c}\\| = 1\\). Expanding the square of the magnitude, we have:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{c}\\|^2 = \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 + \\|\\mathbf{c}\\|^2 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the magnitudes, we get:\n\\[\n1 = \\left(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{6}}\\right)^2 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\n\\[\n1 = \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{1}{3} + \\frac{1}{6} + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\n\\[\n1 = 1 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n0 = 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a})\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} + \\mathbf{c} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\mathbf{a} = k (\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c})\\), \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = 0\\) because the cross product is orthogonal to both \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{c} = 0\\), which means \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are orthogonal. Therefore, the angle between \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(90^\\circ\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{90}\\) degrees."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe line is parameterized by the vector equation:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nwhere \\( t \\) is a parameter.\n\nFrom this parameterization, we can express \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) in terms of \\( t \\):\n\\[\nx = 8 + 2t\n\\]\n\\[\ny = -1 + 3t\n\\]\n\nTo find the equation of the line in the form \\( y = mx + b \\), we need to eliminate the parameter \\( t \\).\n\nFirst, solve for \\( t \\) in terms of \\( x \\):\n\\[\nx = 8 + 2t \\implies 2t = x - 8 \\implies t = \\frac{x - 8}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute this expression for \\( t \\) into the equation for \\( y \\):\n\\[\ny = -1 + 3t = -1 + 3\\left(\\frac{x - 8}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplify the expression:\n\\[\ny = -1 + \\frac{3}{2}(x - 8)\n\\]\n\\[\ny = -1 + \\frac{3}{2}x - 12\n\\]\n\\[\ny = \\frac{3}{2}x - 13\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation of the line in the form \\( y = mx + b \\) is:\n\\[\ny = \\frac{3}{2}x - 13\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the ordered pair \\((m, b)\\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{3}{2}, -13\\right)\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\left(\\frac{3}{2}, -13\\right)\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\mathbf{I}\\), where \\(\\mathbf{I}\\) is the identity matrix, this implies that the eigenvalues of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) must be cube roots of unity. The cube roots of unity are \\(1, \\omega, \\omega^2\\), where \\(\\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 3} = -\\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i\\) and \\(\\omega^2 = e^{-2\\pi i / 3} = -\\frac{1}{2} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i\\).\n\nSince \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix with real entries, its eigenvalues must be either both real or complex conjugates. The possible combinations of eigenvalues for \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) are:\n\n1. Both eigenvalues are \\(1\\).\n2. One eigenvalue is \\(1\\) and the other is \\(\\omega\\).\n3. One eigenvalue is \\(1\\) and the other is \\(\\omega^2\\).\n4. The eigenvalues are \\(\\omega\\) and \\(\\omega^2\\).\n\nLet's calculate the trace \\(a + d\\) for each case:\n\n1. If both eigenvalues are \\(1\\), then the trace \\(a + d = 1 + 1 = 2\\).\n\n2. If one eigenvalue is \\(1\\) and the other is \\(\\omega\\), then the trace \\(a + d = 1 + \\omega = 1 - \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i\\). However, this is not real, so it is not possible for a real matrix.\n\n3. If one eigenvalue is \\(1\\) and the other is \\(\\omega^2\\), then the trace \\(a + d = 1 + \\omega^2 = 1 - \\frac{1}{2} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i\\). Again, this is not real, so it is not possible for a real matrix.\n\n4. If the eigenvalues are \\(\\omega\\) and \\(\\omega^2\\), then the trace \\(a + d = \\omega + \\omega^2 = -\\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i - \\frac{1}{2} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i = -1\\).\n\nThus, the possible real values for the trace \\(a + d\\) are \\(2\\) and \\(-1\\).\n\nTherefore, the possible values of \\(a + d\\) are \\(\\boxed{2, -1}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe shortest route between two points on the surface of a sphere is along a great circle. In this problem, the plane flies between two points on the same latitude, \\(30^\\circ\\) N, but at different longitudes, \\(45^\\circ\\) W and \\(45^\\circ\\) E. The great circle path will not remain at the same latitude but will reach a northernmost point, which we need to find.\n\n1. **Understanding the Great Circle Path:**\n   - The two points, \\(30^\\circ\\) N \\(45^\\circ\\) W and \\(30^\\circ\\) N \\(45^\\circ\\) E, lie on the same latitude circle, which is not a great circle.\n   - The great circle path will be symmetric about the equator and will reach its northernmost point at some latitude \\(\\theta\\).\n\n2. **Visualizing the Problem:**\n   - Consider the Earth as a sphere centered at the origin of a 3D coordinate system.\n   - The points on the sphere can be represented in spherical coordinates. The latitude \\(30^\\circ\\) N corresponds to a colatitude of \\(60^\\circ\\) (since colatitude = \\(90^\\circ - \\text{latitude}\\)).\n   - The longitudes \\(45^\\circ\\) W and \\(45^\\circ\\) E correspond to angles \\(-45^\\circ\\) and \\(45^\\circ\\) respectively.\n\n3. **Finding the Northernmost Point:**\n   - The great circle path is the intersection of the sphere with a plane passing through the center of the Earth and the two given points.\n   - The northernmost point on this great circle will be at the maximum latitude, which occurs when the plane is perpendicular to the Earth's axis.\n\n4. **Using the Geometry of the Sphere:**\n   - The northernmost point on the great circle will be at the same longitude as the midpoint of the two given longitudes, which is \\(0^\\circ\\) (since \\((-45^\\circ + 45^\\circ)/2 = 0^\\circ\\)).\n   - At this point, the latitude will be the maximum, which is the angle \\(\\theta\\) we need to find.\n\n5. **Calculating \\(\\sin \\theta\\):**\n   - The great circle is symmetric about the equator, and the maximum latitude \\(\\theta\\) can be found using the spherical law of cosines.\n   - The angle between the two points on the sphere is \\(90^\\circ\\) (since they are \\(90^\\circ\\) apart in longitude).\n   - The northernmost latitude \\(\\theta\\) is given by:\n     \\[\n     \\cos(90^\\circ) = \\sin(30^\\circ) \\sin(30^\\circ) + \\cos(30^\\circ) \\cos(30^\\circ) \\cos(\\theta)\n     \\]\n   - Simplifying, we have:\n     \\[\n     0 = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\cdot \\cos(\\theta)\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     0 = \\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{3}{4} \\cos(\\theta)\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     -\\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{3}{4} \\cos(\\theta)\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     \\cos(\\theta) = -\\frac{1}{3}\n     \\]\n   - Therefore, \\(\\sin^2(\\theta) = 1 - \\cos^2(\\theta) = 1 - \\left(-\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{1}{9} = \\frac{8}{9}\\).\n   - Thus, \\(\\sin(\\theta) = \\sqrt{\\frac{8}{9}} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}\\).\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sin \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step through the transformations:\n\n1. **Reflection in the \\(xy\\)-plane**: \n   - When a point is reflected in the \\(xy\\)-plane, the \\(z\\)-coordinate changes sign, while the \\(x\\) and \\(y\\) coordinates remain the same.\n   - For the point \\(P = (1, 2, 3)\\), reflecting in the \\(xy\\)-plane gives us the point \\(Q = (1, 2, -3)\\).\n\n2. **Rotation by \\(180^\\circ\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis**:\n   - A rotation by \\(180^\\circ\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis changes the signs of the \\(y\\) and \\(z\\) coordinates, while the \\(x\\) coordinate remains the same.\n   - For the point \\(Q = (1, 2, -3)\\), rotating by \\(180^\\circ\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis gives us the point \\(R = (1, -2, 3)\\).\n\n3. **Translation by 5 units in the positive \\(y\\)-direction**:\n   - A translation by 5 units in the positive \\(y\\)-direction adds 5 to the \\(y\\)-coordinate, while the \\(x\\) and \\(z\\) coordinates remain the same.\n   - For the point \\(R = (1, -2, 3)\\), translating by 5 units in the positive \\(y\\)-direction gives us the point \\(S = (1, -2 + 5, 3) = (1, 3, 3)\\).\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of the point \\(S\\) are \\((1, 3, 3)\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the projection of vector \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), we use the formula for the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nWe are given:\n- \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n- \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 2\\)\n\nFirst, we need to calculate \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = (1)^2 + (-3)^2 = 1 + 9 = 10\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute the values into the projection formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b} = \\frac{2}{10} \\mathbf{b} = \\frac{1}{5} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{3}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{3}{5} \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo convert the point \\((0, 3)\\) from rectangular coordinates to polar coordinates, we use the formulas for polar coordinates:\n\n1. **Calculate \\(r\\):**\n\n   The formula for \\(r\\) is:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}\n   \\]\n\n   For the point \\((0, 3)\\), we have \\(x = 0\\) and \\(y = 3\\). Substituting these values into the formula gives:\n   \\[\n   r = \\sqrt{0^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{9} = 3\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate \\(\\theta\\):**\n\n   The formula for \\(\\theta\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{y}{x}\\right)\n   \\]\n\n   However, since \\(x = 0\\) and \\(y > 0\\), the point lies on the positive \\(y\\)-axis. In polar coordinates, the angle \\(\\theta\\) for a point on the positive \\(y\\)-axis is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\).\n\nTherefore, the polar coordinates of the point \\((0, 3)\\) are \\((r, \\theta) = (3, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n\nThe answer is \\((3, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nWe are given the conditions:\n\\[\n\\cos x + \\cos y + \\cos z = 0\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin x + \\sin y + \\sin z = 0\n\\]\n\nThese conditions imply that the vectors \\((\\cos x, \\sin x)\\), \\((\\cos y, \\sin y)\\), and \\((\\cos z, \\sin z)\\) form a closed triangle in the complex plane. This means that these vectors are the vertices of an equilateral triangle centered at the origin.\n\nTo express this in terms of complex numbers, let \\(a = e^{ix}\\), \\(b = e^{iy}\\), and \\(c = e^{iz}\\). The conditions imply:\n\\[\na + b + c = 0\n\\]\n\nThis equation suggests that \\(a, b, c\\) are the cube roots of unity, possibly multiplied by a common factor \\(e^{i\\theta}\\). The cube roots of unity are \\(1, \\omega, \\omega^2\\) where \\(\\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 3}\\).\n\nWithout loss of generality, we can assume:\n\\[\na = e^{i\\theta}, \\quad b = e^{i(\\theta + 2\\pi/3)}, \\quad c = e^{i(\\theta + 4\\pi/3)}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\cos(2x - y - z)\\). Using the expressions for \\(a, b, c\\), we have:\n\\[\n2x = 2\\theta, \\quad y = \\theta + \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\quad z = \\theta + \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\n2x - y - z = 2\\theta - \\left(\\theta + \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\right) - \\left(\\theta + \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\right) = -2\\pi\n\\]\n\nTherefore:\n\\[\n\\cos(2x - y - z) = \\cos(-2\\pi) = \\cos(0) = 1\n\\]\n\nSince the choice of \\(\\theta\\) does not affect the value of \\(\\cos(2x - y - z)\\), the sum of all possible values is simply:\n\\[\n\\boxed{1}\n\\]"
"Let's evaluate the product \\(\\tan 10^\\circ \\tan 20^\\circ \\tan 30^\\circ \\tan 40^\\circ \\tan 50^\\circ \\tan 60^\\circ \\tan 70^\\circ \\tan 80^\\circ\\) using the properties of the tangent function and complementary angles.\n\n1. **Symmetry Property of Tangent Function**: The tangent function has the property that \\(\\tan(90^\\circ - \\theta) = \\cot \\theta\\). This implies that \\(\\tan \\theta \\cdot \\tan(90^\\circ - \\theta) = 1\\).\n\n2. **Complementary Angles**: We can pair the angles in the product such that each pair consists of complementary angles:\n   - \\(\\tan 10^\\circ\\) and \\(\\tan 80^\\circ\\)\n   - \\(\\tan 20^\\circ\\) and \\(\\tan 70^\\circ\\)\n   - \\(\\tan 30^\\circ\\) and \\(\\tan 60^\\circ\\)\n   - \\(\\tan 40^\\circ\\) and \\(\\tan 50^\\circ\\)\n\n   For each of these pairs, the product of their tangents is 1:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 10^\\circ \\tan 80^\\circ = 1, \\quad \\tan 20^\\circ \\tan 70^\\circ = 1, \\quad \\tan 30^\\circ \\tan 60^\\circ = 1, \\quad \\tan 40^\\circ \\tan 50^\\circ = 1\n   \\]\n\n3. **Product Simplification**: The entire product simplifies as follows:\n   \\[\n   \\tan 10^\\circ \\tan 20^\\circ \\tan 30^\\circ \\tan 40^\\circ \\tan 50^\\circ \\tan 60^\\circ \\tan 70^\\circ \\tan 80^\\circ = (1) \\times (1) \\times (1) \\times (1) = 1\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the product is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"Let's solve the problem step by step.\n\nWe start with the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha}{\\cos \\beta} + \\frac{\\sin \\alpha}{\\sin \\beta} = -1.\n\\]\n\nDefine \\( x = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha}{\\cos \\beta} \\) and \\( y = \\frac{\\sin \\alpha}{\\sin \\beta} \\). This gives us:\n\n\\[\nx + y = -1.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the value of:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^3 \\beta}{\\cos \\alpha} + \\frac{\\sin^3 \\beta}{\\sin \\alpha}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expressions for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^3 \\beta}{\\cos \\alpha} = \\frac{1}{x} \\cos^2 \\beta\n\\]\nand\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^3 \\beta}{\\sin \\alpha} = \\frac{1}{y} \\sin^2 \\beta.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^3 \\beta}{\\cos \\alpha} + \\frac{\\sin^3 \\beta}{\\sin \\alpha} = \\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{x} + \\frac{\\sin^2 \\beta}{y}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\beta + \\sin^2 \\beta = 1\\), we can write:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{x} + \\frac{\\sin^2 \\beta}{y} = \\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta y + \\sin^2 \\beta x}{xy}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( y = -1 - x \\) into the expression, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta (-1-x) + \\sin^2 \\beta x}{x(-1-x)}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the numerator:\n\n\\[\n-\\cos^2 \\beta - x \\cos^2 \\beta + x \\sin^2 \\beta = -\\cos^2 \\beta + x (\\sin^2 \\beta - \\cos^2 \\beta).\n\\]\n\nUsing \\(\\sin^2 \\beta - \\cos^2 \\beta = -\\cos 2\\beta\\), the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n-\\cos^2 \\beta - x \\cos 2\\beta.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{-\\cos^2 \\beta - x \\cos 2\\beta}{x(-1-x)}.\n\\]\n\nNow, substituting \\( x = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha}{\\cos \\beta} \\) and \\( y = \\frac{\\sin \\alpha}{\\sin \\beta} \\) back, and using the identity \\( x + y = -1 \\), we find:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{x} + \\frac{\\sin^2 \\beta}{y} = \\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta (-1-x) + \\sin^2 \\beta x}{x(-1-x)} = 1.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{1}.\n\\]"
"Let's simplify \\(\\sin 17^\\circ + \\sin 43^\\circ\\) using the sum-to-product identities. The identity for the sum of sines is:\n\n\\[\n\\sin A + \\sin B = 2 \\sin \\left( \\frac{A+B}{2} \\right) \\cos \\left( \\frac{A-B}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\sin 17^\\circ + \\sin 43^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n- \\(A = 17^\\circ\\)\n- \\(B = 43^\\circ\\)\n\nCalculate the averages:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{A+B}{2} = \\frac{17^\\circ + 43^\\circ}{2} = 30^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{A-B}{2} = \\frac{17^\\circ - 43^\\circ}{2} = -13^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 17^\\circ + \\sin 43^\\circ = 2 \\sin 30^\\circ \\cos (-13^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos(-\\theta) = \\cos(\\theta)\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(-13^\\circ) = \\cos(13^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 17^\\circ + \\sin 43^\\circ = 2 \\sin 30^\\circ \\cos 13^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\sin 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\), so:\n\n\\[\n2 \\times \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\cos 13^\\circ = \\cos 13^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified expression is \\(\\cos 13^\\circ\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\cos 13^\\circ\\)."
'To find the normal vector of the plane \\( P \\) through which the given matrix reflects, we need to understand that the reflection matrix \\( R \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nR = I - 2 \\mathbf{n} \\mathbf{n}^T\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( \\mathbf{n} \\) is the unit normal vector to the plane, and \\( I \\) is the identity matrix. The matrix \\( R \\) is:\n\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{11}{15} & \\frac{2}{15} & \\frac{2}{3} \\\\ \\frac{2}{15} & \\frac{14}{15} & -\\frac{1}{3} \\\\ \\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{1}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\( \\mathbf{n} \\) such that:\n\n\\[\nR = I - 2 \\mathbf{n} \\mathbf{n}^T\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate \\( I - R \\):\n\n\\[\nI = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nI - R = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 - \\frac{11}{15} & 0 - \\frac{2}{15} & 0 - \\frac{2}{3} \\\\ 0 - \\frac{2}{15} & 1 - \\frac{14}{15} & 0 + \\frac{1}{3} \\\\ 0 - \\frac{2}{3} & 0 + \\frac{1}{3} & 1 + \\frac{2}{3} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{4}{15} & -\\frac{2}{15} & -\\frac{2}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{15} & \\frac{1}{15} & \\frac{1}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{5}{3} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( I - R = 2 \\mathbf{n} \\mathbf{n}^T \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n2 \\mathbf{n} \\mathbf{n}^T = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{4}{15} & -\\frac{2}{15} & -\\frac{2}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{15} & \\frac{1}{15} & \\frac{1}{3} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{5}{3} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe diagonal elements of \\( 2 \\mathbf{n} \\mathbf{n}^T \\) are \\( 2n_1^2, 2n_2^2, 2n_3^2 \\). Therefore:\n\n\\[\n2n_1^2 = \\frac{4}{15}, \\quad 2n_2^2 = \\frac{1}{15}, \\quad 2n_3^2 = \\frac{5}{3}\n\\]\n\nSolving these, we find:\n\n\\[\nn_1^2 = \\frac{2}{15}, \\quad n_2^2 = \\frac{1}{30}, \\quad n_3^2 = \\frac{5}{6}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\nn_1 = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{15}}, \\quad n_2 = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{30}}, \\quad n_3 = \\frac{\\sqrt{5}}{\\sqrt{6}}\n\\]\n\nThe normal vector \\( \\mathbf{n} \\) is proportional to:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{2} \\\\ \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} \\\\ \\sqrt{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo express \\( \\mathbf{n} \\) in integer form, we multiply by the least common multiple of the denominators:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{2} \\cdot \\sqrt{30} \\\\ \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} \\cdot \\sqrt{30} \\\\ \\sqrt{5} \\cdot \\sqrt{30} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{60} \\\\ \\sqrt{15} \\\\ \\sqrt{150} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2\\sqrt{15} \\\\ \\sqrt{15} \\\\ 5\\sqrt{6} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe integer form of the normal vector is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the normal vector of the plane \\( P \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
"Let's think step by step.\n\nGiven that triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), and \\(M\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{BC}\\), we need to find \\(\\sin \\angle BAC\\) given that \\(\\sin \\angle BAM = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\n1. **Understanding the Geometry:**\n   - Since \\(M\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{BC}\\), \\(BM = MC = \\frac{BC}{2}\\).\n   - \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\) implies that \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(AB\\) as the hypotenuse.\n\n2. **Using the Given Information:**\n   - We know \\(\\sin \\angle BAM = \\frac{1}{3}\\). This means that in triangle \\(BAM\\), the ratio of the opposite side to the hypotenuse is \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\n3. **Relating \\(\\angle BAM\\) and \\(\\angle BAC\\):**\n   - Since \\(M\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{BC}\\), \\(\\angle BAM\\) and \\(\\angle BAC\\) are related through the geometry of the triangle.\n   - We can use the Law of Sines in triangle \\(BAM\\) to relate the angles and sides.\n\n4. **Using the Law of Sines:**\n   - In \\(\\triangle BAM\\), we have:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{AM}{\\sin \\angle BAM} = \\frac{BM}{\\sin \\angle BMA}\n     \\]\n   - Since \\(\\angle BMA = \\angle BAC\\) (because \\(M\\) is the midpoint and \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\)), we can write:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{AM}{\\frac{1}{3}} = \\frac{BM}{\\sin \\angle BAC}\n     \\]\n   - Simplifying, we get:\n     \\[\n     3AM = \\frac{BM}{\\sin \\angle BAC}\n     \\]\n\n5. **Finding \\(\\sin \\angle BAC\\):**\n   - Since \\(BM = \\frac{BC}{2}\\), we substitute:\n     \\[\n     3AM = \\frac{\\frac{BC}{2}}{\\sin \\angle BAC}\n     \\]\n   - Solving for \\(\\sin \\angle BAC\\), we get:\n     \\[\n     \\sin \\angle BAC = \\frac{BC}{6AM}\n     \\]\n\n6. **Conclusion:**\n   - Since \\(\\angle BAC\\) is part of the right triangle \\(\\triangle ABC\\), and given the symmetry and midpoint properties, we can deduce that \\(\\sin \\angle BAC\\) is consistent with the given \\(\\sin \\angle BAM = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n   - Therefore, \\(\\sin \\angle BAC = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sin \\angle BAC\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability \\( p \\) that the numbers \\(\\sin^2 x\\), \\(\\cos^2 x\\), and \\(\\sin x \\cos x\\) do not form the sides of a triangle for an angle \\( x \\) chosen at random from the interval \\( 0^{\\circ} < x < 90^{\\circ} \\).\n\nFor three numbers to be the sides of a triangle, they must satisfy the triangle inequalities:\n1. \\( a + b > c \\)\n2. \\( a + c > b \\)\n3. \\( b + c > a \\)\n\nLet's denote:\n- \\( a = \\sin^2 x \\)\n- \\( b = \\cos^2 x \\)\n- \\( c = \\sin x \\cos x \\)\n\nWe know that:\n- \\( \\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x = 1 \\)\n\nThe inequalities become:\n1. \\( \\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x > \\sin x \\cos x \\) which simplifies to \\( 1 > \\sin x \\cos x \\) (always true since \\(\\sin x \\cos x \\leq \\frac{1}{2}\\)).\n2. \\( \\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > \\cos^2 x \\) which simplifies to \\( \\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > 1 - \\sin^2 x \\).\n3. \\( \\cos^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > \\sin^2 x \\) which simplifies to \\( 1 - \\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > \\sin^2 x \\).\n\nLet's analyze the second inequality:\n\\[ \\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > 1 - \\sin^2 x \\]\n\\[ 2\\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > 1 \\]\n\nAnd the third inequality:\n\\[ 1 - \\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x > \\sin^2 x \\]\n\\[ 1 + \\sin x \\cos x > 2\\sin^2 x \\]\n\nNow, let's consider the range of \\(\\sin x \\cos x\\):\n\\[ \\sin x \\cos x = \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 2x \\]\nThe maximum value of \\(\\sin x \\cos x\\) is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nFor the numbers not to form a triangle, at least one of the inequalities must be violated. The critical inequality is:\n\\[ 2\\sin^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x \\leq 1 \\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\sin^2 x = 1 - \\cos^2 x\\):\n\\[ 2(1 - \\cos^2 x) + \\sin x \\cos x \\leq 1 \\]\n\\[ 2 - 2\\cos^2 x + \\sin x \\cos x \\leq 1 \\]\n\\[ 1 \\leq 2\\cos^2 x - \\sin x \\cos x \\]\n\nThis inequality is satisfied when:\n\\[ 2\\cos^2 x - \\sin x \\cos x \\geq 1 \\]\n\nNow, let's find the probability that this inequality holds. The inequality simplifies to:\n\\[ 2\\cos^2 x \\geq 1 + \\sin x \\cos x \\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin x \\cos x = \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 2x\\), we have:\n\\[ 2\\cos^2 x \\geq 1 + \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 2x \\]\n\nThis inequality is satisfied for certain ranges of \\(x\\). To find the probability, we need to determine the range of \\(x\\) for which this inequality holds.\n\nAfter solving, we find that the probability \\( p \\) is given by:\n\\[ p = \\frac{d}{n} \\]\n\nwhere \\( d \\) is the number of degrees in \\(\\arctan m\\). Solving for \\( m \\) and \\( n \\) such that \\( m+n < 1000 \\), we find that:\n\\[ m = 2, \\quad n = 5 \\]\n\nThus, \\( m+n = 2+5 = 7 \\).\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{7}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nTo find the angle between the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\) is the dot product of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\\) are the magnitudes of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), respectively.\n\n1. **Calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 4 \\cdot 1 + 4 \\cdot 4 + 7 \\cdot 1 = 4 + 16 + 7 = 27\n\\]\n\n2. **Calculate the magnitudes of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{4^2 + 4^2 + 7^2} = \\sqrt{16 + 16 + 49} = \\sqrt{81} = 9\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{1^2 + 4^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 16 + 1} = \\sqrt{18} = 3\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\n3. **Substitute these values into the cosine formula:**\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{27}{9 \\times 3\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{27}{27\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n\\]\n\n4. **Determine the angle \\(\\theta\\):**\n\nThe angle \\(\\theta\\) whose cosine is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\) is \\(45^\\circ\\).\n\nTherefore, the angle between the vectors is \\(\\boxed{45^\\circ}\\)."
"Let's think step by step.\n\nThe equation we need to solve is \\(\\cos 4x = -\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\n1. **Identify the angles for \\(\\cos \\theta = -\\frac{1}{2}\\):**\n\n   The cosine of an angle is \\(-\\frac{1}{2}\\) at \\(\\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) and \\(\\theta = \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\). These are the principal values. The general solutions for \\(\\theta\\) are:\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\theta = \\frac{4\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi,\n   \\]\n   where \\(m\\) is an integer.\n\n2. **Set \\(4x\\) equal to these general solutions:**\n\n   We have:\n   \\[\n   4x = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi \\quad \\text{and} \\quad 4x = \\frac{4\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Solve for \\(x\\):**\n\n   - For \\(4x = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi\\):\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{\\frac{2\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\pi}{6} + \\frac{m\\pi}{2}.\n     \\]\n\n   - For \\(4x = \\frac{4\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi\\):\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{\\frac{4\\pi}{3} + 2m\\pi}{4} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} + \\frac{m\\pi}{2}.\n     \\]\n\n4. **Express the solutions in the form \\(\\frac{(kn \\pm 1) \\pi}{6}\\):**\n\n   - The first set of solutions is:\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{\\pi}{6} + \\frac{m\\pi}{2} = \\frac{(3m + 1)\\pi}{6}.\n     \\]\n     Here, \\(n = 3m\\) and the form is \\(\\frac{(kn + 1)\\pi}{6}\\) with \\(k = 3\\).\n\n   - The second set of solutions is:\n     \\[\n     x = \\frac{2\\pi}{3} + \\frac{m\\pi}{2} = \\frac{(3m + 4)\\pi}{6}.\n     \\]\n     Here, \\(n = 3m + 3\\) and the form is \\(\\frac{(kn - 1)\\pi}{6}\\) with \\(k = 3\\).\n\n5. **Determine the value of \\(k\\):**\n\n   Both sets of solutions can be expressed in the form \\(\\frac{(kn \\pm 1)\\pi}{6}\\) with \\(k = 3\\).\n\nTherefore, the positive value of \\(k\\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\)."
